Introduction

Table of Contents

  • Welcome to Narrative Craft
  • What’s in This Guide
  • How to Use This Guide
  • Integration with Other Guides
  • Example Signals

Welcome to Narrative Craft

The Storyteller Guide teaches you to craft compelling narratives at the table. While the GM Guide focuses on mechanics, procedures, and rulings, this guide focuses on the narrative tools that transform individual scenes into meaningful stories: theme, pacing, character arcs, and campaign structure.

Storytelling in tabletop roleplaying is a collaborative craft. You are not writing a novel or scripting a screenplay. You are creating a framework that allows player choices to matter, weaving their actions into coherent arcs with rising tension, meaningful stakes, and satisfying resolutions. This guide gives you the tools to do that work confidently and flexibly.

Think of the Storyteller Guide as your narrative toolkit. Where the GM Guide answers “How do I run this scene?” and “What does this mechanic do?”, this guide answers “How do I structure this arc?”, “What makes this moment meaningful?”, and “How do I maintain momentum across six sessions?”

You do not need to read this guide cover to cover. Each chapter is designed to stand alone and address specific narrative challenges. Use the reading paths below to find the chapters that match your current needs, then integrate those techniques into your table practice.

What’s in This Guide

This guide is organized into three major sections:

Narrative Foundations (Chapters 1-6): Core storytelling concepts including narrative structure, theme, character arcs, pacing, and campaign frameworks. Start here if you are new to narrative GMing or want to strengthen your foundational techniques.

Advanced Techniques (Chapters 7-12): Specialized tools including mechanics as story devices, improvisation, mysteries, social conflict, and faction dynamics. Use these chapters to deepen your craft and tackle specific campaign challenges.

Genre-Specific Guidance (Chapters 13-15): Tailored advice for horror, cozy, and epic fantasy campaigns. Each genre chapter includes worked examples, pacing considerations, and mechanical adjustments.

Planning & Troubleshooting (Chapters 16-18): Practical tools for arc planning, troubleshooting common problems, and quick reference sheets. Keep these chapters handy during prep and between sessions.

Unlike the GM Guide, which emphasizes mechanical procedures and rulings, the Storyteller Guide emphasizes narrative craft: structure, meaning, and collaborative storytelling. Unlike the Player Guide, which focuses on character creation and player-facing mechanics, this guide focuses on the GM’s unique narrative responsibilities: framing scenes, managing pacing, and weaving player choices into coherent arcs.

How to Use This Guide

Choose a reading path based on your current needs:

New to Narrative GMing: Start with Chapters 2 (Narrative Structure), 3 (Theme, Stakes, and Meaning), and 5 (Pacing and Momentum). These three chapters give you a solid foundation for structuring scenes, sequences, and arcs. Then read Chapter 8 (Session Zero) before your first session.

Experienced GM Seeking Campaign Tools: Start with Chapter 6 (Campaign Frameworks) to choose a campaign structure, then read Chapter 16 (Campaign Arc Planning) for practical planning tools. Add Chapter 4 (Character Arcs) and Chapter 12 (Faction Dynamics) to support player-driven narrative.

Building Mystery or Intrigue Campaigns: Read Chapter 10 (Mysteries and Intrigue), Chapter 11 (Social Conflict and Negotiation), and Chapter 12 (Faction Dynamics) in sequence. These chapters build on each other to create information-driven, social-focused campaigns.

Running Genre-Specific Campaigns: Start with your genre chapter (13 for Horror, 14 for Cozy, 15 for Epic Fantasy), then read Chapter 7 (Mechanics as Story Tools) to customize mechanics for your genre. Add Chapter 6 (Campaign Frameworks) to choose your structural approach.

Troubleshooting Stalled Campaigns: Read Chapter 17 (Storytelling Troubleshooting) for diagnostic questions and intervention techniques. Then revisit Chapters 5 (Pacing) and 4 (Character Arcs) to re-engage player interest.

Integration with Other Guides

The Storyteller Guide integrates with the other core books:

GM Guide: Provides mechanical procedures and scene-level guidance. When the Storyteller Guide discusses narrative structure, consult the GM Guide for the mechanical procedures that support each scene (see GM Guide Chapter 5: Scene Structure and Chapter 6: Mechanics Overview).

Player Guide: Defines character creation, progression, and player-facing mechanics. When designing character arcs (Chapter 4), reference Player Guide Chapter 6 (Progression) for mechanical milestones and Chapter 5 (Character Creation) for flags and bonds.

Mechanics Reference: Comprehensive rules for tags, conditions, clocks, and advanced systems. When using mechanics as story tools (Chapter 7), consult the Mechanics Reference for detailed rules and edge cases.

Use this guide alongside the GM Guide during prep. The GM Guide tells you how to run individual scenes; this guide tells you how to connect those scenes into meaningful arcs. Use the Player Guide during Session Zero to align character creation with campaign themes. Use the Mechanics Reference when you need to customize or extend the core mechanics for narrative purposes.

Example Signals

Throughout this guide, you will encounter these signals:

Worked Examples: Complete scenarios showing techniques in practice. Each example includes setup, execution, and outcomes. Use these as templates for your own table.

GM Usage: Practical bullet points for immediate table use. These are the “how to use this now” takeaways for each technique.

Cross-References: Links to related chapters and other core books. Follow these to deepen your understanding or find related techniques.

Callouts: Brief asides highlighting common pitfalls, advanced techniques, or table-tested tips. Read these for nuance and troubleshooting.

Templates and Checklists: Ready-to-use tools for prep and play. Print these or keep them on screen for quick reference during sessions.

The Storyteller Guide is a living document. As you use these techniques at your table, you will discover what works for your group and develop your own narrative style. Treat this guide as a starting point, not a prescription. The best storytelling emerges from understanding principles and adapting them to your table’s unique needs. — title: “Narrative Structure Fundamentals” slug: storyteller-narrative-structure doc_type: book version: 0.1.0 last_updated: 2025-11-08 keywords: [ttrpg, storyteller, narrative, structure, scenes, arcs] —

Narrative Structure Fundamentals

Table of Contents

  • Narrative Layers
  • Beats Within Scenes
  • Scene Sequences
  • Story Arcs
  • Campaign Structure
  • Compression and Expansion
  • Worked Example: Three-Scene Sequence

Narrative Layers

Narrative structure in tabletop roleplaying works in nested layers, each with its own scope and pacing. Understanding these layers helps you zoom in to individual moments or zoom out to see the larger arc.

Scene: A single continuous unit of play in one location with one set of stakes. Scenes last 15-45 minutes and end when the immediate stakes resolve or the situation changes significantly.

Sequence: A group of 2-5 connected scenes with rising or falling tension. Sequences create narrative momentum by linking scenes thematically or causally. A sequence might span one session or bridge two sessions.

Arc: A complete story with setup, development, climax, and resolution, typically spanning 3-8 sessions. Arcs have central themes, escalating stakes, and clear endpoints. Think of an arc as a season of television or a complete adventure module.

Campaign: Multiple arcs connected by setting, characters, and overarching themes. Campaigns can be episodic (standalone arcs with continuity), serial (tightly connected arcs building to a finale), or hybrid (mix of both).

This nested structure lets you compress or expand based on table interest. If players are deeply engaged in a social conflict, expand the sequence from two scenes to four. If they want to skip travel montage, compress three potential scenes into one narrated transition.

Beats Within Scenes

Each scene is built from beats: discrete moments that change the situation or escalate pressure. A well-structured scene includes these beats:

Frame: Establish where characters are, what they are doing, and what immediate problem or opportunity they face. Framing anchors players in the fiction and sets up the stakes.

Stakes: State clearly what is at risk or what can be gained in this scene. Stakes can be immediate (escape the guards) or connected to larger arc themes (preserve your reputation).

Action: Players declare actions and you call for checks when outcomes are uncertain and stakes are meaningful. Use the core resolution mechanics (see GM Guide Chapter 4) to determine success, partial success, or failure.

Consequence: Narrate outcomes based on check results. Successes advance player goals, partials succeed with cost or complication, failures introduce new problems or escalate pressure.

Transition: Close the scene when stakes resolve or when continuing forward would repeat beats without changing state. Transition to the next scene by stating what changed and what new lever players can pull.

Not every scene needs all five beats explicitly. Social scenes might loop through Stakes-Action-Consequence several times. Exploration scenes might compress Frame and Stakes into a single opening beat. Use these beats as a checklist, not a rigid script.

Scene Sequences

Scenes gain power when connected into sequences with rising or falling tension. A sequence creates narrative momentum by making each scene matter to the next.

Rising Tension: Each scene in the sequence escalates stakes, introduces new complications, or reduces player resources. Use rising tension for investigative sequences (each clue leads to a more dangerous location) or action sequences (chase escalates to rooftop confrontation to hostage standoff).

Falling Tension: Each scene in the sequence offers relief, resolution, or recovery after a climax. Use falling tension for aftermath sequences (dealing with consequences of a heist) or downtime sequences (celebrating victory, then discovering what was left unresolved).

Structure a sequence with clear connective tissue. The end of one scene should create a new lever or question that pulls players into the next scene. If Scene 1 reveals a suspect’s location, Scene 2 is the confrontation at that location. If Scene 2 ends with the suspect escaping through a hidden tunnel, Scene 3 is navigating the tunnels or discovering where they lead.

A strong sequence needs 2-5 scenes. Two scenes create a simple cause-and-effect. Three scenes allow setup, complication, and resolution. Four or five scenes risk losing momentum unless each scene changes state significantly.

Story Arcs

An arc is a complete story with clear beginning, middle, and end. Arcs provide structure for 3-8 sessions and give players a sense of progress and resolution.

Setup (1-2 sessions): Introduce the central problem, establish stakes, and give players their first levers. Setup scenes frame the arc’s theme and let players form a plan or choose a direction.

Development (2-4 sessions): Escalate complications, reveal new information, and test player plans. Development is where most play happens: investigation, negotiation, exploration, and tactical problem-solving. Track progress with clocks and adjust pacing based on table engagement.

Climax (1 session): Bring the arc to a high-stakes resolution point. Climax scenes pull together threads from setup and development, giving players a chance to use what they have learned and built. Climaxes are not always combat; they can be social confrontations, heists, or critical decisions.

Resolution (1 session or part of the climax session): Show the consequences of player choices and close open loops. Resolution provides emotional payoff and transitions to the next arc or campaign phase.

Not every arc needs equal weight in each phase. Mystery arcs might spend more time in development (investigation) than setup. Action arcs might compress setup into a single scene and focus on a multi-scene climax.

Campaign Structure

Campaigns organize multiple arcs into larger patterns. Choose a campaign structure based on your group’s preferences and the kind of stories you want to tell.

Episodic: Each arc stands alone with minimal continuity between arcs. Players might face different challenges each arc, with recurring characters or setting but no overarching plot. Episodic campaigns are flexible and forgiving; missing a session or adding new players is easy.

Serial: Arcs build toward a long-term goal or confrontation. Each arc advances an overarching plot or reveals part of a larger mystery. Serial campaigns create strong narrative momentum but require consistent attendance and careful continuity tracking.

Hybrid: Some arcs are standalone; others connect to an overarching plot. Hybrid campaigns balance flexibility with long-term payoff. Use standalone arcs to explore new themes or locations, and serial arcs to advance the central storyline.

Choose your campaign structure at Session Zero (see Chapter 8) and communicate it clearly to players. If you start episodic and discover players want long-term plot, you can pivot by introducing recurring NPCs or faction clocks that bridge arcs.

Compression and Expansion

Adjust narrative scope dynamically based on table interest and pacing needs. Compression and expansion techniques let you speed through routine moments or dwell on meaningful choices.

Montage: Summarize a series of actions or scenes with a single narration or check. Use montage for travel, downtime projects, or repetitive tasks. Example: “You spend three days gathering supplies and repairing the ship. Give me a Supply check to see if you are fully stocked or need to improvise.” Montages keep momentum without skipping narrative consequences.

Flashback: Introduce a scene from the past to answer a present question or provide context. Flashbacks can be player-initiated (“I want to flashback to when I studied that sigil in the library”) or GM-framed (“You recognize this trap; you have seen it before. Let’s flashback to that moment”). Use flashbacks sparingly to avoid disrupting present tension.

Time Jumps: Skip forward days, weeks, or months between arcs or during resolution. Time jumps let campaigns breathe and reset stakes without playing through every day. Example: “Three months pass. Your wounds heal, the city rebuilds, and rumors spread about your role in stopping the cult. We pick up as you are summoned to the palace.”

When players are deeply engaged, expand. Add extra scenes, zoom in on character moments, or let a single scene breathe with multiple beats. When momentum lags, compress. Cut to the next lever, montage the journey, or transition quickly to the next stakes.

Worked Example: Three-Scene Sequence

Sequence Goal: Investigate a warehouse used by a smuggling ring, rising tension from stealthy reconnaissance to direct confrontation.

Scene 1: Waterfront Surveillance (Rising Tension: Setup)

Frame: The party watches the warehouse from a dockside tavern at dusk. Dock workers finish their shifts; a few cloaked figures slip inside the warehouse after dark.

Stakes: Identify patterns, routines, or vulnerabilities without alerting the smugglers.

Action: Players declare surveillance approaches (watching from the tavern, scouting the perimeter, bribing a dock worker). Call for Awareness or Influence checks.

Consequence: Success reveals guard rotations and a sewer entrance. Partial reveals the entrance but also that the guards are alert and suspicious. Failure means guards notice surveillance and increase patrols.

Transition: Players have enough information to plan entry. They move to Scene 2.

Scene 2: Sewer Infiltration (Rising Tension: Complication)

Frame: The party enters through the sewer grate. The tunnel is narrow, damp, and dark. They hear voices and footsteps from above.

Stakes: Reach the warehouse interior without raising an alarm or getting trapped in the tunnels.

Action: Players navigate the sewer (Mobility checks), listen for guard positions (Awareness), and find the interior access point.

Consequence: Success gets them inside undetected with a positional advantage. Partial gets them inside but a guard hears noise and investigates. Failure triggers an alarm or reveals that the sewer entrance is trapped.

Transition: Players are now inside the warehouse but in a precarious position. They move to Scene 3.

Scene 3: Warehouse Confrontation (Rising Tension: Climax)

Frame: Inside, the warehouse is stacked with crates. A group of smugglers counts coin at a central table. The party has surprise if they succeeded in Scene 2, or the smugglers are alert if Scene 2 was a partial or failure.

Stakes: Capture or interrogate the smuggler leader to learn who is funding the operation, or defeat the smugglers and secure evidence.

Action: Players choose their approach (ambush, negotiation, stealth extraction). Use combat mechanics (GM Guide Chapter 11) or social conflict (Chapter 11 of this guide).

Consequence: Success captures the leader and evidence. Partial achieves one goal but the other escapes or is destroyed. Failure means the smugglers flee, destroy evidence, or reinforcements arrive.

Transition: Scene resolves with players gaining new information or complications. Transition to the next sequence or arc based on what they learned and what new levers emerged.

This three-scene sequence demonstrates rising tension: each scene escalates stakes and narrows player options. The consequence of Scene 1 sets up Scene 2; the consequence of Scene 2 shapes the stakes of Scene 3. If players were deeply engaged, you could expand Scene 2 into two scenes (navigating tunnels, then bypassing a trapped door). If they wanted faster pacing, compress Scenes 1 and 2 into a single reconnaissance montage and start at Scene 3.

Cross-Links

  • Scene procedures and transitions: GM Guide Chapter 5: Scene Structure
  • Storytelling foundations: GM Guide Chapter 14: Storytelling Fundamentals
  • Pacing tools and tension curves: Chapter 5: Pacing and Momentum

    title: “Theme, Stakes, and Meaning” slug: storyteller-theme-stakes-meaning doc_type: book version: 0.1.0 last_updated: 2025-11-08 keywords: [ttrpg, storyteller, theme, stakes, narrative, meaning] —

Theme, Stakes, and Meaning

Table of Contents

  • What Theme Is (and Isn’t) at the Table
  • Identifying Campaign Themes
  • Scene Stakes vs Thematic Stakes
  • Reinforcing Theme Through Mechanics
  • Player Agency and Theme
  • Worked Example: Debt-Themed Arc

What Theme Is (and Isn’t) at the Table

Theme is what a story is about beneath the surface. While plot describes events (the party investigates a murder), theme explores meaning (what does justice cost when the law is corrupt?). Theme gives coherence to disparate scenes and helps players understand why their choices matter beyond immediate success or failure.

In tabletop roleplaying, theme is emergent and collaborative. You do not impose theme by scripting character arcs or forcing moral lessons. Instead, you create situations that invite thematic exploration, then weave player choices into a coherent pattern. Theme arises when players see connections between their actions and larger questions.

Theme is not a message or moral. You are not teaching players a lesson about trust or sacrifice. You are creating space for players to explore trust or sacrifice through meaningful choices. Theme asks questions; it does not provide answers.

Theme is not setting or genre. Fantasy, horror, and cozy campaigns can all explore the same themes (identity, community, sacrifice) through different lenses. Theme is how you frame conflicts and what you emphasize in narration, not what world the campaign inhabits.

Identifying Campaign Themes

Choose 1-3 themes per arc. More than three dilutes focus; fewer than one makes arcs feel generic. Themes can be collaborative (chosen at Session Zero) or GM-led (introduced through early scenes and refined based on player engagement).

Collaborative Theme Selection: During Session Zero, ask players what questions they want their characters to face. Offer examples: “What do you owe to family vs chosen companions?”, “When is revenge justified?”, “What does belonging cost?” Players do not need to answer these questions; they just need to agree the questions are interesting. Use player answers to choose arc themes.

GM-Led Theme Introduction: Choose a theme based on the campaign premise, then introduce it through the first arc. If players engage deeply with that theme, continue it. If they seem more interested in a different aspect of the story, pivot. Example: You plan a campaign about debt and obligation, but players are more interested in the factional politics. Shift the arc theme to loyalty and power.

Strong arc themes are specific enough to guide scene design but flexible enough to allow varied player responses. “Trust” is too broad; “trust when everyone has secrets” is specific. “Sacrifice” is generic; “what you give up to protect home” creates clear stakes.

GM usage:

  • Write your arc theme at the top of your session prep notes
  • Check each scene against the theme: does this scene invite exploration of the theme, or is it just plot?
  • If a scene feels disconnected, add a choice or complication that touches the theme

Scene Stakes vs Thematic Stakes

Stakes operate on two levels: immediate and thematic. Immediate stakes answer “what happens if we fail this check?” Thematic stakes answer “what does this moment mean for the larger arc?”

Immediate Stakes: Concrete, scene-level consequences. “If you fail this check, the guards catch you” or “If you succeed, you learn where the artifact is hidden.” Immediate stakes drive moment-to-moment tension and give players clear objectives.

Thematic Stakes: Abstract, arc-level implications. “Succeeding here means trusting an unreliable ally” or “Failing this check means abandoning your principles for pragmatic survival.” Thematic stakes connect scenes to the arc’s central questions.

Both levels matter. Immediate stakes create urgency and tactical interest. Thematic stakes create meaning and emotional weight. Strong scenes include both.

State immediate stakes clearly before calling for a check. Thematic stakes can be subtler, emerging in narration of consequences or in player reflection. Example: “You need to get past the guards (immediate stakes). Do you bribe them with stolen coin, knowing it ties you to the thieves’ guild (thematic stakes: complicity), or talk your way through and risk exposure (thematic stakes: vulnerability)?”

When immediate stakes are low (a routine skill check), elevate thematic stakes to maintain interest. When thematic stakes are high (a climactic moral choice), reduce immediate complexity so players can focus on the decision’s meaning.

GM usage:

  • Before calling for a check, state immediate stakes out loud
  • After resolving a check, narrate consequences in thematic terms when appropriate
  • If a scene feels flat, add a choice that connects to arc theme

Reinforcing Theme Through Mechanics

Mechanics are not just simulation; they are narrative language. Tags, conditions, clocks, and resources can reinforce theme by representing abstract concepts mechanically.

Tags as Thematic Levers: Use tags to represent thematic elements. In a campaign about debt, introduce tags like Indebted to the Guild, Reputation at Risk, or Bound by Oath. Players can invoke these tags for advantage when acting in accordance with obligations, and you can invoke them to complicate actions when obligations conflict with goals.

Conditions as Metaphor: Conditions represent character state (see GM Guide Chapter 6). Use conditions thematically: in a campaign about corruption, introduce conditions like Compromised, Tainted by Power, or Haunted by Choices. These conditions create mechanical consequences (disadvantage on certain checks, NPC reactions) while reinforcing thematic exploration.

Clocks as Thematic Pressure: Clocks track progress and pressure (see GM Guide Chapter 6). Use clocks to represent thematic tensions. In a campaign about trust, run a clock for Faction Suspicion that advances when players act secretively or betray allies. When the clock fills, the faction turns hostile. This creates mechanical stakes that reinforce thematic questions.

Resources as Story Costs: Resources (coin, supplies, reputation) are not just tactical tools; they represent what characters are willing to spend to achieve goals. In a campaign about sacrifice, track what players give up (wealth, allies, safety) and show narrative consequences. Depleting resources to achieve a goal reinforces the theme mechanically.

Mechanical reinforcement works when it creates meaningful choices, not when it restricts player agency. Tags, conditions, and clocks should give players new levers and complications, not remove options.

GM usage:

  • Choose 2-3 thematic tags or conditions per arc
  • Introduce thematic clocks that reflect arc tensions
  • Let players invoke thematic tags for advantage when acting on theme
  • Advance thematic clocks when player choices touch the theme

Player Agency and Theme

Theme exploration requires player agency. You cannot railroad players into thematic moments; you can only create situations where theme-relevant choices matter.

Avoiding Railroading: Railroading happens when you script outcomes to force a thematic point. Example: You want to explore the cost of victory, so you script a scene where an ally dies regardless of player choices. This removes agency and makes theme feel imposed. Instead, create situations where player choices determine costs. Example: Players can save the ally or secure the objective, but not both. Their choice creates the thematic moment.

Offering Genuine Choices: Thematic exploration works when players face genuine dilemmas where both options have costs and benefits. “Sacrifice safety for knowledge” is a genuine choice if both safety and knowledge matter mechanically and narratively. “Sacrifice safety for knowledge” is not a genuine choice if safety never mattered or if knowledge is the only path forward.

Emergent Theme: Sometimes the most powerful thematic moments emerge from player choices you did not plan. If players repeatedly choose loyalty over pragmatism, that emergent pattern becomes a theme. Recognize these patterns and reinforce them in later scenes by creating situations where loyalty is tested in new ways.

Player-Defined Themes: Some tables prefer player-defined themes emerging from character backgrounds, bonds, and flags (see Player Guide Chapter 5). In this approach, you identify themes by listening to player choices and backstory, then design scenes that engage those themes. This requires flexibility and improvisation but creates deeply personal arcs.

GM usage:

  • Design situations, not outcomes; let player choices determine results
  • Offer choices where both options matter and both have costs
  • Notice emergent themes from player behavior and reinforce them
  • Check with players between sessions if thematic exploration feels forced or flat

Worked Example: Debt-Themed Arc

Arc Theme: “What you owe to those who saved you.”

Setup: The party owes a powerful merchant guild for past assistance (resources, information, or rescue). The guild now calls in the debt, asking the party to retrieve a stolen ledger from a rival faction. The ledger contains evidence of the guild’s smuggling operations; if it reaches the city guard, the guild collapses.

Arc Progression

Scene 1: The Debt Called

Immediate Stakes: Accept the guild’s mission or refuse and face consequences (loss of guild support, enemy faction status).

Thematic Stakes: Does loyalty to those who helped you outweigh moral concerns about their crimes?

Player Choice: The party debates accepting the mission. One player invokes their Indebted to the Guild tag for advantage on an Influence check to negotiate better terms (safe passage for informants if they succeed). Partial success: the guild agrees but adds a time limit, starting a Rival Faction Moves clock.

Scene 2: Infiltrating the Rival

Immediate Stakes: Retrieve the ledger from the rival faction’s archives without triggering alarms.

Thematic Stakes: The rival faction is morally cleaner than the guild; stealing from them makes the party complicit in the guild’s corruption.

Player Choice: One player wants to warn the rival faction about the guild’s crimes. Another insists on completing the mission to honor the debt. They compromise: retrieve the ledger, but leave evidence that implicates the guild’s leadership while protecting lower-ranking members.

Consequence: Success retrieves the ledger. The Rival Faction Moves clock advances because the party’s compromise delays their escape.

Scene 3: The Ledger’s Contents

Immediate Stakes: Deliver the ledger to the guild or leverage it for your own purposes.

Thematic Stakes: The ledger reveals the guild funded your original rescue to create a debt and ensure loyalty. Does this manipulation change what you owe?

Player Choice: The party confronts the guild leader, using the ledger as leverage to renegotiate terms. They demand the guild cease smuggling operations or they release the ledger to the guard.

Consequence: The guild leader agrees but marks the party with a No Longer Trusted condition. Future interactions with the guild have disadvantage, and the guild becomes a long-term complication.

Resolution: The arc resolves with the debt cleared but relationships changed. The party explored the arc theme through three scenes, each offering genuine choices with immediate and thematic stakes. The theme was reinforced mechanically (Indebted to the Guild tag, Rival Faction Moves clock, No Longer Trusted condition) and players determined outcomes through their choices, not scripted events.

  • Storytelling foundations: GM Guide Chapter 14: Storytelling Fundamentals

  • Mechanics as story tools: Chapter 7: Mechanics as Story Tools

Character Arcs and Player Agency

Table of Contents

  • Character Arc Types
  • Flags, Bonds, and Hooks
  • Authored Moments vs Emergent Narrative
  • Arc Milestones and Progression Triggers
  • Ensemble Balancing
  • Worked Example: Growth Arc Across Six Sessions

Character Arc Types

Character arcs describe how characters change (or resist change) over the course of a story. Understanding arc types helps you create situations that support character development without scripting player choices.

Growth Arc: The character overcomes a flaw, learns a lesson, or develops a new capability. Growth arcs move from weakness to strength, ignorance to understanding, or isolation to connection. Example: A cynical mercenary learns to trust their companions.

Fall Arc: The character succumbs to temptation, loses something vital, or becomes the thing they opposed. Fall arcs create tragedy and explore what happens when characters compromise core values. Example: A noble knight becomes ruthless in pursuit of justice.

Flat Arc: The character does not change internally; instead, they change the world around them by maintaining their principles. Flat arcs are heroic and aspirational; the character is already who they need to be. Example: A loyal guardian protects their charge despite overwhelming pressure to abandon them.

Ensemble Arc: Multiple characters share an arc, changing together through shared experiences. Ensemble arcs emphasize group dynamics and collective growth. Example: A fractured party learns to work as a cohesive unit.

Not every character needs a defined arc, and arcs can shift mid-campaign based on player choices. Some players prefer reactive characters who respond to events without deep internal change. Other players want transformative arcs with clear emotional beats. Check with players at Session Zero to gauge their interest in character arcs.

Flags, Bonds, and Hooks

Flags, bonds, and hooks are player-authored tools that invite you to engage character backstory and goals. They give you permission to create scenes that test, complicate, or advance character concerns.

Flags: Explicit statements of what a player wants to explore. Flags can be thematic (“I want scenes where my loyalty to the crown is tested”), mechanical (“I want to use my disguise abilities in social infiltration”), or emotional (“I want moments where my character confronts their fear of failure”). Collect flags at Session Zero and revisit them every few sessions.

Bonds: Relationships or obligations that tie characters to NPCs, factions, or places. Bonds create recurring story hooks and give you levers for complicating choices. Example: A bond to a mentor NPC lets you create scenes where the mentor asks for help or their safety is threatened. Bonds are mechanical in some systems (see Player Guide Chapter 5) and purely narrative in others.

Hooks: Unresolved backstory elements or character mysteries that players signal they want addressed. Hooks are questions without answers: Who killed my family? Why was I exiled? What is the artifact I carry? Hooks give you permission to introduce revelations, complications, or choices related to those questions.

Use flags, bonds, and hooks as session prep tools. Choose one or two per session to engage directly, and sprinkle references to others in narration or NPC dialogue. This ensures every player feels seen without overwhelming any single session with character focus.

GM usage:

  • At Session Zero, collect 2-3 flags or hooks per player
  • Track bonds in your campaign notes; update when bonds form or break
  • Choose one flag or hook to spotlight per session
  • Revisit flags every 3-4 sessions to see if player interests have shifted

Authored Moments vs Emergent Narrative

Balancing authored moments (scenes you design to engage character arcs) with emergent narrative (arcs that arise from player choices) is central to supporting character development without railroading.

Authored Moments: Pre-designed scenes or encounters that engage a character’s flag, bond, or hook. Example: You know a player wants their character to confront their estranged sibling, so you introduce the sibling as an NPC in a planned scene. Authored moments give you control over pacing and setup, ensuring key beats happen.

Emergent Narrative: Arcs that develop organically from player choices and table dynamics. Example: A player repeatedly chooses to protect innocents even when tactically disadvantageous, so you build that pattern into a narrative arc by introducing NPCs who recognize and test that heroism. Emergent narrative feels organic and player-driven but requires attentiveness and improvisation.

Both approaches are valid and complementary. Use authored moments for major character beats (confronting a nemesis, choosing between two loyalties) and emergent narrative for developing patterns and unexpected turns.

The key is consent and flexibility. Authored moments work when you check with players beforehand (“I am thinking of introducing your sibling as an NPC next session. Is that interesting to you?”). Emergent narrative works when you follow player cues without forcing outcomes (“I notice you keep protecting innocents; I will create situations that test that value”).

Avoid scripting outcomes. Authored moments should present situations, not dictate results. If you introduce a sibling NPC, do not script reconciliation or conflict; let the player’s choices determine the outcome.

GM usage:

  • Ask players at Session Zero if they want authored character moments or prefer emergent arcs
  • For authored moments, check with players before introducing major backstory NPCs or revelations
  • For emergent narrative, take notes on repeated player choices and build on them
  • Never script outcomes; design situations and let players choose

Arc Milestones and Progression Triggers

Character arcs often align with mechanical progression (see Player Guide Chapter 6), but they can also operate independently as narrative milestones.

Narrative Milestones: Key moments that mark character development. Examples: “Confront your fear,” “Reconcile with an estranged ally,” “Choose between two loyalties,” “Achieve a long-term goal.” Narrative milestones do not grant mechanical benefits; they provide emotional payoff and story closure.

Mechanical Progression Triggers: Moments when character growth translates into new abilities, tags, or mechanical advancement. Example: After a character overcomes their fear in a climactic scene, they gain a new tag (Fearless) or remove a limiting condition (Haunted by Doubt).

Align narrative and mechanical milestones when possible. A character who completes a growth arc by learning to trust might gain a new bond or lose a negative tag at the same moment. This reinforces the connection between story and mechanics.

Not all milestones need mechanical rewards, and not all mechanical progression needs narrative justification. Some players care deeply about aligning story and mechanics; others prefer to keep them separate. Check player preferences and adjust accordingly.

GM usage:

  • Identify 2-3 narrative milestones per character arc
  • Offer mechanical progression triggers when milestones align with arc beats
  • Let players define their own milestones if they prefer emergent arcs
  • Celebrate milestones with in-fiction recognition (NPC reactions, world changes)

Ensemble Balancing

Balancing spotlight across multiple characters is essential for ensemble play. Every player should feel their character matters, even when a specific session focuses on another character’s arc.

Spotlight Rotation: Plan sessions to rotate spotlight across characters. Session 1 focuses on Character A’s flag; Session 2 engages Character B’s bond; Session 3 is ensemble-focused with no single character spotlight. This ensures fairness without making any session feel fragmented.

Shared Arcs: Create arcs where multiple characters have stakes or connections. Example: The party investigates a conspiracy; each character has a personal reason to care (one seeks a missing sibling, another wants to clear their name, a third owes a debt to a victim). Shared arcs let you engage multiple characters simultaneously.

Supporting Roles: When a session spotlights one character, give other characters supporting roles that matter. Example: Session 3 focuses on Character A confronting their mentor. Character B provides tactical support, Character C negotiates with a rival faction to create an opening, and Character D gathers intelligence. Everyone contributes even when the emotional focus is on Character A.

Ensemble Beats: Include ensemble beats in every session: moments where the group dynamic matters more than individual characters. Examples: Shared victories, group decisions, moments of collective tension or relief. Ensemble beats reinforce group cohesion and give quieter players chances to contribute.

Track spotlight over time. If a character has not been spotlighted in 3-4 sessions, prioritize their flag or bond in the next session. If a player seems disengaged, check in privately to see if they feel overlooked or if their interests have shifted.

GM usage:

  • Track spotlight in session notes; aim for every character to be spotlighted every 3-4 sessions
  • Design shared arcs where multiple characters have personal stakes
  • Give supporting roles to non-spotlighted characters
  • Include at least one ensemble beat per session

Worked Example: Growth Arc Across Six Sessions

Character: Kael, a former soldier haunted by a battlefield failure that cost lives. Kael’s flaw: avoids leadership roles and hesitates to make hard tactical calls.

Arc Goal: Kael learns to trust their judgment and accepts leadership responsibility despite past failure.

Session 1: Introduction (Setup)

The party faces a tactical challenge (ambush or complex combat). Another character naturally takes the lead, but their plan is flawed. Kael notices the flaw but stays silent, deferring to the other character. The plan partially succeeds but with avoidable costs. After the scene, an NPC or another player asks why Kael did not speak up. This establishes Kael’s flaw narratively and mechanically (Kael has a Haunted by Failure condition that imposes disadvantage on Leadership checks).

Session 2: First Test (Development)

The party is hired to escort a caravan through dangerous terrain. The employer assumes Kael, as the experienced soldier, will lead the escort. Kael is uncomfortable but agrees. During the journey, bandits attack. Kael must make a tactical call under pressure: fight or retreat to defensible ground. Player chooses to retreat (a sound call). The choice succeeds and the caravan is saved, but Kael second-guesses the decision afterward, wondering if fighting would have been bolder.

Session 3: Complication (Development)

An NPC from Kael’s military past appears, someone who survived the battlefield failure. The NPC is bitter and blames Kael for the deaths. This scene tests Kael emotionally: does Kael accept the blame, deflect it, or try to reconcile? Player chooses to accept responsibility and apologize, but the NPC does not forgive. This reinforces Kael’s internal conflict and deepens the arc.

Session 4: Support (Development)

The party faces a non-combat challenge requiring tactical planning (heist, negotiation, or exploration). Another character explicitly asks Kael to lead the planning. Kael makes a plan, and the party executes it. The plan succeeds (full or partial), and party members vocally appreciate Kael’s leadership. This session provides positive reinforcement and begins shifting Kael’s self-perception.

Session 5: Climactic Test (Climax)

The party faces a major conflict where Kael must make a high-stakes leadership call: sacrifice a tactical advantage to save innocents, or prioritize mission success. The choice echoes Kael’s original failure. Player chooses to save the innocents, accepting the tactical cost. The mission succeeds but barely, and lives are saved. After the scene, the NPC from Session 3 reappears, witnessing Kael’s choice. The NPC acknowledges Kael has changed and offers forgiveness.

Session 6: Resolution (Resolution)

Kael’s Haunted by Failure condition is removed and replaced with a new tag: Tested Leader. The party celebrates their victory, and an NPC or faction offers Kael a formal leadership role (command of a militia, leadership of the party, or a position of responsibility). Player chooses whether to accept. The arc concludes with Kael having proven their growth, and the player decides how that growth manifests going forward.

Arc Structure: Each session built on the previous one, testing Kael’s flaw in different contexts (tactical, emotional, ethical). The arc allowed player choice at every stage (Kael could have rejected leadership, deflected blame, or chosen differently in Session 5). Authored moments (NPC from the past, climactic choice) were balanced with emergent development (party members’ organic support). The arc aligned narrative growth with mechanical progression (Haunted by Failure removed, Tested Leader gained).

Cross-Links

  • Progression mechanics: Player Guide Chapter 6: Progression
  • Theme and stakes: Chapter 3: Theme, Stakes, and Meaning

Pacing and Momentum

Table of Contents

  • Pacing Fundamentals
  • Scene-Level Pacing
  • Session-Level Pacing
  • Arc-Level Pacing
  • Mechanical Pacing Tools
  • Troubleshooting Pacing Problems
  • Worked Example: Session with Three-Act Structure

Pacing Fundamentals

Pacing is the rhythm of tension and release. Good pacing alternates pressure with relief, giving players space to breathe between hard choices while maintaining forward momentum. Pacing operates at multiple scales: scene, session, and arc.

Tension Curves: Visualize tension as a curve that rises and falls over time. Scenes with high tension (combat, critical negotiations, timed challenges) create peaks. Scenes with low tension (planning, travel, downtime) create valleys. A well-paced session or arc includes both peaks and valleys, preventing fatigue and monotony.

Energy Management: Players have finite attention and decision-making energy. Long sessions with constant high-stakes tension exhaust players; long sessions with no tension bore them. Manage energy by varying scene intensity and providing pressure-relief cycles.

Momentum: Forward narrative drive that makes players care about what happens next. Momentum comes from unanswered questions, escalating stakes, and meaningful consequences. Loss of momentum happens when scenes feel repetitive, stakes are unclear, or player choices do not visibly change the situation.

Pacing is not about speed. A slow, atmospheric horror session can have excellent pacing if tension builds steadily. A fast-paced action session can have poor pacing if tension never varies. Pacing is about rhythm and variety.

Scene-Level Pacing

Scenes have their own internal pacing, shaped by beats, spotlight rotation, and cuts.

Beats: Each scene is composed of beats (see Chapter 2: Narrative Structure). Vary beat length based on table engagement. If players are deeply engaged in a negotiation, let the scene breathe with multiple dialogue beats. If players are disengaged or the outcome is clear, compress the scene into fewer beats.

Spotlight Rotation: In ensemble scenes (combat, group planning, social events), rotate spotlight among players to maintain engagement. Avoid long stretches where one player dominates while others wait. Use initiative order in combat, or rotate clockwise/counterclockwise in social scenes.

Cuts: End scenes when stakes resolve or when continuing would repeat beats without changing state. Cuts maintain momentum by moving to the next lever rather than dwelling on resolved situations. Example: The negotiation succeeds and the NPC agrees to provide information. Cut to the next scene where players use that information, rather than playing through the entire conversation’s conclusion.

Zooming In and Out: Adjust scene granularity based on importance and engagement. Zoom in (detail every action, roll every check) for high-stakes moments like climactic combat or critical negotiations. Zoom out (summarize actions, skip routine checks) for low-stakes moments like shopping or travel.

GM usage:

  • Check table engagement every 15-20 minutes; cut or extend scenes accordingly
  • Rotate spotlight every 2-3 minutes in ensemble scenes
  • Cut scenes early if stakes are resolved or if players are ready to move on
  • Zoom in for climaxes, zoom out for routine moments

Session-Level Pacing

Sessions benefit from act structure: beginning, middle, and end with distinct purposes and energy levels.

Act One: Setup and Hook (30-45 minutes)

Begin with a recap of the previous session and immediate hooks. Act One establishes stakes, sets the session’s tone, and gives players their first meaningful choice. This act should answer “What are we doing?” and “Why does it matter?”

Example: The session begins with the party receiving a contract to investigate disappearances in a coastal village. Act One includes travel to the village, initial observations, and a first clue (a witness who saw strange lights near the docks). Players choose whether to investigate the docks immediately or gather more information in the village.

Act Two: Development and Complication (60-90 minutes)

The bulk of the session. Act Two includes multiple scenes with escalating stakes and complications. This is where most checks happen, resources are spent, and clocks advance. Vary scene intensity to prevent fatigue: alternate high-tension scenes (combat, chases, critical negotiations) with medium-tension scenes (investigation, planning, social interactions).

Example: The party investigates the docks (medium tension), discovers smugglers unloading strange cargo (complication), and chooses to ambush the smugglers (high tension combat). After combat, they interrogate a captured smuggler (medium tension) and learn the cargo is headed to a warehouse (new lever).

Act Three: Climax and Resolution (30-45 minutes)

The session’s high point followed by a resolution beat. Act Three delivers on the session’s stakes and provides a sense of closure, even if larger arc questions remain unanswered. End sessions on a clear note: a victory, a defeat, a revelation, or a new choice.

Example: The party infiltrates the warehouse and confronts the smuggler leader (climax). They succeed, capturing the leader and securing the cargo. Resolution: They discover the cargo is cursed artifacts tied to the larger arc’s villain. They return to the village, deliver the captured smuggler to local authorities, and receive payment. The session ends with the party deciding what to do with the cursed artifacts.

Pressure and Relief Cycles: Within each act, alternate pressure (timed challenges, resource costs, difficult checks) with relief (successes, safe spaces, moments to regroup). Act Two should have at least two pressure-relief cycles to prevent exhaustion.

GM usage:

  • Plan sessions with three acts; adjust act length based on table energy
  • Start sessions with a clear hook and immediate stakes
  • Alternate high-tension and medium-tension scenes in Act Two
  • End sessions on resolution beats; avoid cliffhangers unless players prefer them
  • Include at least two pressure-relief cycles per session

Arc-Level Pacing

Arcs span multiple sessions and require longer pacing rhythms. Arc pacing tracks rising action, climax placement, and resolution beats across 3-8 sessions.

Rising Action: Each session in the arc should escalate stakes or introduce complications. Rising action creates momentum by making each session matter to the next. Track rising action with arc clocks: progress clocks (player goals) and pressure clocks (threats or countdowns).

Example: Session 1 introduces the smuggling conspiracy. Session 2 reveals the conspiracy is backed by a noble house. Session 3 shows the noble house is using cursed artifacts to gain power. Each session raises the stakes and adds complexity.

Climax Placement: Place the arc climax in the final or penultimate session. Climaxes need buildup; rushing to climax too early makes later sessions feel anticlimactic. Delaying climax too long risks losing momentum. Aim for climax in Session 5 or 6 of a 6-session arc.

Resolution Beats: After the climax, provide resolution: show consequences of player choices, resolve lingering questions, and transition to the next arc or campaign phase. Resolution can be a full session (if the arc was long or complex) or part of the climax session (if the arc was short or the climax resolved most threads).

Pacing Adjustments: Not all arcs need uniform pacing. Mystery arcs might have slow buildup (Sessions 1-4) and rapid climax (Sessions 5-6). Action arcs might have quick setup (Session 1) and extended climax (Sessions 4-6). Adjust based on genre and table preferences.

GM usage:

  • Track rising action with arc clocks; advance clocks every 1-2 sessions
  • Place climax in the final or penultimate session of the arc
  • Provide resolution beats after climax; do not rush immediately into the next arc
  • Adjust pacing based on arc type and table engagement

Mechanical Pacing Tools

Mechanics can shape pacing by creating tension, tracking progress, and introducing time pressure.

Clocks: Clocks (see GM Guide Chapter 6) track progress (player goals) and pressure (threats, countdowns). Advance pressure clocks during downtime or when players delay decisions, creating urgency. Advance progress clocks when players achieve milestones, providing momentum and satisfaction.

Example: Run a Villain’s Ritual pressure clock (6 segments). Advance it once per session or when players pursue other goals. When the clock fills, the villain completes the ritual and becomes a greater threat. This creates urgency without scripting outcomes.

Timers: Timers are explicit in-fiction countdowns. “You have three days before the ritual completes” or “The guards return in ten minutes.” Timers create immediate pressure and force decisive action. Use timers sparingly; too many timers creates constant stress with no relief.

Conditions: Conditions (see GM Guide Chapter 6) represent character state and create resource pressure. Accumulating conditions worsens outcomes and forces players to rest or spend resources to recover. Conditions naturally pace sessions by encouraging rest after intense scenes.

Montage: Montage compresses time and skips routine actions (see Chapter 2). Use montage to pace through low-interest moments (“You spend two days traveling and restocking; give me a Supply check to see if you are fully prepared”) and focus play on meaningful choices.

GM usage:

  • Use clocks to track long-term pressure and progress
  • Add timers for high-stakes scenes or arc climaxes
  • Let conditions accumulate to encourage rest and relief
  • Use montage to skip routine moments and maintain momentum

Troubleshooting Pacing Problems

Common pacing problems and interventions:

Stalled Momentum: Players feel stuck, unsure what to do next, or trapped in analysis paralysis.

Diagnosis: No clear levers, stakes are unclear, or choices feel meaningless.

Intervention: Introduce a new lever (NPC arrives with urgent news, pressure clock advances visibly, new complication demands response). Restate stakes clearly. Offer a choice with visible consequences to restore agency.

Runaway Planning: Players spend excessive time planning without acting.

Diagnosis: Players fear failure or feel the next scene is too dangerous without perfect preparation.

Intervention: Set a soft timer (“While you plan, guards are approaching; you have a few minutes to decide”). Offer a montage option (“You finalize the plan over the next hour; describe the key elements and roll [relevant skill] to see how prepared you are”). Validate the plan and move to action.

Uneven Tension: Sessions feel exhausting (constant tension) or boring (no tension).

Diagnosis: Scenes do not vary in intensity, or pressure-relief cycles are missing.

Intervention: After high-tension scenes, include relief scenes (safe rest, NPC downtime, celebration). After low-tension stretches, introduce pressure (timer, threat, hard choice). Track tension visually (notes: “high, low, medium”) to ensure variety.

Anticlimactic Resolutions: Arc or session climaxes feel flat or rushed.

Diagnosis: Climax lacked buildup, stakes were unclear, or player choices did not matter.

Intervention: Build toward climaxes with rising action across multiple sessions. State stakes clearly before climax scenes. Ensure player choices determine outcomes, not scripted events.

GM usage:

  • Diagnose pacing problems by checking engagement and table energy
  • Intervene early when momentum stalls; introduce new levers or restate stakes
  • Balance tension with relief; track scene intensity in session notes
  • Build toward climaxes with rising action and clear stakes

Worked Example: Session with Three-Act Structure

Session Goal: Investigate a haunted manor and discover the ghost’s unfinished business.

Act One: Setup and Hook (30 minutes)

Recap: Last session, the party learned of a manor haunted by a restless spirit. Locals refuse to approach it, but a merchant hires the party to retrieve a family heirloom from inside.

Hook: The party arrives at the manor at dusk. The building is decrepit, windows shattered, vines overgrown. As they approach, they hear a faint wailing from inside.

Stakes: Retrieve the heirloom and, if possible, help the spirit find peace. Immediate danger: the manor is structurally unstable and the spirit may be hostile.

First Choice: Players choose an entry point (front door, side window, cellar entrance). Each option has different risks (front door is obvious but direct; cellar is hidden but trapped; window requires climbing).

Outcome: Players choose the cellar. They succeed on a Mobility check to navigate the unstable stairs. Inside, they find signs of old tragedy: burned furniture, scorched walls, a child’s toy. Tension builds. Cut to Act Two.

Act Two: Development and Complication (75 minutes)

Scene 1 (Medium Tension): Exploring the manor. Players investigate rooms, finding clues about the family who lived here: a husband, wife, and young daughter. The husband was an alchemist. Clues suggest an experiment went wrong, causing a fire. Players roll Awareness to notice arcane symbols on the walls and residual magic. Partial success: they find the symbols but trigger a magical trap, creating eerie lights and sounds (atmosphere, minor pressure).

Scene 2 (High Tension): The spirit manifests, a spectral figure of a young girl. She does not attack but wails and vanishes. The temperature drops. Players must choose: pursue the spirit immediately or continue investigating. They choose to investigate further.

Scene 3 (Medium Tension): The party finds the husband’s study. Inside, a journal describes his attempt to save his daughter from illness using forbidden alchemy. The ritual failed, killing the daughter and starting the fire. The heirloom (a locket) is on the desk. Players retrieve it. As they do, the spirit appears again, this time clearly agitated.

Relief Beat: The party retreats to a stable room to regroup, discuss findings, and plan their next move. This is a pressure-relief cycle: low tension, no immediate threat, time to strategize.

Scene 4 (High Tension): The party attempts to communicate with the spirit using the journal and locket. They offer to bury the locket with her remains (if they can find them) or complete a ritual to lay her to rest. Players roll Influence or relevant skills. Partial success: the spirit calms but reveals her remains are in the cellar, where the fire started. The cellar is unstable and dangerous.

Complication: As they descend to the cellar, part of the floor collapses. Players must navigate hazards (Mobility or teamwork checks). Success gets them to the remains without injury. Failure or partial results in conditions (Injured, Exhausted).

Cut to Act Three.

Act Three: Climax and Resolution (30 minutes)

Climax: The party finds the daughter’s remains in the cellar, badly burned. They use the locket and perform a simple ritual (either based on the journal or improvised). Call for a final check (Lore, Influence, or relevant skill) to determine success.

Outcome: Success means the spirit appears, thanks the party, and peacefully dissipates. The haunting ends. Partial success means the spirit dissipates but the manor remains unsettled (future complications). Failure means the spirit becomes hostile (brief combat or frantic escape).

Resolution: The party exits the manor, heirloom in hand. They return to the merchant, deliver the locket, and receive payment. The merchant asks what they learned about the haunting. Players can share the truth (earning the merchant’s gratitude and potential future work) or conceal details (keeping secrets but losing trust).

Final Beat: The session ends with the party resting at an inn. One player reflects on the tragedy, creating a character moment. The GM hints at the next session’s hook (a letter arrives requesting the party’s help with another problem).

Pacing Breakdown:

  • Act One established stakes and tone, providing a clear hook and first choice.
  • Act Two alternated tension: medium (investigation), high (spirit encounter), medium (study discovery), relief (planning), high (cellar hazards). Two pressure-relief cycles prevented fatigue.
  • Act Three delivered a satisfying climax with player choice determining outcomes, followed by resolution and transition to the next session.
  • Total session time: 135 minutes (2.25 hours), with clear act breaks for table energy management.

Campaign Frameworks

Table of Contents

  • Campaign Framework Types
  • Episodic Campaigns
  • Linear Campaigns
  • Sandbox Campaigns
  • Hexcrawl Campaigns
  • Fronts-Based Campaigns
  • Faction-Driven Campaigns
  • Blending Frameworks
  • Choosing and Transitioning Frameworks

Campaign Framework Types

Campaign frameworks are structural approaches that define how narrative, player choice, and GM prep interact. Each framework emphasizes different play patterns and requires different preparation styles. Choosing the right framework (or blending multiple frameworks) shapes your campaign’s identity and long-term sustainability.

Episodic: Standalone arcs with optional continuity. Each arc resolves independently; players can miss sessions without losing narrative threads.

Linear: GM-authored plot with player-driven pacing. The campaign follows a central storyline, but players control how and when they engage plot beats.

Sandbox: Player-driven exploration with GM-authored locations and factions. Players choose where to go and what to pursue; the GM prepares reactive content.

Hexcrawl: Procedural exploration with emergent narrative. The campaign focuses on discovery and survival; story arises from exploration rather than authored plot.

Fronts-Based: GM-authored threats with player-chosen priorities. Multiple active threats progress independently; players decide which to address and when.

Faction-Driven: Living world with competing interests. Factions pursue goals with or without player involvement; players navigate and influence factional conflicts.

No framework is superior. Each supports different play styles and group preferences. Many successful campaigns blend frameworks, using episodic arcs for breathing room between linear plot beats or combining sandbox exploration with faction-driven politics.

Episodic Campaigns

Episodic campaigns prioritize flexibility and accessibility. Each arc is self-contained with beginning, middle, and end, though characters, setting, and some plot threads carry forward.

Strengths: Easy to add or remove players, forgiving of missed sessions, low continuity burden, allows genre and tone experimentation.

Challenges: Harder to build long-term stakes, requires strong arc-level storytelling, character arcs may feel fragmented without connective tissue.

Prep Requirements: Moderate. Design each arc independently with clear stakes and resolution. Prepare recurring NPCs and locations to provide continuity without requiring plot memory.

Session Structure: Each session advances the current arc. Sessions 1-2 establish the arc’s stakes and give players initial levers. Sessions 3-5 develop complications and escalate tension. Session 6 (or final session) delivers climax and resolution.

Continuity: Track recurring NPCs, character relationships, and setting changes. Each arc should reference past arcs lightly (callbacks, consequences) without requiring deep knowledge.

Example: A mercenary company takes contracts each arc. Arc 1: Escort a caravan through bandit territory. Arc 2: Investigate disappearances in a mining town. Arc 3: Protect a noble during a festival. Each arc is standalone, but NPCs recur (the company commander, favored clients) and character relationships deepen over time.

GM usage:

  • Design arcs with clear beginning, middle, and end
  • Include recurring NPCs and locations for continuity
  • Reference past arcs lightly; avoid requiring deep memory
  • Celebrate arc conclusions; treat each resolution as a mini-campaign finale

Linear Campaigns

Linear campaigns follow a central storyline with defined beginning, middle, and end. The GM authors the main plot, but players control pacing, methods, and side diversions.

Strengths: Strong narrative momentum, clear long-term goals, emotionally satisfying payoffs, easier to build thematic coherence.

Challenges: Requires consistent attendance, can feel railroaded if player agency is not prioritized, harder to accommodate unexpected player directions.

Prep Requirements: High upfront, moderate ongoing. Outline the campaign’s main beats (introduction, escalation, climax, resolution). Prepare flexible scenes that allow player choice within the larger structure.

Session Structure: Each session advances the main plot or develops characters and themes. Early sessions establish stakes and introduce the central conflict. Middle sessions escalate complications and test player plans. Late sessions build toward climax and resolution.

Player Agency: Avoid scripting outcomes. Linear structure means the central conflict is GM-authored, but how players engage that conflict is entirely their choice. Offer multiple paths to each plot beat.

Example: The campaign’s central plot: A sorcerer-tyrant seeks to awaken an ancient dragon. Players learn of the threat (arc 1), gather allies and resources (arc 2-3), confront the tyrant’s lieutenants (arc 4-5), and face the tyrant in a final confrontation (arc 6). Within this structure, players choose which allies to recruit, which lieutenants to fight first, and how to approach the finale.

GM usage:

  • Outline campaign beats but not specific scenes
  • Offer multiple paths to each plot milestone
  • Let players set the pace; do not force advancement
  • Include side arcs that develop characters or setting without advancing main plot

Sandbox Campaigns

Sandbox campaigns give players freedom to explore and pursue goals within a GM-authored setting. The GM prepares locations, NPCs, factions, and hooks, then responds to player choices.

Strengths: High player agency, emergent narrative, replayability (different groups explore different areas), supports varied play styles.

Challenges: Requires significant upfront prep, demands GM improvisation, can lack narrative cohesion without deliberate effort.

Prep Requirements: High upfront, low ongoing. Prepare a map with locations, factions, and hooks. Each location has NPCs, secrets, and potential conflicts. Factions have goals, assets, and clocks. Players choose where to go; you prepare content reactively.

Session Structure: Sessions begin with players choosing a goal or destination. Sessions develop based on player choices, ending when the immediate goal resolves or a new lever emerges. Use session-end questions to gauge player interest for next session’s prep.

GM Responsibilities: Maintain setting consistency, track faction clocks, introduce new hooks when players exhaust current interests, ensure player choices have visible consequences.

Example: The campaign is set in a frontier region with six towns, wilderness areas, and three active factions (merchant guild, bandit confederation, religious order). Players choose to investigate rumors of a bandit hideout. You prepare the hideout, its inhabitants, and complications. After the session, players decide to pursue the merchant guild’s offer of work, so you prepare that content for the next session.

GM usage:

  • Prepare locations and factions with goals, not plot
  • Offer 3-5 hooks each session; let players choose
  • Track faction clocks; advance them based on time passing or player actions
  • Use session-end questions to prep reactively

Hexcrawl Campaigns

Hexcrawl campaigns emphasize procedural exploration and survival. The campaign is structured around a map divided into hexes; players explore hexes, encounter procedural content, and discover emergent narrative.

Strengths: Discovery-focused, low continuity burden, supports drop-in play, generates emergent stories from exploration.

Challenges: Can feel aimless without long-term goals, requires procedural generation systems, may lack emotional depth without deliberate narrative scaffolding.

Prep Requirements: Moderate. Prepare a hex map with terrain types, landmarks, and encounter tables. Populate some hexes with authored content (ruins, faction strongholds, unique locations). Use tables for random encounters and discoveries in unpopulated hexes.

Session Structure: Sessions follow the exploration loop: travel (hex-by-hex movement, terrain challenges, random encounters), discover (new locations, NPCs, secrets), decide (rest, continue, investigate, retreat). Use clocks for resource depletion (supplies, time until winter) to create urgency.

Narrative Scaffolding: Add authored content (lost ruins with mysteries, faction conflicts, recurring NPCs) to provide narrative threads. Hexcrawls benefit from light overarching goals (find the lost city, map the region, establish trade routes) that emerge from exploration.

Example: The campaign is set in an uncharted wilderness. Players are explorers mapping the region for a patron. Each session, they travel 2-4 hexes, encountering terrain challenges, wildlife, ruins, and occasional NPCs. Over time, they discover a pattern: ancient ruins mark a lost civilization, and a hostile faction is also searching for these ruins. This emergent narrative becomes a long-term arc.

GM usage:

  • Prepare hex maps with terrain and landmarks
  • Use encounter tables for procedural content
  • Populate key hexes with authored content for narrative hooks
  • Track resources (supplies, time) with clocks to create urgency

Fronts-Based Campaigns

Fronts-based campaigns (inspired by Apocalypse World and Dungeon World) present multiple active threats that progress independently. Players choose which threats to address and when; unaddressed threats escalate.

Strengths: Creates urgency and meaningful choice, supports emergent priorities, generates dynamic world changes, rewards proactive play.

Challenges: Requires tracking multiple timelines, can overwhelm players with choices, risks losing narrative focus if threats are too disparate.

Prep Requirements: Moderate. Design 2-4 fronts (threats) with goals, stakes, clocks, and escalation stages. Each front has a countdown: if players do not intervene, the front progresses toward catastrophic outcomes.

Session Structure: Sessions begin with players choosing which front to address. As players engage a front, they learn more about it and gain tools to oppose it. Meanwhile, unaddressed fronts advance their clocks. Use session-end summaries to show which fronts progressed.

Front Design: Each front includes a threat (villain, disaster, societal collapse), impending doom (what happens if the front completes), stakes (who suffers, what is lost), and countdown (escalation stages as a clock advances).

Example: Three fronts: (1) A plague spreads from the slums (doom: city quarantine and mass death); (2) A noble house plots a coup (doom: civil war); (3) A cult summons a demon (doom: demonic incursion). Players choose to address the plague first, spending two sessions containing it. Meanwhile, the coup plot advances (one stage) and the cult advances (two stages). Players must now choose which escalating threat to tackle next.

GM usage:

  • Design 2-4 fronts with clocks and escalation stages
  • Advance front clocks when players pursue other goals or time passes
  • Show consequences of unaddressed fronts in narration
  • Let players choose which fronts to prioritize

Faction-Driven Campaigns

Faction-driven campaigns focus on competing groups with conflicting goals. Factions act independently, pursuing agendas and responding to player actions. Players navigate factional politics, form alliances, and influence outcomes.

Strengths: Creates living world feel, supports political and social play, generates emergent conflicts and alliances, rewards creative problem-solving.

Challenges: Requires tracking multiple factions and relationships, demands improvisation, can feel overwhelming if factions are too numerous or complex.

Prep Requirements: Moderate. Design 3-6 factions with goals, assets, weaknesses, and relationships. Give each faction a clock tracking progress toward their goals. Track player reputation with each faction.

Session Structure: Sessions involve players interacting with factions (negotiation, espionage, alliance-building, sabotage). Player actions affect faction clocks and relationships. Between sessions, advance faction clocks based on time passing or player inaction.

Faction Dynamics: Factions form alliances, rivalries, and neutral relationships. Player actions shift these dynamics. If players help Faction A against Faction B, A’s relationship improves and B’s worsens. If players betray A, A becomes hostile.

Example: Four factions: Merchant Guild (goal: dominate trade), Thieves’ Collective (goal: control black market), City Guard (goal: maintain order), Religious Order (goal: expand influence). Players work for the Merchant Guild, sabotaging the Thieves’ Collective. The Collective retaliates by framing the Guild for corruption. The City Guard investigates, and the Religious Order offers to mediate for a price. Players navigate this web, making alliances and enemies.

GM usage:

  • Design 3-6 factions with goals, clocks, and relationships
  • Track player reputation with each faction
  • Advance faction clocks between sessions
  • Show faction actions in narration, even when players are not directly involved

Blending Frameworks

Most campaigns benefit from blending frameworks. Use different frameworks for different campaign phases or combine elements from multiple frameworks simultaneously.

Episodic + Linear: Run standalone arcs (episodic) that also advance a long-term plot (linear). Example: Each arc resolves independently, but clues about the overarching villain emerge gradually across arcs.

Sandbox + Faction-Driven: Combine open exploration (sandbox) with living factions (faction-driven). Players explore freely, but factions pursue goals that create reactive narrative. Example: Players explore a region while three factions compete for control; player choices affect which faction succeeds.

Fronts + Linear: Use fronts to create urgency while maintaining a central plot. Example: The main plot is defeating the sorcerer-tyrant (linear), but multiple fronts (plague, coup, cult) provide side conflicts and time pressure.

Hexcrawl + Episodic: Run procedural exploration (hexcrawl) with authored mini-arcs (episodic) at discovered locations. Example: Players explore hexes procedurally, but key hexes contain authored content with 2-3 session arcs.

Blending requires clear communication. Tell players which framework is active and what it means for their choices. Example: “This arc is episodic; feel free to take risks without worrying about long-term consequences. Next arc will be more linear and tie into the campaign’s main plot.”

GM usage:

  • Choose 1-2 frameworks to blend; more than two risks confusion
  • Communicate framework shifts to players
  • Use one framework for structure (episodic, linear) and another for content (sandbox, factions, fronts)
  • Transition frameworks between arcs, not mid-arc

Choosing and Transitioning Frameworks

Choose a framework at Session Zero based on group preferences, GM prep capacity, and campaign goals. Reassess every few arcs and adjust if needed.

Choosing: Ask players during Session Zero:

  • Do you prefer standalone arcs or long-term plot? (Episodic vs Linear)
  • Do you want to explore freely or follow a central story? (Sandbox vs Linear)
  • Do you enjoy procedural discovery or authored narrative? (Hexcrawl vs Authored)
  • Do you like managing multiple threats or focusing on one? (Fronts vs Linear)
  • Do you enjoy political maneuvering and factional play? (Faction-Driven)

Match their answers to frameworks. If preferences conflict, blend frameworks or alternate by arc.

Transitioning: Campaigns can transition frameworks between arcs. Example: Start with episodic arcs to establish characters and setting, then transition to linear for a climactic campaign arc. Or begin with linear, then shift to sandbox after the main plot resolves, letting players explore the changed world.

Communicate transitions clearly: “We have finished the main plot. For the next phase, we will shift to sandbox play. You can explore the regions we have unlocked, pursue personal goals, or investigate rumors. What interests you?”

Red Flags: Reassess if:

  • Players feel railroaded (linear framework may need more sandbox elements)
  • Players feel aimless (sandbox may need fronts or linear structure)
  • Prep is overwhelming (reduce scope, shift to episodic or procedural)
  • Players disengage (framework may not match their preferences; check in and adjust)

GM usage:

  • Choose framework at Session Zero based on player preferences
  • Reassess every 3-4 arcs; adjust if engagement drops
  • Communicate framework shifts explicitly
  • Use transitions to refresh campaign energy and explore new play styles
