Mechanics as Story Tools

Table of Contents

  • Mechanics as Narrative Language
  • Tags as Fiction Levers
  • Conditions as Character State
  • Clocks as Dramatic Tension
  • Resources as Story Costs
  • Custom Mechanics for Genre and Theme
  • Worked Examples by Genre
    • Cozy: Village Harvest Festival
    • Fantasy: The Awakening Dragon
    • Horror: The House Remembers
    • Modern: Corporate Espionage
    • Science Fiction: Station Breach Protocol

Mechanics as Narrative Language

Mechanics do more than simulate reality. They create dramatic tension, signal meaning, and drive narrative forward. A clock is not just a counter; it is rising dread, approaching victory, or accumulating consequence. A condition is not just a debuff; it is exhaustion that makes every step harder, suspicion that poisons trust, or obligation that binds choices.

Think of mechanics as narrative language. Tags describe the fictional state and create fictional permissions. Conditions represent character state and emotional truth. Clocks track dramatic tension and make time visible. Resources measure cost and sacrifice. When you design or use mechanics, ask: What does this mean in the story? What feeling does it create?

This chapter expands on GM Guide Chapter 6 (Mechanics Overview) to show how mechanics become story tools. Rather than treating mechanics as neutral simulation, use them to reinforce genre, theme, and tone. A mystery campaign uses tags like Under Scrutiny and False Alibi. A horror campaign uses conditions like Rattled and Traumatized. A cozy campaign uses clocks for Harvest Progress and Festival Preparations.

Mechanics shape player experience. Heavy resource costs create survival tension. Frequent conditions create vulnerability and stakes. Fast-advancing clocks create urgency. Slow-advancing clocks create long-term investment. Choose mechanics that support the experience you want at the table.

Tags as Fiction Levers

Tags are narrative descriptors with mechanical weight. They establish fictional position, create permission for actions, and provide setup for payoff moments.

Position and Permission: Tags define what is true in the fiction and what actions are possible. A character with the Renowned Duelist tag can reasonably intimidate opponents with their reputation. A location with Heavily Guarded requires stealth or deception to bypass. Tags answer “Can I do this?” and “What is true here?”

Metaphor and Meaning: Tags can be literal (Burning, Locked) or metaphorical (Cursed with Doubt, Bound by Oath). Metaphorical tags create thematic resonance. A character with Haunted by the Past has mechanical disadvantage when confronting old enemies, but also narrative permission to recall crucial memories. Tags make theme tangible.

Setup and Payoff: Tags created in one scene can pay off in later scenes. A player creates Studied the Guard Patterns through preparation, then invokes it during infiltration for advantage. A villain gains Weakened Defenses from accumulated failures, creating an opening for the final confrontation. Tags make past actions matter.

Invoking and Compelling: Players invoke tags for advantage (bonus die, narrative permission, reroll). GMs compel tags to introduce complications (the Notorious tag attracts unwanted attention; the Wounded Leg tag prevents escape). Invocations reward clever setup. Compels create interesting problems.

GM usage:

  • Create situation tags each scene to establish fictional truth (weather, NPC moods, environmental hazards)
  • Encourage players to create tags through preparation or clever actions
  • Invoke tags to reinforce cause and effect (past actions create present advantages)
  • Compel tags to introduce complications that feel fair and narratively earned
  • Use metaphorical tags to reinforce campaign themes

Conditions as Character State

Conditions represent character state: physical harm, emotional strain, social consequence, or resource depletion. Conditions create stakes by making costs visible and accumulating pressure.

Emotional and Social Consequences: Conditions need not be only physical. A character can be Rattled (afraid, uncertain), Suspicious (distrusted by allies), Obligated (owes a debt), or Exhausted (mentally drained). Emotional and social conditions create stakes in non-combat scenes and support genre tone.

Accumulation and Breaking Points: Conditions stack. A character who is Injured and Exhausted and Demoralized is in serious trouble. Accumulation creates pressure and forces tough choices: push forward at risk, or rest and lose time. Define breaking points: how many conditions before a character cannot continue? Use breaking points to create dramatic moments (heroic last stands, desperate gambits, necessary retreats).

Recovery as Narrative Beat: Recovery is not just mechanical bookkeeping; it is a narrative beat. Resting to clear Exhausted is a campfire scene, a moment to reflect and bond. Clearing Suspicious requires social effort, a conversation or demonstrated loyalty. Recovery creates opportunities for character development and relationship beats.

Condition Types by Genre: Choose condition types that support your genre. Horror campaigns use Rattled, Traumatized, Paranoid. Cozy campaigns use Overworked, Flustered, Obligated (softer costs with social stakes). Political intrigue uses Compromised, Indebted, Exposed. Customize conditions to create the right feel.

GM usage:

  • Use emotional and social conditions alongside physical harm
  • Let conditions accumulate to create pressure and hard choices
  • Frame recovery as narrative beats, not just rest actions
  • Customize condition names and effects to fit genre and tone
  • Use breaking points to create dramatic moments and force meaningful decisions

Clocks as Dramatic Tension

Clocks (see GM Guide Chapter 6) track progress and pressure. They make time visible, create urgency, and turn abstract threats into tangible countdowns.

Progress vs Pressure: Progress clocks track player goals (Find the Cure, Earn the Baron’s Trust). Pressure clocks track threats or countdowns (Villain’s Ritual, Spreading Plague, Collapsing Building). Progress clocks reward momentum. Pressure clocks create urgency. Run both simultaneously to create tension: players race progress against pressure.

Nested Clocks: Use nested clocks for complex situations. A Siege of the Castle pressure clock contains smaller clocks for Breach the Outer Wall, Supplies Depleted, Morale Breaks. When a nested clock fills, advance the parent clock. Nested clocks create layered tension and multiple points of intervention.

Clock Visibility: Visible clocks create open tension. Players see the Dragon Awakens clock filling and know time is running out. Hidden clocks create mystery and dread. Players sense something is wrong but do not know how close danger is. Use visible clocks for known threats; hidden clocks for mysteries or slow-building horror.

Clock Pacing: Advance clocks based on narrative logic, not rigid timekeeping. Advance pressure clocks when players pursue other goals, when time passes in fiction, or when they ignore warnings. Advance progress clocks when players achieve meaningful milestones. Clocks create rhythm and consequence.

GM usage:

  • Run progress clocks (player goals) and pressure clocks (threats) simultaneously
  • Use nested clocks for complex situations with multiple failure points
  • Make clocks visible or hidden based on desired tension type
  • Advance clocks based on narrative logic and player choices
  • Use clocks to pace arcs and create urgency without scripting outcomes

Resources as Story Costs

Resources (gear, supplies, relationships, reputation, time) measure cost and sacrifice. Spending resources creates meaningful choices. Resource depletion creates stakes and drives narrative.

Gear and Supplies: Physical resources (ammo, rations, tools) create survival tension. Running low on supplies forces hard choices: ration carefully and risk failure, or spend freely and face scarcity later. Gear as resource works best in exploration or survival campaigns. Cozy campaigns might use supplies differently (ingredients for cooking, materials for crafting) to create low-stakes but meaningful decisions.

Relationships as Resources: Relationships can be spent. Calling in a favor advances the story but weakens the relationship. Asking an ally for help accumulates obligation. Relationships as resources create social stakes and force players to consider long-term costs of short-term gains.

Reputation and Influence: Reputation is a spendable resource. Using your fame to gain access or leverage reduces anonymity and increases scrutiny. In political or intrigue campaigns, reputation shifts based on choices. High reputation grants access but attracts enemies. Low reputation grants freedom but limits options.

Time as Resource: Time pressure turns time into a scarce resource. Players must choose how to spend limited time: rest to recover, investigate thoroughly, or act immediately. Time as resource creates urgency and forces prioritization. Use clocks to make time visible and spendable.

GM usage:

  • Choose resource types that support campaign genre and tone
  • Let resources deplete visibly; avoid infinite resources in survival or exploration campaigns
  • Frame resource spending as meaningful choices with narrative cost
  • Use relationships and reputation as spendable resources in social campaigns
  • Make time a resource through clocks and countdowns

Custom Mechanics for Genre and Theme

Bespoke mechanics reinforce genre and theme. Customize tags, conditions, clocks, and resources to match your campaign’s tone and focus.

Genre-Specific Tags: Create tags that signal genre. Horror campaigns use Cursed, Haunted, Forbidden Knowledge. Cozy campaigns use Well-Liked, Community Favorite, Master Chef. Science fiction uses Quantum Entangled, Nanotech Enhanced, AI-Linked. Genre tags create the right feel and encourage genre-appropriate actions.

Thematic Conditions: Design conditions that reflect campaign themes. A campaign about debt uses Indebted, Leveraged, Desperate. A campaign about identity uses Fractured, Lost, Transformed. Thematic conditions make abstract themes tangible and create mechanical stakes around theme.

Campaign-Specific Clocks: Create clocks for ongoing campaign threads. A rebellion campaign uses Popular Uprising, Government Crackdown, Safe Houses Discovered. A mystery campaign uses Suspicion Grows, Cover Story Unravels, Witness Testimony Collected. Campaign clocks make the world feel alive and responsive.

Custom Resources: Invent resources that fit your setting. A magic-heavy campaign uses Mana Reserves or Spell Components. A social intrigue campaign uses Favors Owed or Blackmail Material. A survival campaign uses Morale or Hope. Custom resources create unique decision points and reinforce setting flavor.

GM usage:

  • Design tags, conditions, clocks, and resources that reinforce genre and theme
  • Involve players in creating bespoke mechanics during Session Zero
  • Test custom mechanics in early sessions; adjust based on table feel
  • Avoid overloading the table with too many custom systems; start simple and add as needed
  • Use custom mechanics to make themes tangible and genre expectations clear

Worked Examples by Genre

Cozy: Village Harvest Festival

Genre Feel: Community-focused, low-stakes, warm relationships, small problems with meaningful solutions.

Custom Mechanics:

  • Tags: Beloved by Townsfolk, Master Baker, Festival Organizer, Overcommitted. Tags emphasize social bonds and skills, not combat prowess.
  • Conditions: Overworked, Flustered, Obligated, Out of Supplies. Conditions are social or logistical, not physical harm. Costs create stress but not danger.
  • Clocks: Festival Preparations (progress clock, 8 segments), Rival Village’s Display (pressure clock, 6 segments). Progress clock tracks community effort. Pressure clock creates friendly competition, not existential threat.
  • Resources: Festival Budget, Community Goodwill, Ingredients, Favors Owed. Resources are spent to solve problems (hire musicians, buy decorations, call in favors for help).

Scenario: The village’s annual Harvest Festival is in three days, but disaster strikes: the main tent collapses in a storm, the head baker sprains their wrist, and a rival village has announced an elaborate festival display. The party (local artisans, merchants, or visiting helpers) must rally the community to save the festival.

Mechanics in Action:

  • Tags as Setup: A player character with Master Baker can teach others to bake festival treats despite the injured baker. Another character with Beloved by Townsfolk can rally volunteers to repair the tent.
  • Conditions as Stakes: Characters who overwork themselves gain Overworked (disadvantage on checks until they rest). Saying yes to too many requests creates Obligated (must fulfill promises or damage Community Goodwill).
  • Clocks as Tension: The Festival Preparations progress clock advances with each successful effort (repairs, baking, decorations). The Rival Village’s Display pressure clock advances each day, representing the rival’s progress. If the progress clock fills before the festival, success. If the pressure clock fills first, the rival’s festival outshines theirs (embarrassing but not catastrophic).
  • Resources as Choices: The party spends Festival Budget to hire emergency labor or buy replacement materials. They spend Favors Owed to get help from neighboring farms. They spend Community Goodwill to ask for volunteers despite short notice.

Outcome: The mechanics create meaningful choices (spend money vs ask for favors? Prioritize repairs vs baking?) without danger. Failures add complications (decorations are mismatched, treats are slightly burnt) but no one is harmed. Success means a joyful festival and strengthened community bonds. Mechanics reinforce cozy tone: stakes are real but costs are soft.

Fantasy: The Awakening Dragon

Genre Feel: Epic stakes, heroic deeds, magical wonder, escalating danger, triumph over great evil.

Custom Mechanics:

  • Tags: Dragonslayer’s Heir, Blessed by the Oracle, Ancient Rune Knowledge, Dragon’s Mark (curse or blessing). Tags emphasize heroic lineage, destiny, and magical significance.
  • Conditions: Seared by Dragonfire, Cursed, Magically Drained, Wounded. Conditions are dramatic and fantastical, fitting epic stakes.
  • Clocks: Dragon Awakens (pressure clock, 8 segments), Forge the Dragon Bane Weapon (progress clock, 6 segments), Evacuate the Valley (progress clock, 4 segments). Multiple clocks create layered tension: can the heroes stop the dragon before it awakens fully? Can they save civilians?
  • Resources: Ancient Lore, Magical Components, Allied Forces, Time. Resources are spent to gain advantages (research dragon weaknesses, craft magical weapons, rally armies).

Scenario: An ancient dragon imprisoned beneath the mountain is awakening. Tremors shake the land. The party must either forge a legendary weapon to slay the dragon, perform a ritual to re-seal it, or evacuate the valley before the dragon emerges.

Mechanics in Action:

  • Tags as Destiny: A character with Dragonslayer’s Heir gains advantage when confronting the dragon, reinforcing heroic narrative. The tag Ancient Rune Knowledge allows deciphering the ritual to re-seal the dragon.
  • Conditions as Sacrifice: Forging the Dragon Bane weapon requires handling volatile magic; the smith risks gaining Magically Drained or Cursed. Confronting the dragon early risks Seared by Dragonfire. Conditions represent heroic sacrifice.
  • Clocks as Epic Countdown: The Dragon Awakens pressure clock advances each session or when the party pursues other goals. When it fills, the dragon emerges fully powered. The Forge the Dragon Bane Weapon progress clock advances with successful quests (gather rare materials, consult ancient smiths, survive magical trials). If the progress clock fills before the pressure clock, the party faces the dragon with advantage.
  • Resources as Heroic Effort: The party spends Ancient Lore (accumulated from earlier sessions) to learn the dragon’s weakness. They spend Allied Forces (rallied from nearby kingdoms) to evacuate civilians or create distractions. They spend Magical Components to empower the weapon or ritual.

Outcome: The mechanics create epic stakes and heroic tension. The countdown (dragon awakening) is visible and urgent. The progress clocks reward proactive heroism. Conditions make confronting the dragon costly but meaningful. Tags reinforce the fantasy of destined heroes facing mythic threats. The final confrontation feels earned, not scripted.

Horror: The House Remembers

Genre Feel: Creeping dread, psychological strain, uncertainty, isolation, uncovering terrible truths.

Custom Mechanics:

  • Tags: Haunted, Seen Too Much, Touched by the Other Side, Skeptic (becomes a liability as horror escalates). Tags reflect psychological states and supernatural influence.
  • Conditions: Rattled, Traumatized, Paranoid, Corrupted. Conditions are psychological and accumulate over time, creating breaking points.
  • Clocks: The House Awakens (pressure clock, 6 segments, hidden from players), Uncover the Truth (progress clock, 8 segments), Sanity (personal countdown clocks, 4 segments per character). Hidden pressure clock creates dread. Sanity clocks create personal stakes.
  • Resources: Trust (between party members), Supplies (light sources, tools), Sanity (personal resource spent to resist fear or push through horror).

Scenario: The party investigates an abandoned house where several people disappeared. The house is not just haunted; it is alive, sentient, and malevolent. The longer they stay, the more the house influences them. They must uncover the truth (what happened here? Why does the house hunger?) and escape before the house claims them.

Mechanics in Action:

  • Tags as Psychological States: A character gains Seen Too Much after witnessing a traumatic event (ghostly manifestation, corpse discovery). The tag grants insight into supernatural events but compels them to act recklessly or freeze in fear. The Skeptic tag initially protects against fear but becomes a compel when evidence mounts (denial prevents rational action).
  • Conditions as Breaking Points: Each frightening encounter risks adding a condition: Rattled (disadvantage on checks), Paranoid (distrust allies, see threats everywhere), Traumatized (flashbacks, panic). Accumulating conditions creates a spiral: fear makes characters less effective, leading to more failures and more fear. At four conditions, a character reaches a breaking point: they flee, collapse, or are claimed by the house.
  • Clocks as Creeping Dread: The hidden The House Awakens pressure clock advances when players linger, split up, or disturb certain areas. Players do not see it but feel its effects (lights fail, doors lock, whispers grow louder). When it fills, the house manifests fully and hunting begins. The Uncover the Truth progress clock advances with successful investigation: finding journals, interpreting symbols, confronting ghosts. Progress rewards courage but takes time, advancing pressure.
  • Resources as Desperation: Trust between party members depletes when paranoia takes hold or when characters act suspiciously. Low trust creates tension: are we safe together? Supplies (flashlights, batteries, tools) deplete through use and loss. Running out of light in a dark house is terrifying. Sanity can be spent to resist fear checks but runs out, forcing eventual breaking points.

Outcome: The mechanics create psychological horror. Conditions accumulate, creating a death spiral. The hidden pressure clock creates dread: something is happening, but players do not know how close they are to disaster. Scarcity (light, supplies, sanity) creates vulnerability. Trust as a resource creates interpersonal tension. Success means uncovering the truth and escaping before the house claims them, but at cost: characters are changed, traumatized, or lost.

Modern: Corporate Espionage

Genre Feel: High-stakes intrigue, social maneuvering, time pressure, technology and deception, contemporary consequences.

Custom Mechanics:

  • Tags: Insider Access, Digital Forensics Expert, Under Surveillance, Blackmail Material. Tags reflect modern skills, access, and complications.
  • Conditions: Exposed, Compromised, Burned (cover blown), Exhausted. Conditions are professional and social consequences.
  • Clocks: Acquire the Prototype (progress clock, 6 segments), Security Audit (pressure clock, 8 segments), Corporate Suspicion (pressure clock, 4 segments). Progress tracks the heist. Pressure tracks tightening security and rising suspicion.
  • Resources: Cover Identity, Hacking Tools, Insider Contacts, Time. Resources are spent to bypass security, gather intel, or create distractions.

Scenario: The party (freelance operatives or rival corporate agents) must infiltrate a tech corporation and steal a prototype device before it is moved to a secure facility in 72 hours. Security is tight, surveillance is everywhere, and any mistake risks exposure.

Mechanics in Action:

  • Tags as Access and Risk: Insider Access allows bypassing certain security (posing as employees, using stolen badges). Digital Forensics Expert grants advantage on hacking checks. Under Surveillance is a compel: security is watching, limiting risky actions. Blackmail Material can be invoked to coerce cooperation from an NPC but risks creating enemies.
  • Conditions as Professional Consequences: Exposed means the character’s face is flagged in security systems (must avoid cameras, change disguise). Compromised means an asset or contact is burned (loses access to resources). Burned means cover identity is blown (must operate in the open or flee). Conditions escalate consequences and force adaptation.
  • Clocks as Ticking Deadline: Acquire the Prototype progress clock advances with successful infiltration steps (gain access, bypass security, locate prototype, extract). Security Audit pressure clock advances with time passing or when players trip alarms. When it fills, security protocols tighten (extra guards, locked doors, surveillance intensifies). Corporate Suspicion pressure clock advances when players are sloppy or unlucky. When it fills, the corporation knows someone is targeting them and moves the prototype early.
  • Resources as Tools and Risks: Hacking Tools can be spent to bypass digital locks but risk detection (failed checks alert security). Insider Contacts provide intel or access but are finite; calling the same contact too often risks burning them. Cover Identity is a resource: each use risks exposure (checks to maintain cover under scrutiny).

Outcome: The mechanics create tense, high-stakes espionage. Clocks create urgency: the window is closing. Conditions escalate risk: exposure forces improvisation. Resources are finite: every tool used is a risk-reward decision. Tags provide capabilities but also vulnerabilities (surveillance, blackmail). Success means stealing the prototype before time runs out and escaping without being identified. Failure means getting caught, burned, or outmaneuvered by corporate security.

Science Fiction: Station Breach Protocol

Genre Feel: High-tech problem solving, vacuum and decompression danger, alien or AI threats, isolated setting, survival and ingenuity.

Custom Mechanics:

  • Tags: Vacuum Sealed, AI-Linked, Radiation Exposure, Engineering Genius, Alien Contamination. Tags blend tech, danger, and sci-fi competencies.
  • Conditions: Hypoxia, Irradiated, Contaminated, Systems Failure. Conditions reflect sci-fi dangers: environmental hazards, equipment failure, contamination.
  • Clocks: Restore Life Support (progress clock, 8 segments), Hull Integrity (pressure clock, 6 segments), Contamination Spread (pressure clock, 4 segments). Progress tracks survival efforts. Pressure tracks escalating crises.
  • Resources: Oxygen, Power Cells, Repair Materials, Suits. Resources are life-or-death: running out of oxygen or power means immediate danger.

Scenario: The space station suffers catastrophic damage: hull breach, life support failing, possible alien contamination in one sector. The crew (player characters) must restore life support, seal the breach, and contain contamination before oxygen runs out or the station tears apart.

Mechanics in Action:

  • Tags as Sci-Fi Abilities and Dangers: AI-Linked allows interfacing with station systems remotely, granting advantages on tech checks. Engineering Genius allows improvising repairs with limited materials. Vacuum Sealed means a section is safe from decompression but isolated. Alien Contamination is a compel: exposure risks infection or worse.
  • Conditions as Environmental Threats: Hypoxia (oxygen deprivation) accumulates in depressurized areas, creating disadvantage and eventual collapse. Irradiated comes from proximity to damaged reactor, requiring treatment or worsening over time. Contaminated results from exposure to alien material, creating unknown dangers (mutation? Mind control? Spreading infection?). Systems Failure applies to equipment: suits, tools, doors, all vulnerable to malfunction.
  • Clocks as Multiple Crises: Restore Life Support progress clock advances with successful engineering checks (reroute power, repair oxygen scrubbers, restart systems). Hull Integrity pressure clock advances with time or structural stress (explosions, failed repairs). When it fills, the station begins to tear apart (sections decompress, crew must evacuate). Contamination Spread pressure clock advances when the alien material is disturbed or ignored. When it fills, contamination reaches critical systems (life support, bridge) and becomes uncontainable.
  • Resources as Survival: Oxygen is tracked per character or per suit. Limited oxygen creates urgency and forces hard choices (who goes into vacuum? How long can we stay?). Power Cells run equipment: tools, doors, lights. Running out of power means working in darkness and manually overriding systems. Repair Materials are scarce: duct tape, welding supplies, spare parts. Improvising repairs risks failure. Suits are limited and can malfunction (Systems Failure condition), creating vulnerability.

Outcome: The mechanics create sci-fi survival tension. Multiple pressure clocks create cascading crises: fix one problem while others worsen. Resources are life-or-death: oxygen and power are time limits. Conditions reflect environmental dangers: vacuum, radiation, contamination. Tags grant sci-fi capabilities (AI interfacing, engineering genius) but also vulnerabilities (contamination, system dependency). Success means restoring life support, sealing the breach, and containing contamination before catastrophic failure. Failure means evacuation, loss of the station, or worse (contamination spreads, crew is lost).

Session Zero and Collaborative Worldbuilding

Table of Contents

  • Session Zero Agenda
  • Safety Tools and Calibration
  • Tone and Expectations Alignment
  • Collaborative Worldbuilding
  • Character Linking and Party Formation
  • Immediate Hooks and First Session Setup
  • Session Zero Checklist

Session Zero Agenda

Session Zero is the foundation for a successful campaign. It is a collaborative planning session where the table aligns on safety, tone, expectations, world details, and characters before play begins. A thorough Session Zero prevents mismatches, sets boundaries, and builds investment.

A typical Session Zero runs 2-3 hours and covers the following agenda. Adjust timing based on table needs.

Agenda Overview (2.5 hours):

  1. Safety Tools and Boundaries (20 minutes)
  2. Campaign Pitch and Tone (15 minutes)
  3. Mechanics Overview and Questions (20 minutes)
  4. Collaborative Worldbuilding (30 minutes)
  5. Character Creation and Linking (45 minutes)
  6. Immediate Hooks and First Session Preview (20 minutes)

Preparation: Before Session Zero, prepare a campaign pitch (genre, tone, core premise, expected length) and decide on safety tools. Have character creation materials ready (see Player Guide and GM Guide Chapter 7). Bring snacks and create a welcoming atmosphere; Session Zero sets the tone for the entire campaign.

Safety Tools and Calibration

Safety tools create a space where everyone can enjoy the game without crossing personal boundaries. Implementing safety tools is not optional; it is fundamental to respectful play.

Lines and Veils

Lines are topics that will not appear in the game at all. They are hard boundaries. Examples: sexual violence, harm to children, graphic torture, spiders (for arachnophobes). If something is a line, it does not exist in the game world.

Veils are topics that can exist in the game world but are not described in detail. They happen off-screen or are summarized. Examples: violence is described but not graphically, romantic relationships fade to black, disease exists but symptoms are not detailed.

How to Implement:

  1. At the start of Session Zero, explain lines and veils clearly.
  2. Provide paper or a shared document where everyone (including the GM) can anonymously write their lines and veils.
  3. Collect and compile the list. The GM reviews and commits to honoring all lines and veils.
  4. Revisit lines and veils periodically (every few sessions or when new players join).

GM Responsibility: The GM enforces lines and veils. If a player accidentally approaches a line or veil, the GM redirects (“That is a veil for this table; let us summarize instead”). If a player repeatedly ignores lines or veils, address it privately and, if necessary, remove them from the table.

X-Card

The X-Card is a safety tool allowing anyone to stop play when content becomes uncomfortable. It is simple and non-disruptive.

How to Implement:

  1. Place a card marked with an X in the center of the table (or use a digital equivalent in online play).
  2. At Session Zero, explain: “If at any time something in the game makes you uncomfortable, tap the X-Card or type ‘X’ in chat. No questions asked, no explanation required. We will rewind or change the scene immediately.”
  3. When someone uses the X-Card, stop the current scene. Ask: “Do we rewind and take a different direction, or skip this entirely?” Respect the answer. Do not ask why or pressure for explanation.

Normalization: Use the X-Card yourself as GM during Session Zero to model its use. Example: “If someone describes a plan involving [topic you find uncomfortable], I might X-Card it. This tool is for everyone.”

Open Door Policy

The Open Door policy allows anyone to leave the session at any time without explanation or judgment. It acknowledges that people have limits and respects their need to step away.

How to Implement:

  1. At Session Zero, state: “If you need to leave at any time, for any reason, you can. No explanation needed. Take care of yourself.”
  2. If someone leaves mid-session, do not follow them immediately or demand reasons. Check in privately after the session if appropriate.

Script Change

Script Change is a set of verbal cues allowing players to adjust content intensity mid-scene.

Cues:

  • Rewind: “Let us go back and take a different direction.”
  • Fast Forward: “Let us skip this part and summarize the outcome.”
  • Pause: “I need a moment to process; let us take a quick break.”

How to Implement:

  1. Explain the cues at Session Zero.
  2. Encourage anyone (players or GM) to use them without explanation.
  3. When a cue is used, honor it immediately.

Calibration and Check-Ins

Calibration is ongoing safety work. Check in with the table regularly to ensure everyone is comfortable and having fun.

How to Calibrate:

  • Mid-Session Check: During breaks, ask: “How is everyone feeling? Too intense? Too slow? Anything we should adjust?”
  • Post-Session Debrief: Spend 5-10 minutes after sessions asking: “What worked? What did not? Anything we should change?”
  • Anonymous Feedback: Provide a way for players to give anonymous feedback (shared document, survey, note cards) for those uncomfortable speaking up.

GM usage:

  • Implement multiple safety tools; different people prefer different tools
  • Explain all safety tools clearly at Session Zero
  • Model use of safety tools yourself to normalize them
  • Honor safety tool use immediately and without question
  • Check in regularly; safety is ongoing, not one-time setup

Tone and Expectations Alignment

Tone and expectations must align across the table. Mismatched expectations create friction. Session Zero is where alignment happens.

Campaign Pitch

The GM presents the campaign pitch: genre, tone, premise, scope, and expected length. The pitch sets expectations and allows players to opt in.

Elements of a Pitch:

  • Genre and Tone: What kind of story is this? (Mystery, horror, cozy, high fantasy, political intrigue, etc.) What is the emotional tone? (Dark and serious, lighthearted, heroic, tense, etc.)
  • Core Premise: What is the campaign about? (Investigating supernatural mysteries, surviving a zombie apocalypse, running a small-town bakery, overthrowing a tyrant, exploring strange worlds, etc.)
  • Expected Length: How many sessions? (One-shot, short arc of 3-6 sessions, ongoing campaign, open-ended until natural conclusion.)
  • Player Agency: How much is authored by the GM vs driven by players? (Linear plot, sandbox exploration, faction-driven emergent narrative, etc.)
  • Content and Themes: What themes will appear? (Betrayal, loyalty, sacrifice, community, corruption, redemption, etc.) What mature content might be present? (Violence, horror, political conflict, romance, etc.)

Example Pitch: “This is a six-session mystery horror campaign. The premise: you are investigators exploring a series of disappearances in a remote mountain town. Tone is tense and atmospheric, with creeping dread and occasional scares. Expect investigation, social interaction, and some combat. Violence and horror will be present but not graphic (veils apply). Player choices will determine which leads you follow and how the mystery unfolds. Themes: isolation, trust, uncovering uncomfortable truths.”

Genre Signals and Expectations

Once the pitch is delivered, discuss genre signals and expectations. What does this genre mean to each player? What tropes are exciting? What cliches should be avoided?

Discussion Questions:

  • What excites you about this genre?
  • What tropes or elements do you want to see?
  • What cliches or elements do you want to avoid?
  • What is your experience with this genre? (New to horror? Veteran mystery player? Never played cozy games?)

GM Role: Listen and adjust. If the table is excited about elements you had not planned, incorporate them. If the table dislikes elements central to your pitch, discuss alternatives or reconsider the campaign.

Campaign Framework Preview

Briefly describe the campaign framework (see Chapter 6: Campaign Frameworks): episodic, linear, sandbox, fronts-based, faction-driven, or hybrid. Explain what this means for session structure and player agency.

Examples:

  • Episodic: Each session is a standalone mystery with optional continuity. Players can expect contained stories with clear resolution each session.
  • Sandbox: The world is open; players choose where to go and what to pursue. Sessions are emergent based on player decisions.
  • Fronts-Based: Multiple threats exist; players choose which to prioritize. The world changes based on inaction or action.

GM usage:

  • Present a clear, specific campaign pitch
  • Discuss genre expectations and tropes
  • Explain the campaign framework and what it means for play
  • Listen to player feedback and adjust if mismatches arise
  • Ensure everyone is excited about the campaign before proceeding

Collaborative Worldbuilding

Collaborative worldbuilding creates shared investment. When players help build the world, they care about it. This section provides prompts and structures for collaborative worldbuilding during Session Zero.

Worldbuilding Boundaries

Set boundaries: What is fixed (core premise, major factions, key NPCs) and what is open for collaboration (locations, minor NPCs, local culture, recent events)?

Example Boundaries: “Fixed: The kingdom is ruled by a weak monarch; three noble houses vie for power; magic is rare and dangerous. Open: Your characters’ home regions, local customs, what your family does, recent events in your area.”

Collaborative Prompts

Ask players to contribute world details during Session Zero. Use prompts that tie to their characters or the setting.

Location Prompts:

  • “Where is your character from? Describe the place: what is it known for?”
  • “Name a local landmark or feature in your region. Why is it significant?”
  • “What is the local industry or resource? How does it shape the culture?”

Cultural Prompts:

  • “What is a local festival or tradition? What does it celebrate?”
  • “What is a common saying or superstition in your region?”
  • “What is the relationship between your region and the capital/central authority?”

NPC Prompts:

  • “Who is someone important in your character’s life? Describe them.”
  • “Who is a local figure everyone knows (mayor, merchant, elder, troublemaker)? What are they like?”
  • “Who is someone your character trusts? Who is someone they distrust?”

Recent Events Prompts:

  • “What recent event has everyone talking in your region?”
  • “What local problem or conflict is brewing?”
  • “What is a local mystery or rumor?”

GM Role: Record contributions. Build on player ideas. Connect disparate contributions into a cohesive world. Example: One player says their region is known for mining; another says their character distrusts a local merchant. The GM connects them: the merchant is accused of selling substandard mining equipment, leading to recent accidents.

Veto Rights and Collaboration Limits

GMs retain veto rights. If a player contribution breaks the world, contradicts core premises, or creates tonal mismatch, the GM can veto and propose alternatives.

How to Veto Respectfully:

  1. Acknowledge the contribution: “I like that idea.”
  2. Explain the conflict: “It does not fit because [reason].”
  3. Offer an alternative: “What if instead [adjusted version]?”

Example: Player: “My character is from a high-tech city with robots.” GM: “I like the advanced city idea, but robots do not fit this low-magic medieval setting. What if instead your city is known for advanced clockwork and intricate machines, but no true AI or robots?” Player: “That works!”

Shared Worldbuilding Tools

Use tools to capture collaborative worldbuilding:

  • Shared Document: Google Doc or equivalent where everyone records contributions.
  • Index Cards: Physical or digital cards for locations, NPCs, factions. Players can add cards during Session Zero.
  • Map: Collaborative map where players mark their home regions, landmarks, or points of interest.

GM usage:

  • Set clear boundaries: what is fixed, what is open for collaboration
  • Use prompts to guide contributions toward useful details
  • Record all contributions and build connections between them
  • Veto respectfully if necessary; offer alternatives
  • Use shared tools to make worldbuilding visible and accessible

Character Linking and Party Formation

Characters need reasons to work together. Character linking creates pre-existing relationships and shared goals, avoiding the “you all meet in a tavern” problem.

Linking Methods

Method 1: Shared Background: All characters come from the same place, organization, or event. Examples: same hometown, same military unit, same guild, survivors of the same disaster.

Method 2: Web of Relationships: Characters know each other through chains of relationships. Player A knows Player B from childhood. Player B met Player C during travels. Player C owes Player D a debt. Everyone is connected by one or two degrees.

Method 3: Shared Goal: Characters have different backgrounds but share an immediate goal. Examples: all hired by the same patron, all seeking the same artifact, all fleeing the same threat.

Method 4: Dramatic Hook: Characters are thrown together by circumstance. Examples: all passengers on a crashing ship, all witnesses to a crime, all accused of a crime they did not commit.

Linking Questions

Use questions to create links between characters:

  • “How do you know [other character]? When did you meet?”
  • “What is your relationship? (Friends, rivals, family, professional colleagues, strangers with common cause?)”
  • “Why do you trust [other character]? Or do you?”
  • “What do you owe [other character], or what do they owe you?”
  • “What shared experience binds you together?”

Bonds and Flags

Encourage players to create bonds (relationships) and flags (narrative hooks) during character creation. Bonds tie characters together. Flags tell the GM what each player wants to explore.

Bonds (see Player Guide): Mechanical and narrative links between characters. Example: “I owe [character] a life debt” or “I am protecting [character] from a secret.”

Flags (see Player Guide): Topics, themes, or story beats players want to engage. Example: “I want to explore my character’s fear of failure” or “I want to uncover my character’s mysterious past.”

GM usage:

  • Use linking methods to create party cohesion before play begins
  • Ask linking questions to establish relationships and history
  • Encourage bonds and flags; record them and incorporate into play
  • Ensure every character has at least one link to another character
  • Build immediate hooks that engage all characters, not just individuals

Immediate Hooks and First Session Setup

Session Zero ends with setup for the first session. Provide immediate hooks so players know what their characters are doing when play begins.

The Opening Scene

Describe the opening scene of Session One. Where are the characters? What is happening? What stakes are immediately present?

Example Openings:

  • “Session One begins with all of you gathered in the village square. The mayor has just announced that children have gone missing. Parents are frantic. The mayor looks at you and says, ‘You are outsiders, but you have skills. Will you help?’”
  • “Session One begins mid-action: you are in the ruins, torches guttering, as you hear footsteps echoing behind you. Whatever you woke up is hunting you now.”
  • “Session One begins at the festival. Music plays, people celebrate, but you all notice something is wrong. The mayor is acting strangely. What do you do?”

Immediate Questions

Give players immediate questions to answer during the first session. Questions create engagement and agency.

Examples:

  • “What do you do first: investigate the disappearances, talk to the parents, or search the last known location?”
  • “How do you react when the mayor asks for help? Do you agree immediately, negotiate payment, or hesitate?”
  • “What catches your attention at the festival? The mayor’s strange behavior, the nervous guards, or the hooded stranger watching from the shadows?”

Prep Between Session Zero and Session One

Ask players to prep between Session Zero and Session One:

  • Finalize character details (appearance, personality, bonds, flags).
  • Think about character goals for the first session.
  • Review mechanics relevant to their character (skills, conditions, resources).

GM prep between sessions:

  • Incorporate collaborative worldbuilding contributions into session notes.
  • Build NPCs and locations based on player contributions.
  • Design the first session to engage character bonds and flags.
  • Prepare opening scene and immediate hooks.

GM usage:

  • Describe the opening scene clearly
  • Provide immediate questions and stakes
  • Give players time to ask questions about the setup
  • Assign light prep for both players and GM between Session Zero and Session One
  • Start Session One with momentum; recap Session Zero contributions briefly and dive in

Session Zero Checklist

Use this checklist to ensure a complete Session Zero. Adjust based on table needs.

Before Session Zero:

During Session Zero:

After Session Zero:

Optional Additions:

GM usage:

  • Use this checklist to track Session Zero tasks
  • Adjust timing and content based on table size and experience
  • Prioritize safety tools; do not skip them
  • Ensure every player contributes to worldbuilding and character linking
  • End Session Zero with clear momentum toward Session One

Advanced Improvisation Techniques

Table of Contents

  • Improvisation Foundations
  • Reincorporation and Callbacks
  • Emergent Narrative from Mechanics
  • Converting Player Ideas to Mechanics
  • Handling Unexpected Directions
  • Preparation for Improvisation
  • Worked Examples
    • Example 1: Player Invents an NPC
    • Example 2: Failed Check Creates New Direction
    • Example 3: Players Ignore the Hook

Improvisation Foundations

Improvisation is the art of building narrative collaboratively in the moment. No amount of preparation eliminates improvisation; players will always surprise you. Embracing improvisation makes your game flexible, responsive, and alive.

Good improvisation is not making things up randomly. It is building on established fiction, listening to player input, and using frameworks to guide choices. Improvisation requires preparation: not rigid plans, but flexible tools and prompts.

Yes-And

Yes-And is the foundational improvisation technique. When a player proposes something, your default response is to accept it (Yes) and build on it (And).

Yes: Accept the player’s contribution as true within the fiction. If a player says, “I search the desk for secret compartments,” they are proposing that secret compartments might exist. Yes means acknowledging the possibility.

And: Build on the contribution. Add complexity, consequence, or connection. “Yes, you find a secret compartment, and inside is a coded letter. The handwriting matches the mayor’s.”

When to Say No: Yes-And is not absolute. Say No when the contribution breaks established fiction, contradicts core premises, or undermines safety (crosses lines or veils). When you say No, offer an alternative: “No, the castle is not made of glass (that contradicts the medieval setting), but it could have stained glass windows famous throughout the land.”

Practicing Yes-And: Train yourself to default to acceptance. Before saying No, ask: Does this break anything essential? If not, say Yes-And. Players will surprise you with brilliant ideas if you let them.

Reframe

Reframing is adjusting a player contribution to fit the fiction without rejecting it outright.

Example: Player: “I convince the guard to let us in.” GM: “He is loyal and does not budge, but he mentions that the side gate is less guarded. You could reframe your approach or try a different angle.”

Reframing honors player intent while steering toward fictional consistency. It is a softer tool than No.

Build on Player Input

Players constantly provide input: through questions, speculation, character actions, and table talk. Mine this input for improvisational gold.

Listen for Questions: Player questions reveal interest. If a player asks, “Is the innkeeper acting nervous?” they are interested in the innkeeper. Make the innkeeper significant, even if you had not planned it.

Listen for Speculation: When players speculate about the plot, they are offering ideas. If a player says, “What if the mayor is secretly funding the bandits?” and it does not contradict your prep, consider adopting it. Players feel brilliant when their speculations come true.

Listen for Actions: Player actions reveal priorities. If players spend time investigating the docks despite your planned noble intrigue plot, the docks matter to them. Adjust accordingly.

GM usage:

  • Default to Yes-And when players contribute ideas
  • Reframe contributions that do not fit, rather than rejecting outright
  • Listen actively to player questions, speculation, and actions
  • Use player input to guide improvisation
  • Say No only when necessary; offer alternatives when you do

Reincorporation and Callbacks

Reincorporation is bringing back earlier details in new contexts. Callbacks are explicit references to past events. Both techniques create cohesion and reward player attention.

Reincorporation

Reincorporation makes seemingly minor details significant. A throwaway NPC mentioned in Session One becomes crucial in Session Three. A random clue becomes the key to the mystery. Reincorporation creates the feeling of a planned, interconnected world, even when improvised.

How to Reincorporate:

  1. Take Notes: During sessions, jot down details players notice or ask about. These are seeds for reincorporation.
  2. Review Between Sessions: Before the next session, review notes and identify details to reincorporate.
  3. Elevate Minor Details: Take a minor detail and give it new significance. The bartender mentioned once becomes an informant. The strange symbol noticed in Session One appears again, now with meaning.

Example: Session One: Players pass through a market and notice a merchant selling unusual charms. The GM notes this. Session Three: Players need information about a magical artifact. The GM reincorporates: “You remember the merchant in the market who sold unusual charms. Perhaps they know something about magical artifacts.”

Callbacks

Callbacks are explicit references to past events, reinforcing continuity and player impact.

Types of Callbacks:

  • Character Callbacks: NPCs remember past interactions. “You helped my village. I will not forget that.”
  • Location Callbacks: Returning to locations shows change. “The inn you saved from the fire has been rebuilt. The owner greets you warmly.”
  • Consequence Callbacks: Past choices have present effects. “The bandit you spared has joined the city guard. He recognizes you and nods in thanks.”

How to Create Callbacks:

  1. Track Player Choices: Note decisions with potential long-term consequences.
  2. Show Change: When players return to a location or meet an NPC affected by past actions, show how things have changed.
  3. Reward or Complicate: Callbacks can reward (allies remember kindness) or complicate (enemies remember slights).

GM usage:

  • Take detailed session notes; minor details become reincorporation seeds
  • Review notes between sessions to identify reincorporation opportunities
  • Elevate minor details by giving them new significance
  • Use callbacks to show player impact and reinforce continuity
  • Balance rewarding callbacks and complicating callbacks

Emergent Narrative from Mechanics

Mechanics generate narrative. Dice results, conditions, clocks, and tags create prompts for improvisation. Rather than fighting mechanical outcomes, use them to drive story.

Failures as Prompts

Failed checks are not dead ends; they are complications and new directions. When a player fails, ask: What interesting consequence moves the story forward?

Example: A player fails to pick a lock. Instead of “You do not open it,” the GM says: “You open it, but it takes too long. You hear footsteps approaching. What do you do?”

Failures create urgency, introduce new problems, or reveal information. Use the outcome tiers (see GM Guide Chapter 6): Fail means complication, not blockage.

Partials as Opportunities

Partial successes offer built-in improvisation: success with cost or complication. Use partials to introduce interesting choices or narrative twists.

Example: A player partially succeeds at persuading a merchant. GM: “He agrees to provide the supplies, but he wants a favor in return: deliver a package to someone in the next town. He will not say what is inside.”

Partials add texture and consequence. They create new levers for future scenes.

Conditions as Story Prompts

Conditions (Injured, Exhausted, Suspicious, etc.) are narrative hooks. When a player gains a condition, describe it fictionally and use it to shape scenes.

Example: A character gains the Exhausted condition. GM: “You are exhausted. Your hands shake as you try to prepare the ritual. How does this exhaustion manifest? What are you thinking about?”

Conditions invite player collaboration in describing their character’s state. They create opportunities for character moments.

Clocks as Narrative Drivers

When clocks advance, describe the change in the fiction. Clocks create visible momentum and prompt improvisation.

Example: The “Villain’s Ritual” pressure clock advances. GM: “As you rest, you feel a tremor. The sky darkens briefly. Whatever the cultists are doing, it is progressing. Locals are starting to panic.”

Advancing clocks force decisions and create urgency. They turn abstract threats into immediate concerns.

GM usage:

  • Use failures to introduce complications or urgency, not blockage
  • Frame partials as successes with interesting costs or choices
  • Treat conditions as narrative prompts; describe them fictionally
  • When clocks advance, describe the change in the world
  • Let mechanics drive narrative rather than resisting mechanical outcomes

Converting Player Ideas to Mechanics

Players propose actions or ideas that do not map neatly to existing mechanics. Improvise mechanics on the fly to support player creativity.

Creating On-the-Fly Tags

When a player describes a creative action, create a tag to represent it.

Example: Player: “I spend the evening studying the guard patrol routes.” GM: “Great. Roll Awareness. Success: You create the tag ‘Studied the Routes,’ which you can invoke later for advantage during infiltration.”

On-the-fly tags reward preparation and clever thinking. They create setup-payoff moments.

Improvising Clocks

When a player pursues a long-term goal not covered by existing clocks, create one.

Example: Player: “I want to earn the blacksmith’s trust so he will share information about the strange metal.” GM: “Good plan. I am creating a ‘Earn Blacksmith’s Trust’ progress clock with 4 segments. Each time you help him or share something valuable, we will advance it.”

Clocks make abstract goals tangible and track progress.

Generating NPCs on the Spot

When players ask about an NPC you have not prepared, improvise.

Quick NPC Framework:

  1. Name: Use a name generator or pick something simple.
  2. Trait: One defining trait (nervous, greedy, kind, suspicious).
  3. Want: What does this NPC want right now?
  4. Connection: How does this NPC connect to the larger situation?

Example: Player: “Is there anyone in the tavern who looks like they know about the ruins?” GM (improvising): “Yes, an old woman sits in the corner. She is called Mara. She is wary but not hostile. She wants to warn people away from the ruins, but she also wants to know why you are interested. She lost her son in the ruins twenty years ago.”

Quick NPCs can be detailed later. Get them into play and refine as needed.

Modular Scenes and Encounters

Prepare modular content: scenes, NPCs, or encounters not tied to specific locations or times. When players take unexpected directions, deploy modular content.

Example: You prepared a market encounter where players meet an informant. Players skip the market. The informant can appear at the tavern instead. Modular content is flexible.

GM usage:

  • Create tags on the fly to reward creative player actions
  • Improvise clocks for player-initiated goals
  • Use quick NPC frameworks to generate NPCs in the moment
  • Prepare modular scenes and encounters deployable anywhere
  • Record improvised content (tags, clocks, NPCs) in notes for consistency

Handling Unexpected Directions

Players will take unexpected directions. They will ignore hooks, pursue tangents, or invent new goals. Handling unexpected directions gracefully is essential to improvisation.

Pivot Techniques

When players take an unexpected direction, pivot your prep rather than railroading them back.

Technique 1: Relocate Prep: Move prepared content to the new location. If players go to the docks instead of the noble’s estate, the clue you placed at the estate appears at the docks instead.

Technique 2: Adjust NPCs: Change which NPC delivers information or provides hooks. If players befriend the bartender instead of the guard captain, the bartender becomes the information source.

Technique 3: Reframe Stakes: If players ignore the main plot, make the main plot come to them. The cultists they ignored attack the tavern. The noble they avoided sends an assassin. Consequences create re-engagement.

Ask Questions

When players take an unexpected direction, ask questions to understand their goals.

Examples:

  • “What are you hoping to find by investigating the docks?”
  • “Why is earning the merchant’s trust important to you?”
  • “What is your character’s goal here?”

Player answers reveal intent. Once you understand intent, you can build content that satisfies it.

Offer Choices

When the path forward is unclear, offer choices to players.

Example: GM: “You have learned the cult meets in either the warehouse district or the old temple. Time is short. Where do you go?”

Offering choices restores player agency and allows the GM to focus prep on chosen paths.

Retcon Protocols

Sometimes improvisation creates contradictions or mistakes. Retconning (retroactively changing established facts) is acceptable if done carefully.

When to Retcon:

  • When an improvised detail contradicts core fiction or themes
  • When a mistake breaks internal consistency
  • When the table agrees a change improves the story

How to Retcon:

  1. Acknowledge the issue: “I realize I said the vault was locked, but I forgot the rogue has the key. Let us rewind.”
  2. Propose the retcon: “Instead of locked, let us say it is trapped. Does that work?”
  3. Confirm with the table: “Everyone okay with that adjustment?”

Retcons are tools, not failures. Use them to maintain consistency, not to take back player victories.

GM usage:

  • Pivot prep rather than railroading players back to planned content
  • Relocate prepared scenes, adjust NPCs, or reframe stakes to fit new directions
  • Ask players questions to understand unexpected actions and goals
  • Offer choices when paths are unclear
  • Use retcons carefully to maintain consistency; confirm with the table

Preparation for Improvisation

Improvisation requires preparation. The paradox of improvisation: the more you prepare, the easier it is to improvise. Prepare flexible tools, not rigid scripts.

Flexible Prep

Flexible prep includes:

  • NPCs Not Tied to Locations: Create NPCs with goals and personalities, but do not lock them to specific places. They can appear wherever players go.
  • Modular Encounters: Prepare encounters (social, combat, exploration) deployable in multiple contexts.
  • Clue Lists: Prepare clues for mysteries, but do not tie them to specific locations. Deploy them wherever players investigate.
  • Situation, Not Plot: Prepare situations (conflicts, threats, opportunities) rather than plots. Situations react to player actions; plots railroad.

Example: Instead of “Players infiltrate the estate, find the clue in the study, and confront the noble,” prepare: “The noble is hiding evidence of their involvement. The evidence could be in the estate, with an ally, or in a safe deposit. Players choose how to investigate.”

NPC Banks

Create an NPC bank: a list of 5-10 NPCs with names, traits, and wants. When you need an NPC, pull from the bank.

NPC Bank Template:

  • Name: [Name]
  • Trait: [One defining trait]
  • Want: [What they want]
  • Connection: [How they connect to the setting or plot]

Example NPC Bank Entry:

  • Name: Joran the Fence
  • Trait: Greedy but reliable
  • Want: Wants to expand his smuggling operation
  • Connection: Has contacts in the city guard; can provide information or illegal goods

Location Banks

Create a location bank: a list of interesting locations with brief descriptions. When players go somewhere unexpected, pull from the bank.

Location Bank Template:

  • Name: [Location name]
  • Description: [One-sentence description]
  • Notable Feature: [One interesting detail]
  • Potential Hook: [How this location could matter]

Example Location Bank Entry:

  • Name: The Drowned Pier
  • Description: An abandoned pier, half-collapsed into the bay
  • Notable Feature: Local rumor says smugglers use it at night
  • Potential Hook: Could contain smuggled goods, a hidden meeting place, or clues about missing cargo

Scenario Frameworks

Use frameworks to structure improvisation. Frameworks provide guardrails without scripting outcomes.

Mystery Framework: Three-clue rule (see Chapter 10). Players need three paths to each key revelation. Prepare clues, not solutions. Let players connect dots.

Social Framework: Goals, leverage, costs (see Chapter 11). Each NPC has a goal, leverage to offer or withhold, and costs they will pay. Improvise dialogue but stick to framework.

Combat Framework: Environment, enemies, objectives (see GM Guide Chapter 11). Prepare set pieces with interesting terrain and clear objectives. Improvise tactics based on player actions.

GM usage:

  • Prepare flexible, modular content not tied to specific sequences
  • Create NPC and location banks for quick deployment
  • Use frameworks (mystery, social, combat) to structure improvisation
  • Prepare situations and stakes, not rigid plots
  • Review and refine banks and frameworks between sessions

Worked Examples

Example 1: Player Invents an NPC

Setup: The party is investigating disappearances in a city. They decide to visit the docks to gather information. The GM has not prepared dock NPCs.

Player Action: “I approach a dockworker and ask if they have seen anything strange. Maybe someone who works here regularly would know?”

Improvisation in Action:

  1. Yes-And: The GM accepts the proposal. “Yes, there is a dockworker who has been here for years. Let me create them.”

  2. Quick NPC Creation:

    • Name: The GM quickly picks “Tam.”
    • Trait: Nervous and observant (fits the mystery tone).
    • Want: Tam wants to avoid trouble but is worried about the disappearances.
    • Connection: Tam saw something but is afraid to speak openly.

  3. Introduce the NPC: GM: “You see Tam, a dockworker unloading crates. He is nervous, glancing around frequently. When you approach, he tenses but does not run.”

  4. Build on Player Input: The player rolls Influence (social skill). Partial success. GM: “Tam is reluctant but talks. He says he saw a ship unload strange cargo late at night three days ago. Hooded figures, no insignia. He did not ask questions; people who ask questions around here disappear. He will say no more unless you promise to protect him.”

  5. Create a Hook: Tam becomes a recurring NPC. If players promise protection, Tam provides more information later. If players ignore him, Tam might disappear next, escalating stakes.

Outcome: The GM improvised an NPC in the moment using a quick framework. The NPC feels real and connected to the plot. The player’s idea (talking to a dockworker) created a new lead and potential ally. The improvisation honors player agency and moves the story forward.

Example 2: Failed Check Creates New Direction

Setup: The party is infiltrating a mansion to steal a document. The rogue attempts to pick the lock on the study door.

Player Action: The player rolls to pick the lock. Result: Fail.

Improvisation in Action:

  1. Failure as Complication: The GM does not say “You fail to pick the lock, and nothing happens.” Instead: “The lock is more complex than expected. You struggle with it, and it takes longer than planned. You hear footsteps approaching down the hall. Someone is coming. What do you do?”

  2. Create Urgency: The failed check introduces a complication: time pressure. The party must decide quickly: hide, bluff, fight, or flee.

  3. Build on Player Response: The players decide to hide in a side room. They roll Stealth. Partial success. GM: “You hide, but one of you knocks over a vase. It clatters. The approaching guard pauses. ‘Who is there?’ he calls out. He is cautious but investigating.”

  4. Emergent Narrative: The guard was not part of the original plan. The failed lock pick created him. Now the players must deal with the guard: distract him, ambush him, talk their way out, or wait for him to leave. The story branches in an unplanned but interesting direction.

  5. Advance a Clock: The GM advances the “Mansion Security Alert” pressure clock. If it fills, the entire mansion goes on lockdown. The failure has consequences beyond the immediate scene.

Outcome: The failed check created new tension and narrative branches. The GM used the failure to introduce a complication (guard approaching) and long-term consequence (clock advancement). The story is now more dynamic and unpredictable. The players must improvise solutions, and the GM improvises responses.

Example 3: Players Ignore the Hook

Setup: The GM prepared a session where a mysterious stranger offers the party a job to retrieve an artifact from dangerous ruins. The GM expects players to accept and go to the ruins.

Player Action: The party listens to the stranger’s offer and declines. They are more interested in investigating recent disappearances in town, a side plot the GM mentioned in passing.

Improvisation in Action:

  1. Do Not Railroad: The GM resists the urge to force the players to accept the job. Instead: “Alright, you decline. The stranger looks disappointed but leaves. You turn your attention to the disappearances. What is your first move?”

  2. Pivot Prep: The GM has prepared ruins content (maps, encounters, clues). Instead of abandoning it, they pivot. The disappearances lead to an underground hideout. The ruins map becomes the hideout. The encounters fit thematically (cultists instead of ruin guardians). The artifact the stranger wanted is connected to the disappearances.

  3. Reframe Stakes: The GM makes the main plot come to the players. As they investigate disappearances, they discover the kidnapped victims are being used in a ritual requiring the artifact from the ruins. The players’ investigation and the stranger’s job are now connected. The stakes are higher: people are dying.

  4. Ask Questions: The GM asks: “You are investigating the disappearances. Where do you start? The families of the missing, the city guard, the locations they were last seen, or somewhere else?” This clarifies player intent and helps the GM focus improvisation.

  5. Deploy Modular Content: The GM deploys a modular NPC from their bank: a grieving mother who provides leads. They also deploy a modular encounter: discovering a hidden basement with evidence of the ritual.

Outcome: The GM improvised a new session structure by pivoting prep, reframing stakes, and deploying modular content. The players felt heard and empowered: their choice to investigate disappearances mattered. The GM connected the ignored hook to the players’ chosen path, creating a richer narrative. The session succeeded despite the unexpected direction.
