Mysteries and Intrigue

Table of Contents

  • Mystery Design Fundamentals
  • Clue Types and Information Flow
  • Investigation Structures
  • Revelation Pacing
  • Fail-Forward Mystery Design
  • Intrigue and Social Mysteries
  • Worked Example: Node-Based Murder Mystery

Mystery Design Fundamentals

Mystery campaigns are about revelation and discovery. Players gather information, connect dots, and uncover truths. Designing mysteries for TTRPGs differs from novels: players must feel clever, and the path to revelation must be robust enough to survive improvisation and failed checks.

Two principles underpin good mystery design:

  1. The Three-Clue Rule: Every critical revelation needs at least three independent paths leading to it. If players miss one clue, two others remain. This prevents single points of failure.
  2. Node-Based Design: Structure mysteries as webs of interconnected information nodes (locations, NPCs, clues), not linear sequences. Players can approach nodes in any order, discovering different clues depending on their path.

The Three-Clue Rule

The Three-Clue Rule is a safety net. Critical information must have multiple paths to discovery. If learning “the mayor is the killer” requires only one clue (a journal in the mayor’s office), and players never search the office, the mystery stalls.

How to Apply:

  1. Identify Critical Revelations: What must players learn to progress? (Example: The killer’s identity, the artifact’s location, the cult’s goal.)
  2. Create Three Paths: For each revelation, create three independent clues or sources. Spread them across different locations, NPCs, or investigation types.
  3. Test Independence: Ensure each clue is findable independently. If Clue A requires finding Clue B first, they are not independent.

Example: Critical revelation: The mayor is the killer.

  • Clue 1: A witness saw someone matching the mayor’s description near the crime scene.
  • Clue 2: The murder weapon (a rare dagger) matches one missing from the mayor’s collection.
  • Clue 3: A coded letter found at the victim’s home, when deciphered, references “the town’s leader.”

Players can find any one, two, or all three clues. Each points to the mayor. Missing one does not derail the mystery.

Node-Based Mystery Structure

Node-based mysteries structure information as interconnected nodes. Each node (location, NPC, event) contains clues or leads to other nodes. Players explore nodes in any order, discovering information non-linearly.

Nodes Include:

  • Locations: Crime scenes, suspects’ homes, public places, hidden sites.
  • NPCs: Witnesses, suspects, informants, investigators.
  • Events: Auctions, meetings, confrontations, discoveries.

Connections: Nodes link to other nodes via leads. A clue at Node A points to Node B. An NPC at Node C provides access to Node D. Players follow leads, building their understanding.

Example Structure:

  • Node A (Crime Scene): Contains clues pointing to Node B (suspect’s home) and Node D (witness).
  • Node B (Suspect’s Home): Contains clues pointing to Node C (hidden meeting place) and confirms suspicions from Node A.
  • Node C (Hidden Meeting Place): Contains clues revealing the conspiracy and pointing to Node E (mastermind).
  • Node D (Witness): Provides leads to Node A (crime scene details) and Node E (saw the mastermind).
  • Node E (Mastermind): Final node where confrontation or revelation occurs.

Players can explore nodes in many orders: A → B → C, A → D → E, D → A → B → C → E. The web structure supports exploration and prevents railroading.

GM usage:

  • Design mysteries with three independent clues for each critical revelation
  • Use node-based structures to allow non-linear investigation
  • Ensure nodes connect via leads; no isolated or unreachable nodes
  • Test your mystery: Can players reach critical revelations via multiple paths?
  • Embrace player-driven exploration; let them choose which leads to follow

Clue Types and Information Flow

Not all clues are equal. Different clue types serve different purposes. Understanding clue types helps you pace information flow and create satisfying revelations.

Core Clues

Core clues are essential to solving the mystery. They point directly to critical revelations. Core clues must be discoverable through multiple paths (Three-Clue Rule).

Example Core Clues:

  • The murder weapon found at the scene (points to the killer’s identity).
  • A coded message revealing the conspiracy’s goal.
  • A witness who saw the culprit’s face.

Delivery: Core clues should be easy to find if players investigate relevant nodes. Do not hide core clues behind difficult checks or obscure reasoning. Use fail-forward design (see below): even failed checks reveal core clues, possibly with complications.

Supporting Clues

Supporting clues add detail, confirm theories, or provide context. They are not essential but enrich the mystery and make revelations more satisfying.

Example Supporting Clues:

  • Background on the victim (motive, relationships, secrets).
  • Details about the murder method (expertise required, timing).
  • Evidence of the killer’s movements (footprints, receipts, alibis).

Delivery: Supporting clues reward thorough investigation. They can be gated behind successful checks or clever player questions. Missing supporting clues does not derail the mystery but reduces confidence in conclusions.

Red Herrings

Red herrings are false leads that misdirect players temporarily. Used carefully, they create suspense and make revelations more satisfying. Overused, they frustrate players.

Good Red Herrings:

  • Have plausible explanations when revealed as false.
  • Lead somewhere interesting even if not to the solution (a red herring suspect has their own secrets).
  • Do not waste excessive player time; reveal them as false relatively quickly.

Example: A suspect has no alibi and acts suspiciously. Players investigate and discover they were smuggling contraband (illegal but unrelated to the murder). The red herring is resolved, and players refocus on the real killer.

Bad Red Herrings:

  • Have no explanation or payoff (arbitrary misdirection).
  • Waste excessive time with no narrative benefit.
  • Make players feel cheated when revealed.

Usage: Use red herrings sparingly. One or two per mystery adds complexity. Too many creates frustration.

Leads

Leads are clues that point to other nodes. They guide investigation by suggesting where to go next.

Example Leads:

  • A business card found on the victim (points to an NPC node).
  • A reference to a location (points to a location node).
  • An NPC mentions another NPC (points to an interview node).

Delivery: Leads are abundant. Provide multiple leads at each node so players have choices. Let players decide which leads to follow first.

GM usage:

  • Distribute core clues across multiple paths; ensure they are discoverable
  • Use supporting clues to reward thorough investigation and add depth
  • Use red herrings sparingly; ensure they lead somewhere interesting
  • Provide multiple leads at each node to guide player choices
  • Balance information flow: reveal enough to maintain momentum without overwhelming players

Investigation Structures

Investigations can be structured as linear, node-based, or open. Each structure suits different campaign styles and player preferences.

Linear Investigations

Linear investigations follow a sequence: Clue A leads to Clue B leads to Clue C leads to the solution. Linear structure is simple and clear but risks railroading and single points of failure.

When to Use:

  • One-shot mysteries with limited time.
  • Introductory mysteries for new players.
  • Tight, authored narratives with specific story beats.

How to Mitigate Railroading:

  • Apply the Three-Clue Rule to each step.
  • Use fail-forward design to prevent stalls.
  • Allow player creativity to skip or reorder steps.

Example: The party investigates a theft. Clue A (witness description) leads to Clue B (suspect’s hideout) leads to Clue C (stolen goods) leads to the culprit’s arrest. If players miss Clue A, alternate paths (investigating the crime scene, asking informants) provide Clues B or C directly.

Node-Based Investigations

Node-based investigations (described above) allow non-linear exploration. Players follow leads in any order, discovering clues dynamically. This structure is flexible and robust.

When to Use:

  • Campaign mysteries spanning multiple sessions.
  • Mysteries emphasizing player-driven investigation.
  • Complex conspiracies or multi-layered plots.

How to Manage:

  • Map nodes and connections before play; use visual diagrams.
  • Provide multiple leads at each node; let players choose direction.
  • Track which nodes players have explored and which clues they have found.

Example: See the worked example (Node-Based Murder Mystery) below.

Open Investigations

Open investigations are unstructured. Players investigate freely without predefined nodes or leads. The GM improvises clues and leads based on player actions.

When to Use:

  • Emergent, player-driven campaigns.
  • Experienced players comfortable with open-ended exploration.
  • Mysteries where the GM improvises solutions based on player theories.

How to Manage:

  • Maintain flexible prep: NPC and location banks, modular clues.
  • Listen to player theories; incorporate plausible ones into the solution.
  • Use the Three-Clue Rule even when improvising; ensure revelations have support.

Example: Players investigate a haunting with no predefined solution. The GM listens to player theories (ghost of a murder victim, residual magic, cursed artifact) and chooses the most interesting one, then improvises clues supporting it.

GM usage:

  • Choose investigation structures based on campaign style and player preferences
  • Use linear structures for simplicity and tight narratives
  • Use node-based structures for flexibility and player-driven investigation
  • Use open structures for improvisation and emergent narrative
  • Apply the Three-Clue Rule and fail-forward design across all structures

Revelation Pacing

Revelation pacing determines when and how players learn answers. Good pacing maintains tension and rewards investigation without withholding information arbitrarily.

When to Reveal

Early Revelations: Reveal minor mysteries or partial answers early to build momentum and confidence. Example: Session One reveals the victim’s identity and basic motive; Session Three reveals the killer’s identity; Session Five reveals the larger conspiracy.

Mid-Mystery Revelations: Reveal critical information in the middle to shift focus or escalate stakes. Example: Players discover the killer’s identity mid-arc but learn they are a pawn in a larger conspiracy. The mystery evolves from “whodunit” to “who is behind this?”

Late Revelations: Reserve final revelations for climactic moments. The ultimate truth, the mastermind’s identity, or the conspiracy’s goal are revealed in the final confrontation or session.

Layered Mysteries: Design mysteries with layers. Each revelation answers one question and raises new ones. Example: Layer One: Who killed the merchant? Layer Two: Why? Layer Three: Who ordered it?

How to Deliver Revelations

Revelations should feel earned, not arbitrary. Tie revelations to player actions and investigation efforts.

Methods:

  • Discovery: Players find physical evidence (documents, artifacts, crime scenes).
  • Interview: NPCs provide information through conversation or interrogation.
  • Deduction: Players connect clues and deduce conclusions. The GM confirms or complicates their theory.
  • Event: Revelations occur during dramatic events (confrontations, chases, ambushes). Example: The mastermind reveals themselves during a climactic confrontation.

Avoid:

  • Unprompted Exposition: NPCs spontaneously reveal everything without player effort.
  • Arbitrary Gating: Withholding information without fictional justification.

GM usage:

  • Pace revelations across the arc; early reveals build momentum, late reveals deliver climax
  • Design layered mysteries where each revelation raises new questions
  • Tie revelations to player actions; make them feel earned
  • Use varied delivery methods (discovery, interview, deduction, events)
  • Avoid arbitrary withholding or unprompted exposition

Fail-Forward Mystery Design

Fail-forward design ensures failed checks do not stall mysteries. Players always learn something; failures add complications, not dead ends.

Outcome Tiers in Mysteries

Use outcome tiers (see GM Guide Chapter 6) to structure clue delivery:

  • Full Success: Players learn the clue clearly and without complication. They may learn supporting clues as well.
  • Partial Success: Players learn the clue but with cost or complication. Examples: It takes longer (pressure clock advances), they attract attention (new complication), or the clue is incomplete (requires follow-up).
  • Failure: Players learn the clue but with significant complication or alternative information. Example: They learn the clue but alert enemies, or they learn misleading information requiring correction later.

Example:

Players investigate a crime scene, searching for clues about the killer.

  • Full Success: They find the murder weapon (core clue) and notice distinctive boot prints (supporting clue). Both point clearly to the killer.
  • Partial Success: They find the murder weapon but disturb the scene, alerting the local guard. Now they must deal with the guard’s questions (complication).
  • Failure: They miss the murder weapon but notice boot prints leading away from the scene. Following them leads to a red herring (innocent bystander) but also reveals a secondary clue (witness saw someone fleeing).

In all cases, players learn something useful. Failures create complications but do not halt progress.

Never Gate Core Clues Behind Single Checks

Do not require a single successful check to find a core clue. Use the Three-Clue Rule to provide alternate paths. If players fail to find Clue A, Clues B and C remain.

Example: The murder weapon (core clue) can be found by:

  1. Searching the crime scene (Awareness check).
  2. Interviewing a witness who saw it discarded (Influence check).
  3. Checking with local blacksmiths who might recognize it (Investigation check).

If players fail the crime scene search, they can still learn about the weapon through interviews or investigation.

Complications Over Blockages

When players fail, add complications rather than blocking progress.

Complications:

  • Time pressure (clocks advance).
  • Alerting enemies (new threats appear).
  • Social costs (reputation damage, suspicion).
  • Incomplete information (requires follow-up or cross-referencing).

Example: Players fail to pick a lock on a suspect’s office. Instead of “You do not get in,” the GM says: “You get in, but it takes longer than expected. The suspect returns home early and is climbing the stairs. You have moments to search. What do you do?”

GM usage:

  • Use outcome tiers to deliver clues at all success levels
  • Never gate core clues behind single checks; provide alternate paths
  • Frame failures as complications, not blockages
  • Advance clocks or introduce threats when players fail
  • Ensure players always learn something, even on failures

Intrigue and Social Mysteries

Social mysteries focus on intrigue, secrets, leverage, and trust. Players navigate webs of relationships, uncover hidden motives, and manipulate social dynamics.

Leverage and Secrets

Leverage is information or resources used to influence NPCs. In social mysteries, leverage is currency. Discovering secrets grants leverage over those who hold them.

Example: Players discover a noble is embezzling funds. This secret is leverage: they can blackmail the noble, trade the information to rivals, or use it to force cooperation.

Designing Leverage:

  1. Give NPCs Secrets: Every major NPC has something to hide (crime, scandal, forbidden relationship, hidden goal).
  2. Make Secrets Discoverable: Secrets are nodes in the investigation. Clues point to them.
  3. Let Players Use Leverage: When players gain leverage, allow them to spend it. Leverage creates new options and choices.

Social Clocks

Social clocks track relationship dynamics, reputation, or intrigue progress.

Examples:

  • Earn NPC’s Trust (progress clock): Advances when players help the NPC or share valuable information.
  • Suspicion Grows (pressure clock): Advances when players act suspiciously or are caught in lies.
  • Scandal Spreads (pressure clock): Advances when secrets are revealed publicly or rumors spread.

Social clocks make abstract social dynamics tangible and create stakes.

Intrigue Structures

Structure social mysteries similarly to investigation mysteries: node-based design with NPCs as nodes.

NPC Nodes:

  • Each major NPC is a node containing secrets, goals, and connections.
  • Players interact with NPCs to learn secrets or gain leverage.
  • NPCs provide leads to other NPCs (alliances, rivalries, past relationships).

Example Intrigue Web:

  • NPC A (Noble): Secretly funding rebels. Allied with NPC C. Rivals with NPC B.
  • NPC B (Merchant): Smuggling contraband. Connected to NPC D. Seeking leverage over NPC A.
  • NPC C (Rebel Leader): Coordinating with NPC A. Distrusts NPC D.
  • NPC D (City Guard): Investigating NPC B. Has secrets NPC B could exploit.

Players navigate this web, gathering leverage and choosing alliances. Each interaction reveals new connections and secrets.

GM usage:

  • Give major NPCs secrets; make secrets discoverable and valuable
  • Use leverage as currency in social mysteries
  • Track social dynamics with clocks (trust, suspicion, reputation, scandal)
  • Structure intrigue as webs of NPC nodes connected by relationships
  • Let players use leverage to create new options and reshape social dynamics

Worked Example: Node-Based Murder Mystery

Mystery Premise: A wealthy merchant, Aldric Marrow, is found dead in his study. The cause: poisoning. The suspect pool includes his business partner, his estranged daughter, a rival merchant, and his personal secretary. Players must uncover the killer and their motive.

Critical Revelations

Revelation 1: Aldric was poisoned with rare venom (not common poison).

Revelation 2: The killer had access to Aldric’s private study.

Revelation 3: The killer is the secretary, Lenara. Motive: Aldric discovered she was embezzling funds and threatened to report her.

Three-Clue Rule Application

Revelation 1 (Rare Venom):

  • Clue A: The coroner’s report identifies the venom type.
  • Clue B: An alchemist in town recently sold this venom; records show the buyer.
  • Clue C: A witness saw someone matching the killer’s description purchasing venom.

Revelation 2 (Access to Study):

  • Clue A: The study door was locked; only a few people have keys.
  • Clue B: The window was locked from inside; entry was via the door.
  • Clue C: A servant mentions who had access to the study keys.

Revelation 3 (Lenara is the Killer):

  • Clue A: Financial records show embezzlement; Lenara managed the books.
  • Clue B: A threatening letter from Aldric to Lenara found in her quarters.
  • Clue C: A witness saw Lenara entering the study shortly before Aldric’s death.

Node Map

Node 1: Crime Scene (Aldric’s Study)

  • Description: Aldric slumped at his desk, a wine glass nearby. The room is locked from inside.
  • Core Clues: Wine glass contains venom residue (Revelation 1, Clue A). Door and window locked from inside (Revelation 2, Clue B).
  • Supporting Clues: Aldric’s last journal entry mentions “dealing with a betrayal.”
  • Leads: Investigate the coroner (Node 2), talk to the household staff (Node 3), search Lenara’s quarters (Node 5).

Node 2: Coroner’s Report

  • Description: The city coroner examined the body.
  • Core Clues: Coroner identifies the venom as rare and expensive (Revelation 1, Clue A).
  • Leads: Check local alchemists who sell rare poisons (Node 4).

Node 3: Household Staff Interviews

  • Description: Servants, guards, and household members.
  • Core Clues: A servant mentions Lenara, Aldric’s business partner, and his daughter all had keys to the study (Revelation 2, Clue C).
  • Supporting Clues: A maid mentions Lenara seemed nervous recently. A guard saw Lenara entering the study shortly before Aldric’s body was discovered (Revelation 3, Clue C).
  • Leads: Investigate Lenara (Node 5), talk to the business partner (Node 6), talk to the daughter (Node 7).

Node 4: Alchemist Shop

  • Description: A shady alchemist who sells rare ingredients and poisons.
  • Core Clues: Records show a customer matching Lenara’s description bought the venom a week ago (Revelation 1, Clue B).
  • Supporting Clues: The alchemist remembers the buyer seemed anxious and paid in cash.
  • Leads: Investigate Lenara (Node 5).

Node 5: Lenara’s Quarters

  • Description: Lenara’s private rooms in Aldric’s estate.
  • Core Clues: Financial ledgers hidden in her room show embezzlement (Revelation 3, Clue A). A threatening letter from Aldric: “I know what you have done. We will discuss this tomorrow. If you do not confess, I will report you” (Revelation 3, Clue B).
  • Supporting Clues: A vial (empty) matching the venom description.
  • Leads: Confront Lenara (Node 8).

Node 6: Business Partner Interview

  • Description: Aldric’s business partner, a rival merchant named Torian.
  • Red Herring: Torian has no alibi and benefits from Aldric’s death (inherits business share). Investigation reveals Torian was meeting with investors at the time of death (alibi confirmed). Torian is not the killer but becomes a future ally or complication.
  • Supporting Clues: Torian mentions Aldric was worried about financial irregularities recently.
  • Leads: Check financial records (leads to Node 5).

Node 7: Daughter Interview

  • Description: Aldric’s estranged daughter, Mira. She and Aldric argued frequently.
  • Red Herring: Mira had motive (inheritance) and access (has a key). Investigation reveals she was out of town during the murder (confirmed by travel records and witnesses).
  • Supporting Clues: Mira mentions Lenara was “too loyal, almost obsessive” about Aldric’s finances.
  • Leads: Investigate Lenara (Node 5).

Node 8: Confrontation with Lenara

  • Description: Players confront Lenara with evidence.
  • Outcome: Lenara confesses or attempts to flee/fight, depending on evidence presented and player approach. If overwhelming evidence, she confesses: she embezzled funds to pay personal debts, Aldric discovered it, and she panicked and poisoned him.
  • Resolution: Players decide Lenara’s fate: turn her over to authorities, blackmail her for information, or let her escape in exchange for leverage.

Investigation Flow Examples

Path 1: Crime Scene → Coroner → Alchemist → Lenara’s Quarters → Confrontation

Players investigate the crime scene, learn about the venom, trace it to the alchemist, discover Lenara bought it, search her quarters, find evidence of embezzlement and the threatening letter, confront her. Revelations discovered in order.

Path 2: Staff Interviews → Lenara’s Quarters → Coroner → Confrontation

Players talk to staff first, learn Lenara acted suspiciously, search her quarters, find the ledgers and letter, confirm venom via coroner, confront her. Revelations discovered out of order but lead to same conclusion.

Path 3: Business Partner → Daughter → Staff → Lenara’s Quarters → Confrontation

Players investigate red herrings first (Torian and Mira), rule them out, talk to staff, learn about Lenara, investigate her, confront her. Red herrings resolved before discovering the real killer.

All paths lead to Lenara. The Three-Clue Rule ensures players find enough evidence regardless of path. Node structure supports non-linear exploration.

GM Prep and Play

Prep:

  1. Map nodes visually (draw diagram showing connections).
  2. Write clues for each node on index cards or digital notes.
  3. Track which nodes players explore and which clues they discover.
  4. Prepare Lenara’s stats and tactics for potential confrontation or escape.

During Play:

  1. Let players choose investigation paths; do not railroad.
  2. Deliver clues based on player actions and checks (use outcome tiers).
  3. Advance a “Suspicion Grows” clock if players are careless or Lenara realizes she is being investigated. When the clock fills, Lenara flees or prepares defenses.
  4. Use red herrings (Torian, Mira) to add complexity but resolve them relatively quickly.
  5. Build toward the confrontation; make it climactic and allow player choice in resolution.

Resolution:

Players confront Lenara with evidence. Possible outcomes:

  • Lenara confesses and is arrested. Players are rewarded by Torian (new business partner) or Mira (grateful daughter).
  • Lenara flees; players pursue (chase scene or future complication).
  • Lenara attacks; players subdue her (combat encounter).
  • Players strike a deal with Lenara for information or future leverage (intrigue path).

Cross-Links

  • Scene structure and pacing: GM Guide Chapter 5: Scene Structure

Social Conflict and Negotiation

Table of Contents

  • Social Conflict Fundamentals
  • Leverage and Pressure
  • Negotiation Structures
  • Social Combat
  • Reputation and Influence Systems
  • Social Tags and Conditions
  • Worked Examples
    • Example 1: Simple Negotiation
    • Example 2: Social Combat
    • Example 3: Reputation Challenge

Social Conflict Fundamentals

Social conflict is narrative conflict using words, influence, and leverage instead of violence. Players negotiate deals, persuade adversaries, manipulate rivals, or build reputations. Social conflict creates drama and stakes without combat.

Three elements define social conflict:

  1. Goals: What each side wants. Clear goals create stakes and shape negotiation.
  2. Leverage: What each side can offer or threaten. Leverage is currency in social conflict.
  3. Costs: What each side is willing to pay. Costs create tension and hard choices.

Social conflict is not about forcing outcomes. NPCs have agency. Players cannot simply roll high and make NPCs do anything. Instead, players use leverage, manage costs, and negotiate outcomes. Success means achieving goals through influence, not domination.

Goals and Stakes

Every social conflict begins with goals. Players want something; NPCs want something. Stakes are what changes based on outcomes.

Player Goals (Examples):

  • Gain information (NPC reveals a secret).
  • Secure cooperation (NPC agrees to help).
  • Avoid conflict (NPC does not attack or report them).
  • Gain resources (NPC provides goods, money, or access).

NPC Goals (Examples):

  • Protect secrets (refuse to reveal information).
  • Demand payment or favors (cooperation has a price).
  • Maintain authority (will not be intimidated or manipulated).
  • Advance personal agenda (uses interaction to further own goals).

Stakes: What happens if players succeed or fail? If players persuade the merchant, they gain critical supplies. If they fail, they must find another source or pay exorbitant prices. Stakes make social conflict matter.

GM usage:

  • Establish clear goals for players and NPCs at the start of social conflict
  • Define stakes: what changes based on outcomes
  • Ensure NPCs have goals and agency; they are not passive obstacles
  • Frame social conflict as negotiation, not domination
  • Use leverage and costs to create tension and hard choices

Leverage and Pressure

Leverage is anything that influences an NPC: information, resources, threats, promises, reputation, relationships. Leverage gives players (and NPCs) tools to achieve goals.

Types of Leverage

Positive Leverage (Incentives):

  • Offers: Money, goods, services, alliances.
  • Promises: Future rewards, favors owed, oaths of loyalty.
  • Shared Goals: Appeals to mutual interests or common enemies.
  • Reputation: Fame, heroism, or respected status that grants credibility.

Negative Leverage (Threats):

  • Blackmail: Secrets or scandals that can be revealed.
  • Threats: Harm, exposure, or loss (physical, social, economic).
  • Withdrawal: Removing support, abandoning alliances, or leaving the NPC vulnerable.

Neutral Leverage (Information and Access):

  • Information: Secrets, clues, or knowledge the NPC wants.
  • Access: Introductions to important people, entry to restricted places, or resources only players can provide.

Using Leverage

Players declare leverage when negotiating. “I offer 100 gold” (positive leverage). “I know your secret; cooperate or I reveal it” (negative leverage). “I can introduce you to the Duke” (access leverage).

GMs determine if leverage is effective based on NPC goals, personality, and fictional positioning.

Effective Leverage:

  • Aligns with NPC goals or fears.
  • Is credible (players can actually deliver or enact).
  • Has weight (offers something significant or threatens something important).

Ineffective Leverage:

  • Contradicts NPC goals or personality.
  • Is not credible (empty threats, impossible promises).
  • Has no weight (trivial offers or threats).

Example: Players try to bribe a loyal knight to betray their lord. The knight values honor and loyalty above gold. The bribe is ineffective leverage. However, if players reveal the lord is corrupt (information leverage aligned with the knight’s values), the knight reconsiders.

Pressure

Pressure is accumulated leverage or urgency that forces decisions. Pressure can be mechanical (clocks, conditions) or narrative (time limits, escalating stakes).

Pressure Clocks: Track rising tension in social conflict. Example: A “Suspicion Grows” clock advances as players press the NPC. When it fills, the NPC refuses further negotiation or calls for guards.

Pressure Conditions: Social conditions like Cornered, Defensive, or Enraged represent NPC states under pressure. Conditions affect NPC responses: a Cornered NPC might lash out or make desperate concessions.

GM usage:

  • Identify what leverage players have (information, resources, reputation, threats)
  • Determine leverage effectiveness based on NPC goals and personality
  • Use leverage to create offers, threats, or incentives
  • Track pressure with clocks or conditions to represent rising tension
  • Let NPCs use leverage against players; social conflict is two-sided

Negotiation Structures

Negotiations range from simple (single check) to complex (multi-stage with leverage, costs, and outcomes). Choose structure based on importance and table interest.

Simple Negotiations

Simple negotiations resolve in one or two checks. Use for minor interactions or when time is limited.

Procedure:

  1. Establish Goals: What does each side want?
  2. Declare Leverage: What is the player offering, threatening, or appealing to?
  3. Check: Player rolls relevant skill (Influence, Deception, Insight, etc.).
  4. Outcome:
    • Full Success: Player achieves goal; NPC cooperates fully.
    • Partial Success: Player achieves goal with cost (favor owed, information revealed, time lost, partial cooperation).
    • Failure: Player does not achieve goal; NPC refuses or demands something the player cannot pay.

Example: Players ask a bartender for information about a suspect. The bartender wants money (goal: profit). Players offer 10 gold (leverage). Roll Influence. Success: The bartender provides detailed information. Partial: The bartender provides vague information but hints they know more if players return with more gold. Failure: The bartender refuses; they are too afraid to talk (revealing their fear of the suspect).

Complex Negotiations

Complex negotiations involve multiple stages, leverage exchanges, and escalating stakes. Use for critical interactions or high-stakes social conflict.

Procedure:

  1. Establish Goals and Leverage: Both sides state goals and available leverage.
  2. Negotiation Phases: The negotiation proceeds in phases. Each phase involves offers, counter-offers, checks, and leverage exchanges.
  3. Leverage Spending: Players and NPCs spend leverage to gain advantage or apply pressure. Leverage is finite; once spent, it is gone.
  4. Outcome Tiers: Each check advances negotiation or introduces complications.
  5. Resolution: Negotiation resolves when one side achieves goals, both sides compromise, or negotiation breaks down.

Example Structure (Three Phases):

Phase 1: Opening Offers

  • Players and NPC state initial positions.
  • Players declare leverage (offer, threat, appeal).
  • Roll Influence or relevant skill.
  • Outcome determines NPC response: interested, skeptical, or hostile.

Phase 2: Counter-Offers and Pressure

  • NPC makes counter-offer or demands concession.
  • Players adjust leverage, spend new resources, or apply pressure.
  • Roll again.
  • Outcome: progress toward agreement or escalation.

Phase 3: Closing

  • Final offers and commitments.
  • Roll to seal the deal.
  • Outcome: agreement reached, compromise, or breakdown.

Tracking: Use a clock (e.g., “Negotiation Progress” 6 segments) to track progress. Successful checks advance the clock. When it fills, agreement is reached. Use pressure clocks (e.g., “Patience Wears Thin” 4 segments) to track NPC frustration. If it fills before agreement, negotiation fails.

Multi-Party Negotiations

Multi-party negotiations involve three or more factions with conflicting goals. Players must manage competing interests and build coalitions.

Procedure:

  1. Map Interests: Identify each faction’s goals and leverage.
  2. Choose Approach: Players negotiate with factions individually, build coalitions, or mediate conflicts.
  3. Track Faction Clocks: Each faction has a “Support” or “Opposition” clock. Players advance support clocks with successful negotiations or by addressing faction concerns.
  4. Resolution: Negotiation succeeds when enough factions support the player’s goal (majority or unanimous, depending on stakes).

Example: Players negotiate a peace treaty between three factions: the Crown, the Rebels, and the Merchants. Each faction has demands (Crown wants order restored, Rebels want reforms, Merchants want trade protections). Players must satisfy enough demands to gain majority support.

GM usage:

  • Use simple negotiations for minor interactions or time efficiency
  • Use complex negotiations for critical, high-stakes interactions
  • Track progress and pressure with clocks
  • Use multi-party negotiations for political or factional conflicts
  • Let negotiations fail if leverage is insufficient or NPCs are intractable; failure creates new story directions

Social Combat

Social combat is structured, turn-based social conflict. It uses mechanics similar to physical combat but focuses on influence, arguments, and psychological pressure. Use social combat for intense, dramatic confrontations where every word matters.

When to Use Social Combat

Use social combat when:

  • The confrontation is climactic (courtroom drama, public debate, critical negotiation).
  • Both sides are evenly matched in influence or leverage.
  • Failure has serious consequences (reputation destroyed, war declared, alliance broken).
  • The table enjoys structured, tactical social interaction.

Do not use social combat for casual conversations, minor negotiations, or when quick resolution is needed.

Social Combat Structure

Social combat follows a turn-based structure.

Setup:

  1. Establish Stakes: What happens if players win? If they lose?
  2. Determine Participants: Who is involved? (Players, primary NPC, audience, allies.)
  3. Set Initiative: Roll initiative (use Influence, Insight, or Awareness).
  4. Define Social Hit Points: Each side has a “Composure” or “Resolve” pool representing their psychological resilience. This can be abstract (e.g., 3 hits to win) or tied to stats (e.g., Resolve = Influence skill + Social tags).

Turns:

On each participant’s turn, they take a social action:

  • Persuade: Roll Influence to sway opinion or gain advantage. Success reduces opponent’s Resolve.
  • Deceive: Roll Deception to mislead or create doubt. Success creates openings or disrupts opponent’s arguments.
  • Intimidate: Roll Presence or relevant skill to threaten or pressure. Success reduces Resolve or imposes social conditions (Rattled, Defensive).
  • Insight: Roll Insight to read opponent, predict their arguments, or uncover weaknesses. Success grants advantage on next action.
  • Defend: No roll; gain temporary Resolve or remove a social condition.

Leverage in Social Combat: Spend leverage to gain advantage on rolls (bonus die, reroll) or impose disadvantage on opponents.

Social Conditions: Social combat can impose conditions:

  • Rattled: Disadvantage on Influence checks; credibility shaken.
  • Defensive: Must succeed on Insight check to take offensive actions; on the back foot.
  • Exposed: Opponent has advantage against you; weakness revealed.
  • Humiliated: Lose remaining Resolve at the end of your next turn unless you recover; public credibility destroyed.

Resolution:

  • Victory: Reduce opponent’s Resolve to zero. You achieve your goal (persuade the crowd, discredit the rival, gain the judge’s favor).
  • Defeat: Your Resolve is reduced to zero. Opponent achieves their goal (you are discredited, judged guilty, or forced to concede).
  • Compromise: Both sides retain some Resolve but negotiate a middle ground. Partial victory for both.

Example Social Combat:

Players (acting as defense attorneys) face a prosecutor in a courtroom drama. Stakes: The defendant’s freedom. Each side has Resolve 5. Players roll initiative, then take turns making arguments (Persuade checks), presenting evidence (Insight to find contradictions), or cross-examining witnesses (Deceive to create doubt). The prosecutor counters with their own arguments and evidence. The first side to reduce the other’s Resolve to zero wins the case.

Audiences and Observers

Social combat often has audiences (crowds, judges, councils). Audiences affect outcomes.

Audience Influence:

  • Successful social actions may sway the audience. Track audience favor with a clock or scale (e.g., “Crowd Favor” 6 segments; advance toward players or opponent based on performance).
  • When audience favor is won, the audience provides bonuses (advantage on rolls) or penalties to the opposition.
  • In some cases, winning audience favor is the victory condition, not reducing opponent’s Resolve.

Example: Players debate a rival in a public forum. The goal is to sway the crowd. Each successful Persuade check advances the “Crowd Favor” clock toward the players. If the clock fills, the crowd supports the players and the rival is discredited.

GM usage:

  • Use social combat for climactic, high-stakes confrontations
  • Define Resolve pools and social actions clearly
  • Use leverage to grant advantages or impose disadvantages
  • Impose social conditions to create dynamic, tactical play
  • Include audiences to add complexity and stakes
  • Ensure failure and victory have narrative consequences

Reputation and Influence Systems

Reputation and influence track characters’ social standing and ability to affect the world. Reputation systems add depth to social campaigns.

Reputation Tracks

Reputation represents how factions, groups, or regions view the party. Reputations can be positive (Respected, Renowned) or negative (Notorious, Distrusted).

Tracking Reputation:

  • Use clocks or scales for each major faction or region.
  • Actions advance or reduce reputation clocks.
  • Reputation levels grant benefits (access, allies, discounts) or costs (hostility, suspicion, higher prices).

Example Reputation Levels (with a faction):

  • Unknown (0 segments): The faction does not know you.
  • Aware (2 segments): The faction knows of you but has no strong opinion.
  • Respected (4 segments): The faction trusts you; you gain minor favors and cooperation.
  • Renowned (6 segments): The faction reveres you; you gain significant support, resources, or political weight.

Negative reputation works similarly but imposes penalties.

Gaining Reputation:

  • Complete missions for the faction.
  • Publicly perform heroic deeds aligned with faction values.
  • Defeat faction enemies or threats.

Losing Reputation:

  • Betray the faction or break agreements.
  • Commit acts opposed to faction values.
  • Fail critical missions or cause harm to faction interests.

Influence Systems

Influence measures a character’s ability to affect social situations. Influence can be tied to reputation, resources, or personal traits.

Influence as Resource:

  • Characters have an “Influence” pool (e.g., 3-6 points).
  • Spend Influence to gain social advantages: call in favors, sway NPCs without checks, access restricted areas, or reduce costs in negotiations.
  • Influence regenerates slowly (e.g., 1 point per session or after significant social victories).

Influence as Clout:

  • Characters with high Influence can affect factions, politics, or public opinion.
  • Influence checks determine if characters can enact large-scale changes (e.g., “Can I convince the council to declare war?” Roll Influence; success means you sway enough councilors).

Combining Reputation and Influence:

  • High Reputation grants bonus Influence points or advantage on Influence checks.
  • Low Reputation imposes penalties or blocks Influence use.

Example: A character with “Renowned” reputation in the Merchant’s Guild has 5 Influence points. They spend 2 Influence to secure a meeting with the Guild Master without checks. They still have 3 Influence points for future use.

Social Tags and Reputation

Tags tied to reputation or influence create mechanical benefits.

Examples:

  • Renowned Hero: Gain advantage on Influence checks with factions that value heroism.
  • Notorious Criminal: Disadvantage on social checks with lawful factions but advantage with criminal underworld.
  • Trusted by the Crown: Access royal resources or audiences without checks.
  • Marked for Death: Certain factions or NPCs attack on sight or refuse interaction.

Tags make reputation tangible and create fictional permissions.

GM usage:

  • Use reputation tracks to reflect party standing with factions or regions
  • Advance or reduce reputation based on player actions
  • Grant benefits or impose penalties based on reputation levels
  • Use Influence as a spendable resource for social advantages
  • Create social tags tied to reputation or influence
  • Let reputation affect access, costs, and NPC attitudes

Social Tags and Conditions

Social tags and conditions create narrative and mechanical stakes in social conflict.

Social Tags

Social tags represent social states, advantages, or vulnerabilities.

Positive Social Tags:

  • Well-Liked: Advantage on Influence checks in this community.
  • Credible Witness: Others believe your testimony without checks.
  • Connected: You know people in high places; gain access or information easily.

Negative Social Tags:

  • Mistrusted: Disadvantage on social checks; others doubt your words.
  • Under Scrutiny: Authorities or rivals are watching; risky actions attract attention.
  • Compromised: Someone has leverage over you; they can compel actions.

Using Social Tags:

  • Players create tags through roleplay or successful checks. “I spend time building relationships” (create Well-Liked tag).
  • GMs impose negative tags based on failures or complications. “Your deception is exposed” (gain Mistrusted tag).
  • Tags can be invoked (for advantage) or compelled (for complications).

Social Conditions

Social conditions represent psychological or social states affecting characters in social conflict.

Examples:

  • Rattled: Shaken confidence; disadvantage on Influence checks until recovery.
  • Suspicious: Others distrust you; social checks are harder (increase DC or impose disadvantage).
  • Obligated: You owe a favor or debt; can be compelled to act.
  • Humiliated: Public reputation damaged; social standing reduced until you redeem yourself.
  • Charmed: Influenced by charisma or deception; you trust the source (can be resisted with Insight checks).

Recovery:

  • Social conditions recover through narrative actions: apologizing, fulfilling obligations, rebuilding trust, or proving credibility.
  • Some conditions recover with time or successful social interactions.

GM usage:

  • Create social tags to represent social standing, advantages, or vulnerabilities
  • Use tags in social conflict for mechanical advantages or complications
  • Impose social conditions based on social conflict outcomes
  • Let conditions affect social checks and NPC attitudes
  • Recovery requires narrative action, not just rest

Worked Examples

Example 1: Simple Negotiation

Setup: The party needs supplies for a journey. The local merchant, Greta, has the supplies but is reluctant to part with them. The roads are dangerous, and she is not sure the party will return to pay later.

Goals:

  • Players: Secure supplies (food, rope, torches) for the journey.
  • Greta: Ensure payment or get something of equal value.

Leverage:

  • Players: They can offer immediate payment (50 gold), promise to return with a favor (future leverage), or intimidate (threaten to take supplies by force, though this risks reputation).
  • Greta: She can refuse, offer partial supplies, or demand a higher price.

Negotiation:

  1. Players Declare Approach: “We offer 50 gold for the supplies.”
  2. GM Determines Effectiveness: 50 gold is reasonable but Greta is cautious. Roll Influence to convince her.
  3. Check: Player rolls Influence. Result: Partial success.
  4. Outcome: Greta agrees to provide the supplies but asks for a small favor in return: deliver a letter to her sister in the next town. “Do this, and we have a deal.”

Resolution: Players gain the supplies (goal achieved) but gain a minor obligation (cost). The negotiation succeeds with a complication, creating a future hook.

Example 2: Social Combat

Setup: The party stands trial for trespassing in a restricted area. The prosecutor, a stern magistrate named Theron, argues they are spies. The party must defend themselves in open court before a judge and audience (townsfolk).

Stakes:

  • Victory: The party is acquitted and free to continue their mission.
  • Defeat: The party is imprisoned or exiled.

Participants:

  • Players: Defense (3 party members).
  • Theron (Prosecutor): NPC opponent with Resolve 5.
  • Judge: Neutral; decides based on final Resolve and audience favor.
  • Audience: Townsfolk; tracked with “Crowd Favor” clock (6 segments, starts neutral).

Setup:

  • Players and Theron both start with Resolve 4.
  • Initiative: Players roll Influence; highest goes first.

Turn 1 (Player 1): Persuade

  • Action: Player 1 argues they were investigating a threat to the town (truth). Roll Influence. Success.
  • Effect: Reduce Theron’s Resolve by 1 (Resolve 4 → 3). Advance “Crowd Favor” clock by 1 segment toward players (crowd is sympathetic).

Turn 2 (Theron): Intimidate

  • Action: Theron questions Player 1’s credibility aggressively. Roll Intimidate. Success.
  • Effect: Impose Rattled condition on Player 1 (disadvantage on next check). Advance “Crowd Favor” by 1 segment toward Theron (crowd is uncertain).

Turn 3 (Player 2): Insight

  • Action: Player 2 observes Theron, looking for weaknesses. Roll Insight. Success.
  • Effect: Gain advantage on next social action against Theron (spotted a contradiction in his argument).

Turn 4 (Player 3): Persuade (with Advantage)

  • Action: Using Player 2’s insight, Player 3 presents evidence (a map showing the restricted area is unmarked). Roll Influence with advantage. Success.
  • Effect: Reduce Theron’s Resolve by 2 (critical argument). Theron’s Resolve: 3 → 1. Advance “Crowd Favor” by 2 segments toward players (crowd is swayed).

Turn 5 (Theron): Defend

  • Action: Theron steadies himself, recovering composure. No roll.
  • Effect: Remove Rattled condition (if he had one); gain +1 Resolve (Resolve: 1 → 2).

Turn 6 (Player 1, recovering from Rattled): Persuade

  • Action: Player 1 appeals to the judge directly, citing the town’s safety. Roll Influence (no longer disadvantaged). Success.
  • Effect: Reduce Theron’s Resolve by 1 (Resolve: 2 → 1). Advance “Crowd Favor” by 1 segment toward players (clock: 5/6 toward players).

Turn 7 (Theron): Final Argument (Desperate)

  • Action: Theron makes a last-ditch argument, questioning the party’s motives. Roll Influence. Failure.
  • Effect: No effect on player Resolve. The argument falls flat.

Turn 8 (Player 2): Closing Argument

  • Action: Player 2 delivers a passionate closing statement. Roll Influence. Success.
  • Effect: Reduce Theron’s Resolve by 1 (Resolve: 1 → 0). Advance “Crowd Favor” clock fully (6/6 toward players).

Resolution:

  • Theron’s Resolve is reduced to zero. The judge, swayed by the players’ arguments and the crowd’s favor, acquits the party.
  • Outcome: Players are freed. Theron is discredited but not removed (potential future rival). The townsfolk view the party favorably (gain Well-Liked tag in this town).

Example 3: Reputation Challenge

Setup: The party has “Respected” reputation (4 segments) with the Thieves’ Guild. They need the Guild’s help to infiltrate a noble’s estate, but the Guild Master, Vex, is hesitant. A recent failure has made the Guild cautious.

Goals:

  • Players: Secure the Guild’s support (intel, tools, safe house).
  • Vex: Protect the Guild’s interests; avoid risky operations that could expose them.

Reputation and Leverage:

  • Players have “Respected” reputation, granting them a starting advantage (Vex will listen).
  • Players offer leverage: a cut of the noble’s treasure (positive leverage) and information about a rival Guild threatening Vex’s territory (information leverage).

Negotiation:

  1. Opening Offer: Players present their plan and offer leverage. Roll Influence with advantage (due to “Respected” reputation). Success.
    • Effect: Vex is interested but cautious. “I need assurance this will not blow back on us.”
  2. Counter-Offer: Players promise to frame the heist as an inside job (the noble’s servants) rather than Guild activity. Roll Deception to convince Vex the plan is sound. Partial success.
    • Effect: Vex agrees to provide intel and tools but not the safe house (partial cooperation). “If this goes wrong, the Guild will disavow you.”
  3. Final Push: Players sweeten the deal by offering the information about the rival Guild immediately (spending leverage). No roll needed; Vex accepts.
    • Effect: Vex agrees to provide the safe house as well. “You have proven yourselves. Do not make me regret this.”

Resolution:

  • Players gain full Guild support (intel, tools, safe house).
  • Players advance their reputation with the Guild by 1 segment (Respected → 5/6 toward Renowned). The successful negotiation builds trust.
  • Vex becomes a recurring ally but expects continued cooperation.

Future Implications:

  • If the heist succeeds, the party’s reputation advances further (Renowned). They gain significant Guild support in future endeavors.
  • If the heist fails and exposes the Guild, their reputation plummets (Respected → Aware or Mistrusted). Vex becomes an enemy or demands compensation.

Faction Dynamics and Living Worlds

Table of Contents

  • Faction Design
  • Faction Clocks
  • Inter-Faction Dynamics
  • Player Interaction with Factions
  • Emergent World Changes
  • Fronts and Countdowns
  • Worked Example: Three-Faction Intrigue Web

Faction Design

Factions are groups with goals, resources, and agency. They are not static background elements; they act, react, and change based on player choices and world events. Factions create a living world where the setting evolves with or without player intervention.

Factions can be organizations (guilds, governments, cults), communities (villages, tribes, districts), or abstract forces (nature, cosmic entities, ideological movements). Factions drive emergent narrative: their goals and conflicts create opportunities, threats, and hooks.

Faction Template

Design factions using this template:

Name: The faction’s identity.

Goals: What the faction wants (short-term and long-term). Goals drive faction actions.

Assets: Resources, strengths, or advantages. What makes this faction powerful or influential?

Weaknesses: Vulnerabilities or limitations. What can undermine this faction?

Key NPCs: Leaders, agents, or representatives. Faces for the faction that players can interact with.

Hooks: Story opportunities tied to the faction. What might draw players to interact with this faction?

Example Faction: The Silver Compact (Merchant Guild)

  • Name: The Silver Compact
  • Goals: Control trade routes in the region; increase profits; gain political influence in the city council.
  • Assets: Wealth, trade network, bribes and leverage over city officials, skilled negotiators and spies.
  • Weaknesses: Greedy; vulnerable to rivals undercutting prices; internal corruption creates distrust.
  • Key NPCs: Guildmaster Elara Voss (shrewd, calculating); Trade Captain Jareth (loyal but secretly embezzling).
  • Hooks: Players need rare supplies only the Compact can provide; the Compact hires players to eliminate a rival merchant; Jareth’s embezzlement threatens to expose the Compact’s corruption.

Goals and Motivations

Faction goals create narrative drive. Goals should be clear, actionable, and conflicting with other factions or player interests.

Short-Term Goals: Achievable within 1-3 sessions. Example: Secure a contract, eliminate a rival, recruit new members.

Long-Term Goals: Achievable over an arc or campaign. Example: Control the region’s economy, overthrow the government, summon a deity.

Conflicting Goals: Factions with conflicting goals create natural tension and intrigue. Example: The Merchant Guild wants to open trade with a neighboring kingdom; the Isolationist Council wants to close borders.

Assets and Weaknesses

Assets make factions formidable. Weaknesses create opportunities for players (or rival factions) to undermine them.

Assets (Examples):

  • Resources: Wealth, weapons, magic, information.
  • Influence: Political power, public support, blackmail.
  • Personnel: Skilled agents, loyal followers, powerful leaders.
  • Territory: Controlled regions, fortified locations, strategic positions.

Weaknesses (Examples):

  • Internal Conflicts: Corruption, betrayal, rival subfactions.
  • External Pressure: Rivals, enemies, resource scarcity.
  • Moral Limits: Unwillingness to use certain tactics, adherence to codes.
  • Public Perception: Unpopular, distrusted, vulnerable to scandal.

GM usage:

  • Design 3-5 major factions per campaign or arc
  • Give each faction clear goals, assets, and weaknesses
  • Create key NPCs to personify factions
  • Ensure faction goals conflict with each other or player interests
  • Use assets to create faction power; use weaknesses to create player opportunities

Faction Clocks

Faction clocks track faction progress toward goals, escalating conflicts, or changing states. Clocks make faction activity visible and create dynamic, evolving worlds.

Types of Faction Clocks

Progress Clocks: Track faction goals. When a progress clock fills, the faction achieves something.

Examples:

  • The Thieves’ Guild Consolidates Power (8 segments): When filled, the Guild controls the city’s underworld completely.
  • The Cult Summons the Demon (6 segments): When filled, the demon is summoned.
  • The Rebellion Recruits Support (10 segments): When filled, the rebellion has enough support to launch an uprising.

Pressure Clocks: Track threats or countdowns affecting factions. When a pressure clock fills, something bad happens to the faction.

Examples:

  • The Merchant Guild’s Corruption Exposed (4 segments): When filled, the corruption becomes public and the Guild collapses.
  • The Kingdom’s Food Supply Depleted (6 segments): When filled, famine begins.
  • The Alliance Fractures (5 segments): When filled, the alliance dissolves into infighting.

Conflict Clocks: Track escalating tensions between factions. When a conflict clock fills, open conflict (war, duel, coup) erupts.

Examples:

  • Guild War Brewing (6 segments): When filled, the two guilds go to war.
  • Noble Houses Clash (8 segments): When filled, civil war begins.

Advancing Faction Clocks

Advance faction clocks based on:

  • Time Passing: Advance clocks when in-game time passes (days, weeks, sessions). Factions act even when off-screen.
  • Faction Actions: Advance clocks when factions successfully pursue their goals (NPCs complete missions, acquire resources, etc.).
  • Player Inaction: Advance pressure or conflict clocks when players ignore threats or faction activities.
  • Player Actions: Players can advance or reverse clocks through direct intervention. Helping a faction advances their progress clocks. Hindering a faction reverses their clocks or advances pressure clocks against them.

Visibility: Clocks can be visible (players know the cult is summoning a demon and see the clock advancing) or hidden (players sense something is happening but do not know details). Use visible clocks for known threats; hidden clocks for mysteries or slow-building danger.

GM usage:

  • Create clocks for major faction goals and threats
  • Advance clocks between sessions or when in-game time passes
  • Let player actions affect clocks: helping or hindering factions matters
  • Make some clocks visible, others hidden, based on player knowledge
  • When clocks fill, show consequences in the fiction: factions succeed or fail visibly

Inter-Faction Dynamics

Factions do not exist in isolation. They interact: forming alliances, competing, or fighting. Inter-faction dynamics create complexity and emergent narrative.

Faction Relationships

Map relationships between factions:

  • Allies: Factions cooperate toward shared goals. Alliances can be stable or fragile.
  • Rivals: Factions compete for resources, influence, or territory. Rivalry is tense but not violent (yet).
  • Enemies: Factions actively oppose each other, often violently.
  • Neutral: Factions have no strong relationship; they coexist without cooperation or conflict.

Relationship Shifts: Relationships change based on events, player actions, or faction goals. Allies can become rivals. Rivals can become enemies. Enemies can forge temporary alliances against greater threats.

Example Relationship Map:

  • The Silver Compact (Merchant Guild) and The Ironclad Union (Labor Guild): Rivals. The Compact exploits workers; the Union demands better conditions.
  • The Ironclad Union and The Reformist Council (Political Faction): Allies. Both seek to limit the Compact’s power.
  • The Silver Compact and The Shadow Syndicate (Criminal Network): Neutral but tense. The Compact hires the Syndicate for dirty work but distrusts them.
  • The Reformist Council and The Shadow Syndicate: Enemies. The Council seeks to eliminate crime; the Syndicate undermines reform efforts.

Faction Conflicts

Conflicts between factions create dramatic stakes and player opportunities.

Types of Conflicts:

  • Economic: Trade wars, price manipulation, resource competition.
  • Political: Elections, coups, influence over authorities.
  • Territorial: Disputes over land, borders, or strategic locations.
  • Ideological: Opposing beliefs, values, or goals.
  • Violent: Skirmishes, assassinations, open warfare.

Escalation: Conflicts escalate over time. Start with tension (rivals), escalate to sabotage (covert actions), then open conflict (violence or war). Use conflict clocks to track escalation.

Player Role: Players can take sides, mediate, manipulate factions against each other, or remain neutral. Player choices affect conflict outcomes.

GM usage:

  • Map faction relationships (allies, rivals, enemies, neutral)
  • Let relationships shift based on events and player actions
  • Create conflicts between factions (economic, political, territorial, ideological, violent)
  • Use conflict clocks to track escalation
  • Let players intervene, take sides, or mediate conflicts

Player Interaction with Factions

Players interact with factions in multiple ways: joining, opposing, manipulating, or mediating. Player-faction interaction drives emergent narrative.

Joining Factions

Players can align with factions, gaining benefits but also obligations.

Benefits:

  • Resources: Access to faction assets (gear, money, information, safe houses).
  • Support: NPCs assist players (provide backup, intel, introductions).
  • Reputation: Improved standing with the faction and its allies.
  • Missions: Faction provides hooks and goals aligned with their interests.

Obligations:

  • Loyalty: Players are expected to support faction goals.
  • Missions: Factions assign tasks; refusal damages reputation.
  • Conflicts: Aligning with one faction creates tension with rivals or enemies.

Mechanics: Track faction reputation with clocks or scales (see Chapter 11: Social Conflict). High reputation grants benefits; low reputation imposes costs.

Example: Players join the Reformist Council. Benefit: The Council provides safe houses and political support. Obligation: The Council expects players to undermine the Silver Compact and Shadow Syndicate. If players refuse or act against Council interests, reputation falls.

Opposing Factions

Players can oppose factions, hindering their goals and creating enemies.

Methods:

  • Sabotage: Disrupt faction operations (destroy supplies, steal assets, expose secrets).
  • Assassination: Eliminate key NPCs (leaders, agents).
  • Propaganda: Damage faction reputation publicly.
  • Alliances: Join rival factions or form coalitions against the target faction.

Consequences:

  • Factions retaliate: sending agents to attack, hiring assassins, or using political pressure.
  • Faction goals are delayed or reversed (clocks are reduced or stalled).
  • Reputation with the faction becomes hostile; players are marked as enemies.

Example: Players sabotage the Silver Compact’s trade shipments. Consequence: The Compact’s “Control Trade Routes” clock is reduced by 2 segments. Retaliation: The Compact hires the Shadow Syndicate to eliminate the players.

Manipulating Factions

Players can manipulate factions, playing them against each other or steering them toward desired outcomes.

Methods:

  • Deception: Provide false information to provoke faction actions.
  • Leverage: Use secrets or blackmail to force faction cooperation or inaction.
  • Mediation: Broker alliances or peace between factions for player benefit.

Example: Players leak false information to the Silver Compact that the Shadow Syndicate plans to betray them. The Compact attacks the Syndicate, weakening both factions and creating opportunities for the players.

Remaining Neutral

Players can avoid faction entanglements, remaining neutral and unaligned.

Benefits:

  • Freedom to work with any faction without obligation.
  • No enemies created by factional allegiances.
  • Flexibility to choose missions and goals.

Costs:

  • No faction support or resources.
  • Factions may not trust neutral parties or may try to recruit or coerce them.
  • Harder to gain reputation or influence without alignment.

GM usage:

  • Let players join factions; grant benefits but impose obligations
  • Let players oppose factions; show consequences and retaliation
  • Let players manipulate factions; reward clever strategies but create risks
  • Respect player neutrality but create pressure to choose sides
  • Advance or reverse faction clocks based on player actions

Emergent World Changes

Faction activity creates emergent world changes. The world evolves based on faction successes, failures, and conflicts. Emergent changes make the world feel alive and responsive.

Clock-Driven Narrative

When faction clocks fill, the world changes visibly.

Examples:

  • The Cult Summons the Demon (clock fills): A demon manifests in the city. The city descends into chaos. New factions form (demon worshippers, demon hunters). The campaign’s tone and stakes shift.
  • The Rebellion Launches Uprising (clock fills): The rebellion attacks the capital. The government mobilizes forces. Players must choose sides or flee. The map changes as territories shift.
  • The Merchant Guild’s Corruption Exposed (clock fills): The Guild collapses. Rival merchants seize control. Prices fluctuate wildly. NPCs associated with the Guild flee or seek revenge.

Clock-driven changes are not scripted outcomes; they are consequences of faction activity and player choices. Players can prevent, delay, or accelerate clock fills through intervention.

Faction Victories and Defeats

When factions achieve goals or are defeated, the world reflects the outcome.

Faction Victories:

  • Faction gains power, influence, or territory.
  • New opportunities arise (the victorious faction offers missions, alliances).
  • Other factions react (rivals plan retaliation; allies seek to benefit).

Faction Defeats:

  • Faction loses power, collapses, or is absorbed by rivals.
  • Power vacuums create new conflicts (other factions compete to fill the void).
  • NPCs associated with the defeated faction seek revenge, flee, or join new factions.

Example: The Silver Compact achieves its goal: controlling all trade routes. Victory: The Compact dominates the economy. Prices rise. Rival merchants are forced out. The Ironclad Union (labor guild) intensifies protests. New hook: The Union hires the players to sabotage the Compact.

Example: The Shadow Syndicate is defeated by the Reformist Council. Defeat: The Syndicate collapses. Crime drops temporarily. Power vacuum: A new criminal organization rises to fill the void, more brutal and organized. New hook: The Council asks players to investigate the new threat.

Living World Principles

Principle 1: Factions Act Off-Screen: Factions pursue goals even when players are not present. Advance clocks between sessions. Show faction activity through NPC reports, rumors, or visible changes (new guards, closed shops, burned buildings).

Principle 2: Player Choices Matter: Player actions affect faction success or failure. Helping a faction advances their clocks. Opposing a faction delays them. Ignoring a faction allows them to act unopposed.

Principle 3: Consequences Ripple: Faction changes affect other factions and the world. A faction victory creates new tensions. A faction defeat creates power vacuums. Changes cascade.

Principle 4: No Scripted Outcomes: Do not script faction outcomes. Let clocks, player actions, and faction interactions determine results. Be prepared to improvise when factions succeed or fail unexpectedly.

GM usage:

  • Advance faction clocks between sessions; show off-screen activity
  • When clocks fill, show visible world changes
  • Let faction victories and defeats reshape the world
  • Create ripple effects: changes affect other factions and NPCs
  • Embrace emergent outcomes; do not script faction success or failure

Fronts and Countdowns

Fronts are large-scale threats or campaigns with countdowns. Fronts represent faction-driven dangers that escalate over time, forcing players to prioritize and act.

Front Structure

A front is a major threat with:

  • Name: The threat’s identity.
  • Goals: What the front aims to achieve (catastrophic or world-changing outcomes).
  • Stages: Phases of escalation (3-5 stages). Each stage represents progress toward the goal.
  • Countdown Clock: A clock tracking front progress. When it fills, the front achieves its goal.
  • Portents: Signs or events indicating front progress. Portents create tension and inform players.
  • Impending Doom: What happens if the front succeeds. The catastrophic outcome.

Example Front: The Devouring Plague

  • Name: The Devouring Plague
  • Goals: Spread across the region; consume all life; transform the land into a blighted wasteland.
  • Stages:
    1. Isolated outbreaks in remote villages.
    2. Plague spreads to towns; refugees flee.
    3. Major cities affected; quarantine fails.
    4. Plague mutates; infected become monstrous.
    5. The region is overrun; civilization collapses.
  • Countdown Clock: 10 segments (advances 1 segment per session or when players ignore it).
  • Portents:
    • Stage 1: Rumors of illness in a distant village.
    • Stage 2: Refugees arrive, carrying infection.
    • Stage 3: City gates close; supplies run low.
    • Stage 4: Infected creatures attack settlements.
    • Stage 5: The land itself withers; sky darkens.
  • Impending Doom: The region becomes uninhabitable. Survivors flee or die. The campaign setting is destroyed.

Running Fronts

Advance the Front: Progress the countdown clock based on time or player inaction. Advance portents as the clock fills. Describe changes in the world to create urgency.

Player Intervention: Players can slow or stop fronts through direct action (research a cure, destroy the source, rally defenses). Each success reduces the clock or delays advancement.

Multiple Fronts: Run 2-3 fronts simultaneously. Players must prioritize: stop the plague, prevent the war, or thwart the cult? Choices have consequences; ignored fronts progress.

Resolution: Fronts resolve when players defeat them (clock is stopped or reversed) or when the countdown fills (impending doom occurs). Either way, the world changes.

Example Front Resolution:

  • Success: Players discover the plague’s source (a corrupted magical artifact) and destroy it. The plague’s clock stops at 7/10 segments. The plague is contained but not eradicated. Some towns are lost; others recover. Players are hailed as heroes.
  • Failure: Players focus on other threats. The plague clock fills (10/10 segments). Impending doom occurs: the region collapses. Players must flee or adapt to a post-apocalyptic setting.

GM usage:

  • Design fronts for major threats or campaigns
  • Use countdown clocks and portents to create escalating tension
  • Advance fronts based on time or player inaction
  • Let players intervene to slow or stop fronts
  • Run multiple fronts to force prioritization and hard choices
  • Resolve fronts with visible consequences: success or catastrophic failure

Worked Example: Three-Faction Intrigue Web

Campaign Setup: The city of Greyhaven is a hub of trade, politics, and crime. Three factions vie for control: The Silver Compact (merchants), The Reformist Council (politicians), and The Shadow Syndicate (criminals). Players are independent operatives who can ally with, oppose, or manipulate factions.

Faction Profiles

Faction 1: The Silver Compact (Merchant Guild)

  • Goals: Control all trade routes; gain seats on the city council; maximize profits.
  • Assets: Wealth, bribes, trade network, skilled negotiators.
  • Weaknesses: Internal corruption (embezzlement); public perception (greedy, exploitative); rivals (Ironclad Union labor guild).
  • Key NPCs: Guildmaster Elara Voss (shrewd, ruthless); Trade Captain Jareth (secretly embezzling funds).
  • Clocks:
    • Control Trade Routes (progress, 8 segments): When filled, the Compact monopolizes trade.
    • Corruption Exposed (pressure, 4 segments): When filled, the Compact’s corruption becomes public and the Guild collapses.

Faction 2: The Reformist Council (Political Faction)

  • Goals: Limit corporate power; improve labor conditions; eliminate organized crime.
  • Assets: Popular support, political influence, grassroots network.
  • Weaknesses: Fractured leadership (internal disagreements); vulnerable to bribes and blackmail; idealistic (unwilling to compromise).
  • Key NPCs: Councilor Mira Thane (idealistic reformer); Captain Aric Dray (city guard allied with Council).
  • Clocks:
    • Pass Reform Legislation (progress, 6 segments): When filled, the Council enacts reforms limiting the Compact’s power.
    • Council Fractures (pressure, 5 segments): When filled, internal disagreements split the Council; reform efforts stall.

Faction 3: The Shadow Syndicate (Criminal Network)

  • Goals: Control the underworld; expand smuggling and extortion; corrupt city officials.
  • Assets: Spies, assassins, blackmail material, fear and intimidation.
  • Weaknesses: Hunted by the Council and city guard; internal power struggles (rival gangs); public hatred.
  • Key NPCs: The Viper (mysterious crime lord, identity unknown); Shade (enforcer, loyal to the Viper).
  • Clocks:
    • Corrupt City Officials (progress, 6 segments): When filled, the Syndicate controls key officials; Council reforms are blocked.
    • Syndicate Hunted (pressure, 4 segments): When filled, the Council and guard launch a major crackdown; Syndicate is driven underground or destroyed.

Faction Relationships

  • The Silver Compact and The Reformist Council: Enemies. The Council seeks to limit the Compact’s power. The Compact bribes officials to block reforms.
  • The Reformist Council and The Shadow Syndicate: Enemies. The Council seeks to eliminate crime. The Syndicate sabotages reform efforts.
  • The Silver Compact and The Shadow Syndicate: Neutral but tense. The Compact hires the Syndicate for dirty work (sabotaging rivals, intimidating labor organizers) but distrusts them.

Interlocking Clocks

The factions’ clocks are interconnected:

  • If the Compact’s Control Trade Routes clock fills, the Council’s Pass Reform Legislation clock slows (disadvantage; the Compact has too much power).
  • If the Council’s Pass Reform Legislation clock fills, the Compact’s Control Trade Routes clock is reduced (reforms limit the Compact’s monopoly).
  • If the Syndicate’s Corrupt City Officials clock fills, the Council’s Pass Reform Legislation clock is blocked (corrupt officials sabotage reforms).
  • If the Council’s Syndicate Hunted clock fills, the Syndicate’s Corrupt City Officials clock is reduced (Syndicate loses influence).

Player Hooks

Hook 1 (Compact): Guildmaster Elara Voss hires the players to eliminate a rival merchant (secretly a Council ally). Payment: 500 gold and trade connections. Complication: If players accept, the Council becomes hostile.

Hook 2 (Council): Councilor Mira Thane asks the players to investigate the Compact’s corruption. Payment: Political favor and public recognition. Complication: The Compact retaliates if players are discovered.

Hook 3 (Syndicate): The Viper (through an intermediary) offers the players a job: smuggle cursed artifacts into the city. Payment: 300 gold and Syndicate protection. Complication: The artifacts are dangerous and illegal.

Hook 4 (Neutral): A labor organizer (Ironclad Union) begs the players to protect workers from Syndicate intimidation. Payment: None (they are poor), but the Union’s gratitude and moral satisfaction. Complication: The Syndicate targets the players.

Campaign Flow (Sessions 1-6)

Session 1: Introduction

  • Players arrive in Greyhaven. They witness a protest (Ironclad Union vs Compact). They are approached by multiple factions offering jobs (hooks above).
  • Players choose which hook to pursue. Their choice determines initial faction alignment.
  • GM advances all faction clocks by 1 segment (off-screen activity).

Session 2: First Mission

  • Players complete their chosen mission (smuggle artifacts, investigate corruption, eliminate rival, or protect workers).
  • Outcome determines faction clock advancement: helping a faction advances their progress clocks; opposing a faction advances their pressure clocks.
  • Example: If players investigate the Compact’s corruption, they find evidence (Trade Captain Jareth is embezzling). The Compact’s Corruption Exposed clock advances by 2 segments.

Session 3: Faction Reacts

  • The faction players opposed retaliates. Example: If players exposed Jareth’s embezzlement, the Compact hires the Syndicate to assassinate the players.
  • Players must respond: fight, negotiate, or flee.
  • GM advances clocks: Compact’s Control Trade Routes (1 segment), Syndicate’s Corrupt City Officials (1 segment), Council’s Pass Reform Legislation (1 segment, but slowed by Syndicate corruption).

Session 4: Escalation

  • Multiple clocks are filling. Tension rises. The Compact and Council clash publicly (debate in the city square). The Syndicate assassinates a Council member.
  • Players must choose: intensify efforts with their allied faction, switch sides, or manipulate factions against each other.
  • Example: Players leak the Compact’s corruption evidence to the public. The Corruption Exposed clock fills (4/4 segments). The Compact collapses (Elara Voss flees; Jareth is arrested). The Council celebrates; the Syndicate moves to fill the power vacuum.

Session 5: Power Vacuum

  • The Compact’s collapse creates new conflicts. The Syndicate expands operations (Corrupt City Officials clock advances by 2 segments). The Council struggles to maintain order (Council Fractures clock advances by 1 segment due to disagreements on how to manage the transition).
  • New hooks: The Council asks players to stop the Syndicate’s expansion. The Syndicate offers players a deal: join them or be eliminated.
  • Players choose their endgame strategy.

Session 6: Climax and Resolution

  • Final confrontations. Players either help the Council eliminate the Syndicate or join/manipulate the Syndicate to control the city.
  • Possible Outcomes:
    • Council Victory: The Council’s Pass Reform Legislation clock fills. Reforms are enacted. The Syndicate is driven underground (Syndicate Hunted clock fills). Greyhaven becomes a reformed, lawful city. Players are hailed as heroes.
    • Syndicate Victory: The Syndicate’s Corrupt City Officials clock fills. The Syndicate controls the city through corruption. The Council fractures (Council Fractures clock fills). Greyhaven becomes a criminal haven. Players are either powerful allies or enemies of the new order.
    • Stalemate: No faction fully wins. The city remains in tense balance. Players become power brokers, mediating conflicts and managing faction dynamics indefinitely.

GM Preparation

Prep Before Play:

  1. Map faction clocks and relationships visually (draw diagram).
  2. Prepare key NPCs (stats, goals, personalities).
  3. Design hooks for each faction.
  4. Prepare modular encounters (assassinations, investigations, chases, debates) deployable based on player choices.

During Play:

  1. Track faction clocks each session. Advance based on time and player actions.
  2. Let players choose faction alliances freely; do not railroad.
  3. Show faction activity off-screen (NPCs report developments; visible changes in the city).
  4. Adjust faction tactics based on player actions (factions react intelligently).
  5. Build toward climax as clocks fill; make final sessions dramatic and consequential.

Post-Campaign:

  • Reflect on faction outcomes. Which faction won? How did players’ choices shape the city?
  • Use outcomes as the foundation for future campaigns or arcs. Example: A Syndicate-controlled city becomes the setting for a rebellion campaign.

Cross-Links

  • Clocks and timers: GM Guide Chapter 6: Mechanics Overview
  • Campaign frameworks: Chapter 6: Campaign Frameworks

  • Reputation systems: Chapter 11: Social Conflict and Negotiation
