Running Horror and Mystery Campaigns

Table of Contents

  • Horror Fundamentals
  • Mystery Integration
  • Fear and Stress Mechanics
  • Information Control and Uncertainty
  • Cosmic Horror vs Personal Horror
  • Safety in Horror Games
  • Worked Example: Haunted House Investigation
  • Cross-Links

Horror Fundamentals

Horror thrives on atmosphere, not mechanics. It emerges from dread, uncertainty, and the slow erosion of safety. The goal is to make players feel tension while keeping their characters in motion.

Dread vs Shock

Dread builds over time through atmosphere, foreshadowing, and escalating uncertainty. Shock delivers sudden impact through reveals or violence. Effective horror balances both: dread sustains tension, shock punctuates it.

Dread tools:

  • Repeated sensory details that suggest wrongness
  • Clocks that tick without clear consequences
  • NPCs who behave erratically or know too much
  • Environments that shift subtly between scenes

Shock tools:

  • Sudden reveals after prolonged tension
  • Failed rolls that introduce immediate danger
  • Environmental hazards triggered by investigation
  • NPC betrayals or transformations

Atmosphere Building

Atmosphere comes from what you emphasize in descriptions and what you withhold. Focus on sensory details that suggest threat without confirming it.

Atmosphere techniques:

  • Describe sound and smell before sight
  • Use silence as a tool; pause before answering questions
  • Repeat specific details to create pattern recognition
  • Leave gaps in descriptions; invite players to fill them

Example atmosphere cues:

  • “The hallway smells like copper and wet earth.”
  • “You hear breathing, but it matches your own rhythm.”
  • “The door is open exactly as wide as when you left.”
  • “Everything is where it should be. That’s what bothers you.”

Pacing Dread

Horror pacing differs from action pacing. Stretch scenes longer than comfortable, compress relief moments, and escalate pressure through accumulation rather than spikes.

Pacing structure:

  1. Establish normalcy with one safe scene
  2. Introduce wrongness through subtle details
  3. Escalate wrongness through investigation
  4. Deliver shock after prolonged dread
  5. Offer brief relief that feels incomplete
  6. Repeat with higher baseline tension

GM usage:

  • Time horror scenes by player discomfort, not clock
  • Use pressure clocks that advance on investigation, not just failures
  • Compress travel and preparation; expand investigation and confrontation
  • Cut relief scenes before full resolution

Mystery Integration

Horror and mystery pair naturally. Investigation drives players into danger while dread makes them hesitate. The tension between curiosity and caution creates compelling choices.

Investigation Pacing in Horror

Horror mysteries reward slow investigation. Use the three-clue rule but deliver clues with cost. Every answer should raise new questions or introduce complications.

Investigation structure:

  • Core clues reveal on Partial successes with complications
  • Supporting clues reveal on Successes but with atmospheric cost
  • Failures introduce danger or corrupt existing information
  • Red herrings reinforce theme or foreshadow true threat

GM usage:

  • Plant three routes to each core clue
  • Make clues visceral: not just information but experience
  • Use conditions as investigative costs (Shaken, Paranoid, Obsessed)
  • Reward investigation with dread escalation, not safety

Information as Horror

The unknown terrifies more than the known. Control information flow to maximize uncertainty. Reveal mechanics partially, describe threats incompletely, and let players imagine worse than reality.

Information techniques:

  • Describe effects before causes
  • Use unreliable narration for perception-based rolls
  • Provide conflicting clues from different sources
  • Reveal threats through absence and negative space

Example information reveals:

  • Partial (5): “You find three sets of tracks. Two enter the room. Five leave.”
  • Success (7-9): “The ritual circle is fresh. The symbols match the book, but they’re inverted. Someone tried this wrong, or they tried something else.”
  • Critical (10+): “The chalk is still warm. They finished this within the last hour, and the circle isn’t for summoning. It’s for hiding.”

Fear and Stress Mechanics

Horror campaigns benefit from mechanics that track psychological erosion alongside physical harm. Use conditions, clocks, and breaking points to make fear tangible without removing agency.

Fear Conditions

Fear conditions represent temporary psychological states triggered by horror. They impose narrative costs and mechanical complications without disabling characters.

Example fear conditions:

  • Shaken: -1 to first roll in each scene until you take protective action
  • Paranoid: You must ask one suspicious question before trusting information
  • Obsessed: You must pursue investigation leads even when risky
  • Unraveling: Describe one irrational behavior each scene

GM usage:

  • Apply fear conditions on failures against horror threats
  • Let players clear conditions through roleplay or downtime
  • Use conditions to suggest choices, not dictate them
  • Escalate conditions if ignored (Shaken → Paranoid → Unraveling)

Stress Clocks

Stress clocks track cumulative psychological pressure over multiple scenes. They represent breaking points, not immediate failure. When a stress clock fills, the character faces a crisis that demands meaningful choice.

Stress clock structure:

  • 4-segment clock for single-session horror
  • 6-segment clock for arc-length horror
  • Advance on exposure to horror, failed saves, or voluntary investigation
  • Fill triggers crisis scene, not automatic breakdown

Crisis options when clock fills:

  • Break: Take major condition but gain clarity on threat
  • Flee: Remove self from danger but abandon investigation
  • Deny: Refuse to acknowledge threat; gain temporary calm but future vulnerability
  • Confront: Face fear directly with boosted stakes

GM usage:

  • Make clock fills dramatic moments, not punishment
  • Offer meaningful choices at crisis points
  • Allow recovery through resolution or downtime
  • Use multiple PCs filling clocks near-simultaneously for ensemble horror

Breaking Points

Breaking points occur when stress clocks fill or when characters face overwhelming horror. They create dramatic moments where players choose how their character responds to fear.

Breaking point procedure:

  1. Announce the breaking point trigger
  2. Describe the immediate horror
  3. Offer three response options (break/flee/deny/confront)
  4. Resolve choice with appropriate mechanical consequence
  5. Adjust character state and campaign tension

GM usage:

  • Telegraph breaking points one scene ahead
  • Make all options costly but viable
  • Respect player choice even if it derails investigation
  • Use breaking points to reveal character depth

Information Control and Uncertainty

Horror depends on what players don’t know. Control information through timing, reliability, and presentation to maintain uncertainty without frustrating investigation.

Partial Revelations

Reveal information in layers. First impressions mislead, second looks add detail, and full understanding comes only through accumulation and risk.

Revelation structure:

  • Immediate: Sensory surface (sight, sound, smell)
  • Investigation: Context and history
  • Risk: Mechanics and true nature
  • Crisis: Implications and cost

Example revelation layers (ritual site):

  • Immediate: “The basement smells like charcoal and rust.”
  • Investigation (Partial): “The floor has scorch marks in a circle. Recent.”
  • Investigation (Success): “The symbols are protection wards, but they’re drawn backward. This doesn’t keep something out. It keeps something in.”
  • Risk (touching the circle): “The wards flare blue. You feel a pull from below, and something responds.”

Unreliable Narration

Use perception-based rolls to introduce uncertainty about reality. Describe what characters experience, not objective truth. Let players wonder if they can trust their senses.

Unreliable narration triggers:

  • Failed perception checks return false positives
  • Partial successes provide accurate information with misleading context
  • Critical successes confirm truth but introduce new uncertainty
  • Conditions (Paranoid, Obsessed) color future narration

GM usage:

  • Describe perception failures as positive experiences, not absences
  • Mix true and false details on partials
  • Let players compare notes to identify discrepancies
  • Confirm or deny reality only when mechanically necessary

The Unknown

Some horror works best when never fully explained. Preserve mystery by revealing enough to act but not enough to dismiss. Let players imagine horrors beyond what you describe.

Techniques for preserving mystery:

  • Describe threats through effects on environment and NPCs
  • Provide contradictory information from credible sources
  • Reveal mechanics partially (some vulnerabilities, not all)
  • End some investigations with ambiguous resolution

GM usage:

  • Decide in prep what stays unknown
  • Distinguish need-to-know (mechanics for confrontation) from mystery (origin, nature)
  • Respect player curiosity but reward it with deeper questions
  • Use aftermath scenes to suggest unresolved elements

Cosmic Horror vs Personal Horror

Horror scales from intimate dread to existential terror. Cosmic horror emphasizes insignificance and incomprehensibility. Personal horror emphasizes violation and loss. Both work in the same campaign at different scales.

Cosmic Horror

Cosmic horror presents threats beyond human understanding or resistance. It emphasizes powerlessness, incomprehensibility, and the insignificance of human concerns.

Cosmic horror elements:

  • Threats with unclear motives or alien logic
  • Victories that reveal larger dangers
  • Knowledge that corrupts rather than empowers
  • Scale that dwarfs human agency

Cosmic horror mechanics:

  • Stress clocks that advance on learning truth
  • Conditions triggered by comprehension (Enlightened, Marked)
  • Victories that pause threats but don’t eliminate them
  • Resolution through acceptance or sacrifice, not triumph

GM usage:

  • Keep threat mechanics simple; complexity lives in description
  • Make investigation reveal scale, not solutions
  • Offer victories that matter without implying control
  • Use cosmic horror sparingly; contrast with human-scale stakes

Personal Horror

Personal horror focuses on violation, loss, and transformation. It makes threats intimate and consequences permanent. The danger threatens identity, relationships, or body.

Personal horror elements:

  • Threats that know or target the PCs specifically
  • Stakes rooted in character bonds and history
  • Transformation or corruption over time
  • Moral compromises with lasting consequences

Personal horror mechanics:

  • Conditions that change character abilities or restrictions
  • Clocks representing corruption or transformation
  • Social costs that damage bonds and reputation
  • Choices between bad options with permanent effects

GM usage:

  • Root horror in character flags and bonds
  • Make consequences visible in roleplay and mechanics
  • Use personal horror to escalate cosmic horror (the alien becomes intimate)
  • Allow some victories to preserve agency and hope

Blending Scales

Effective horror campaigns often blend cosmic and personal elements. Cosmic horror provides the setting and stakes. Personal horror provides the emotional weight and immediate danger.

Blending structure:

  • Open with personal horror to establish tone and stakes
  • Introduce cosmic elements gradually as investigation deepens
  • Use personal consequences to make cosmic horror tangible
  • Resolve cosmic arcs through personal sacrifice or transformation

Example blend:

  • Arc opens with personal stakes (missing family member)
  • Investigation reveals cosmic threat (ritual to summon entity)
  • Personal horror escalates (PCs marked by entity, relationships strained)
  • Resolution requires personal sacrifice (break bond to close portal)
  • Aftermath suggests cosmic horror persists (entity remains, but sleeping)

Safety in Horror Games

Horror requires explicit safety tools and ongoing calibration. What terrifies one player traumatizes another. Establish boundaries, check in frequently, and prioritize player well-being over narrative effect.

Pre-Game Safety

Discuss boundaries in Session Zero before introducing horror content. Cover specific content areas, general discomfort, and exit procedures.

Session Zero horror checklist:

  • Lines and veils (content to exclude vs content to keep offscreen)
  • Specific phobias or trauma triggers
  • Preferred horror subtypes (psychological vs gore vs cosmic)
  • Comfort with PC death or permanent consequences
  • Exit procedures (breaks, session end, campaign exit)

GM usage:

  • Provide content questionnaire before Session Zero
  • Discuss boundaries privately if needed
  • Document lines and veils for reference
  • Revisit boundaries if campaign escalates

Safety Tools at the Table

Use established safety tools and adapt them to your table’s needs. Make them accessible and normalize their use.

Recommended tools:

  • X-Card: Any player can tap out of content without explanation
  • Open Door: Players can leave and return without judgment
  • Script Change: Players can rewind, fast-forward, or pause scenes
  • Check-In: Regular calibration questions during and after sessions

GM usage:

  • Introduce tools explicitly; demonstrate use
  • Use them yourself to normalize
  • Respect tool use immediately without requiring explanation
  • Follow up privately if patterns emerge

Aftercare

Horror sessions can leave residual tension. Provide transition back to normalcy through debrief, tone shift, and emotional check-in.

Aftercare practices:

  • End sessions 15 minutes early for debrief
  • Shift tone with casual conversation or comedy
  • Offer stars and wishes (highlights and concerns)
  • Check in individually with players who seemed affected

GM usage:

  • Build aftercare time into session schedule
  • Normalize emotional response to horror content
  • Adjust intensity if aftercare becomes consistently needed
  • Offer campaign breaks or tone shifts if burnout appears

Adjusting Horror Intensity

Horror tolerance varies by player and changes over time. Calibrate intensity through observation, check-ins, and willingness to adjust.

Calibration signals:

  • Player disengagement or distraction during horror scenes
  • Requests for breaks or topic changes
  • Reduced roleplay or mechanical engagement
  • Post-session comments about discomfort

Intensity adjustments:

  • Reduce descriptive detail; focus on mechanical effects
  • Shift from personal to cosmic horror (or reverse)
  • Introduce more relief scenes and victories
  • Pause horror arc for palette cleanser episodes

GM usage:

  • Observe player engagement, not just stated comfort
  • Adjust proactively when patterns emerge
  • Discuss changes privately if individual adjustment needed
  • Remember: good horror requires trust, not endurance

Worked Example: Haunted House Investigation

This example demonstrates horror pacing, mystery integration, and stress mechanics in a single-session investigation.

Scenario Setup

The PCs investigate a manor house where the owner, Lord Ashford, has locked himself in his study for three days. His servants are too frightened to enter. The house has a history of tragedy: Ashford’s wife drowned in the estate pond five years ago.

Horror elements:

  • Personal horror: The house reflects Ashford’s grief and guilt
  • Mystery: What happened to Lady Ashford, and what’s happening now?
  • Stress: Investigation reveals both supernatural threat and human tragedy

Arc structure:

  1. Arrival and investigation (dread building)
  2. Discovery and escalation (first shock)
  3. Confrontation and choice (crisis)
  4. Resolution and aftermath (ambiguous ending)

Scene 1: Arrival

Frame: The PCs arrive at dusk. The house is dark except for candlelight in the upper study window. The servants wait outside, refusing to enter.

Atmosphere cues:

  • “The pond is perfectly still. No insects, no ripples.”
  • “The servants won’t meet your eyes when you ask what they heard.”
  • “The front door is unlocked. It swings open before you touch it.”

Investigation prompts:

  • Question servants (Success reveals: wet footprints appear and vanish; whispers from empty rooms)
  • Examine the pond (Partial reveals: something large beneath the surface; plants grow in spiral patterns)
  • Enter the house (automatic; door closes behind them)

Stress clock introduced: 4-segment “Breaking Point” clock for the house investigation.

Advance stress clock (1 segment): Upon entering. “The air pressure changes. Your ears pop.”

Scene 2: Investigation

The PCs explore the ground floor. Evidence suggests someone has been moving through the house, but not Lord Ashford.

Clues (three routes to each):

Core clue 1: Lady Ashford died during a ritual, not by drowning.

  • Library: Partial reveals diary entry about “binding ceremony”
  • Drawing room: Success reveals ritual circle hidden under rug
  • Servants’ testimony: Success reveals she went to pond “prepared”

Core clue 2: Lord Ashford completed the ritual three days ago.

  • Study door (locked): Failed force check reveals fresh scratches on inside
  • Kitchen: Success reveals ingredients for ritual (salt, iron, sage) recently used
  • Ashford’s bedroom: Partial reveals he hasn’t slept here in weeks

Core clue 3: The ritual bound Lady Ashford’s spirit to the house, but something went wrong.

  • Ritual circle: Success reveals inverted symbols (binding became imprisonment)
  • Pond examination: Critical reveals her body never recovered
  • Study door whispers: Partial reveals two voices arguing (Ashford and another)

Stress advances:

  • Advance clock (2 segments): When wet footprints appear and lead toward the study
  • Advance clock (3 segments): When whispers start calling PC names

Shock moment: A figure appears in a mirror. It’s Lady Ashford, and she gestures toward the study. When PCs turn, she’s behind them. Stress clock fills (4 segments).

Scene 3: Breaking Point

The stress clock fills. Each PC faces a breaking point choice:

  • Confront: Approach Lady Ashford and demand answers
  • Flee: Leave the house and report failure
  • Deny: Insist this is delusion and force the study door
  • Break: Accept the horror and ask how to help

Example resolution (Confront):

PC approaches Lady Ashford. She speaks: “He won’t let me go. I won’t let him forget.”

This reveals core truth: Ashford bound her out of guilt, and she’s trapped between wanting peace and wanting him to suffer. The PCs can now choose sides.

Scene 4: Confrontation

The PCs force the study door (or Lady Ashford opens it). Inside, Lord Ashford maintains the ritual, believing he’s protecting her. He’s gaunt, exhausted, and convinced letting go means accepting her death.

Confrontation options:

  • Convince Ashford to release her (opposed social check)
  • Break the ritual by force (combat against manifestations)
  • Bargain with Lady Ashford (negotiation with supernatural stakes)
  • Discover compromise (investigation to find modified ritual)

Mechanical stakes: Whichever approach succeeds, someone pays a cost:

  • Ashford’s release means accepting her death (he may not survive grief)
  • Forced break traps her spirit elsewhere (pond, PCs, or dispersed)
  • Bargain requires PC to carry part of her binding (condition: Haunted)
  • Compromise requires sacrifice (bond, memory, or time)

Resolution: The PCs choose their approach and make the check. Success releases Lady Ashford. Partial releases her but with consequences. Failure traps everyone until they find another way.

Example outcome (Partial on social check):

Lady Ashford is released, but Ashford dies of exhaustion as the ritual breaks. She manifests one final time to thank the PCs and warn: “Some bindings can’t be undone. Only transferred.” One PC gains condition Haunted (minor): “You sometimes see her reflection in still water.”

Aftermath

The PCs leave the house. The servants report the candle in the study has gone out. The pond is still, but now it reflects the stars.

Ambiguous elements preserved:

  • Did Lady Ashford find peace, or just new imprisonment?
  • Was Ashford’s love genuine, or was the binding selfish?
  • What did she mean by “some bindings can’t be undone”?

GM usage:

  • Use this as a one-shot or introduce Haunted condition as arc hook
  • If continuing, Lady Ashford’s warning foreshadows larger ritual network
  • Stress clock resets, but PCs remember this breaking point in future horror scenes
  • Adjust resolution based on player choices; preserve some mystery

Running Cozy and Low-Combat Campaigns

Table of Contents

  • Cozy Campaign Fundamentals
  • Reframing Conflict
  • Downtime as Primary Play
  • Soft Failure Costs
  • Social and Exploration Emphasis
  • Celebration and Ritual
  • Worked Example: Harvest Festival Crisis
  • Cross-Links

Cozy Campaign Fundamentals

Cozy campaigns prioritize comfort, community, and low-stakes problem solving. The tone emphasizes collaboration over competition, growth over conflict, and belonging over heroism. Stakes matter, but consequences lean toward complication and obligation rather than harm and loss.

What Makes a Campaign Cozy

Cozy campaigns focus on daily life, relationships, and community challenges. The world can have danger, but it stays at the margins. The PCs create safety and belonging rather than fight to survive.

Cozy elements:

  • Stakes rooted in community needs, not life-or-death crises
  • Conflict driven by misunderstanding, scarcity, or natural challenges
  • Resolution through collaboration, creativity, and relationship building
  • Tone that balances earnestness with gentle humor
  • Setting that feels lived-in and improvable

Not cozy elements (to avoid or minimize):

  • Violence as primary conflict resolution
  • Betrayal or cruelty from trusted NPCs
  • Irreversible loss or tragedy
  • Prolonged tension without relief
  • Mechanics that punish rather than complicate

Stakes Without Violence

Cozy campaigns need stakes that matter without threatening harm. The key is making outcomes meaningful to character identity, relationships, and community standing.

Cozy stakes examples:

  • Will the harvest festival succeed? (community reputation)
  • Can we convince the merchant to stay? (economic stability)
  • Will we finish the renovation in time? (personal pride)
  • Can we mediate the neighbor dispute? (social harmony)
  • Will the recipe work for the competition? (creative validation)

GM usage:

  • Frame stakes in terms of belonging and reputation, not survival
  • Make failure create obligation or complication, not catastrophe
  • Let successes build community resources and relationships
  • Use stakes to showcase PC skills and character growth

Community Focus

Cozy campaigns center on community as character. The town, village, or neighborhood has personality, needs, and growth arcs. The PCs are embedded members, not passing adventurers.

Community elements:

  • Recurring NPCs with goals, quirks, and relationships
  • Communal spaces (market, tavern, square, workshop)
  • Seasonal events and rituals that structure time
  • Shared resources and collective challenges
  • Reputation and social standing as progression

GM usage:

  • Create 8-12 core NPCs with distinct voices and needs
  • Map physical community spaces for consistent reference
  • Use seasonal calendar to pace events and downtime
  • Track community reputation as a clock or resource
  • Let PCs shape community through projects and relationships

Reframing Conflict

Cozy campaigns still need conflict, but it takes different forms. Reframe traditional obstacles into social, economic, and environmental challenges that reward creativity over combat.

Social Challenges

Social challenges arise from misunderstanding, incompatible needs, or resource competition. They resolve through communication, compromise, and relationship building.

Social challenge types:

  • Mediation: Two parties need help finding compromise
  • Persuasion: Someone needs convincing to try something new
  • Collaboration: Multiple parties must work together despite differences
  • Reputation: PCs must prove themselves to skeptical community members
  • Teaching: PCs help someone learn a skill or overcome fear

Example social challenge (mediation):

The baker and the miller are feuding over flour prices. The baker can’t afford the increase, and the miller needs to cover rising costs. Neither will compromise because of old grudges. The PCs must mediate, find creative solutions, or help them remember why they were friends.

Possible resolutions:

  • Negotiate bulk pricing that works for both
  • Find additional revenue streams for the miller
  • Organize community investment in the mill
  • Facilitate a conversation about the original grudge
  • Create a festival event that requires their cooperation

Economic Challenges

Economic challenges involve resource management, trade, and sustainability. They reward planning, creativity, and community cooperation.

Economic challenge types:

  • Scarcity: Essential resource is limited or expensive
  • Trade: Need to establish or maintain trade relationships
  • Investment: Project requires collective resources
  • Competition: Market or festival creates friendly rivalry
  • Sustainability: Balance resource extraction with renewal

Example economic challenge (scarcity):

Winter came early, and the fishing boats can’t reach the deep waters. The community needs protein sources, but hunting would require entering the contested forest. The PCs must find alternative solutions or negotiate access.

Possible resolutions:

  • Establish mushroom farming in cellars
  • Negotiate with forest wardens for limited access
  • Trade with distant community for preserved fish
  • Organize ice fishing on the frozen river
  • Create protein alternatives from available crops

Environmental Challenges

Environmental challenges come from weather, geography, or natural cycles. They require adaptation, preparation, and respect for natural forces.

Environmental challenge types:

  • Weather: Storm, drought, or cold threatens community
  • Terrain: Geography limits access or creates hazards
  • Wildlife: Animals cause problems but aren’t monsters
  • Seasons: Seasonal changes require preparation
  • Natural events: Floods, fires, or earthquakes

Example environmental challenge (weather):

Unexpected rain threatens the apple harvest. The fruit must be picked and stored within three days or it will rot. The community needs all hands, but several families are dealing with flooded homes. The PCs must coordinate response to both crises.

Possible resolutions:

  • Organize community harvest with shifts for home repair
  • Devise quick-drying storage methods
  • Prioritize and accept partial loss with dignity
  • Use magical or technological solutions (if available)
  • Turn crisis into opportunity for cider making or preserves

GM usage:

  • Mix challenge types to create variety
  • Layer challenges (economic crisis caused by environmental event)
  • Let failures create new challenges, not dead ends
  • Reward creative solutions with community recognition
  • Use challenges to showcase different PC skills

Downtime as Primary Play

Traditional TTRPGs treat downtime as bookkeeping between adventures. Cozy campaigns make downtime the main play mode. Projects, relationships, and routines become the content.

Project Play

Projects give structure to downtime. They’re multi-session endeavors that require planning, resource gathering, and collaboration. They provide tangible progress and community impact.

Project types:

  • Construction: Build or renovate structure (workshop, garden, community center)
  • Creation: Craft item or art piece (tapestry, recipe collection, memorial)
  • Event planning: Organize celebration or competition (festival, wedding, tournament)
  • Problem solving: Address community need (irrigation system, school curriculum)
  • Exploration: Map area or document nature (local ecosystem, historical sites)

Project structure:

  1. Conception: PCs identify need or desire
  2. Planning: Determine steps, resources, and timeline
  3. Execution: 2-4 sessions of play focused on project tasks
  4. Complication: Obstacle requires adaptation or help
  5. Completion: Deliver project and see community impact

Example project (construction):

The PCs decide to build a greenhouse to extend the growing season. They need to gather materials, design the structure, recruit help, and construct it before winter. The project spans four sessions with social, economic, and practical challenges.

Session breakdown:

  • Session 1: Planning and material gathering (negotiate with woodcutter, trade for glass)
  • Session 2: Site preparation and foundation (environmental challenge: rocky soil)
  • Session 3: Construction (social challenge: coordinate volunteers)
  • Session 4: Completion and celebration (showcase success, plan first crops)

GM usage:

  • Use clocks to track project progress (6-8 segments)
  • Introduce one complication per project to create drama
  • Let failures delay or modify projects, not cancel them
  • Celebrate completions with community events
  • Make projects create new opportunities or challenges

Relationship Development

Relationships are both motivation and content. Track bonds with NPCs, develop them through play, and let them create story hooks and mechanical benefits.

Relationship mechanics:

  • Bond clocks: Track relationship development (stranger → acquaintance → friend → close friend)
  • Relationship moves: Special interactions available at different bond levels
  • Favor economy: Help others, receive help in return
  • Community standing: Reputation affects initial NPC disposition
  • Relationship conflicts: Incompatible bonds create interesting choices

Example relationship development:

The PCs befriend the elderly herbalist. Initially wary (stranger), she warms after they help repair her fence (acquaintance). They become friends when they spend downtime learning about local plants. At close friend level, she shares family recipes and teaches rare herb identification.

Mechanical benefits by bond level:

  • Acquaintance: Willing to trade fairly, answer basic questions
  • Friend: Offers favors, shares useful information, provides hospitality
  • Close friend: Provides significant help, teaches skills, trusts with secrets
  • Confidant: Becomes narrative anchor, provides home base, supports risky choices

GM usage:

  • Create relationship clocks for 6-8 core NPCs
  • Advance clocks through play, not just time
  • Let bonds create obligations and story hooks
  • Use relationship conflicts for drama (help friend A or friend B?)
  • Make high-level bonds provide mechanical advantages

Routine and Ritual

Cozy campaigns benefit from routine. Recurring activities create rhythm, showcase character development, and provide reliable comfort between challenges.

Routine examples:

  • Morning market: Weekly shopping and gossip gathering
  • Communal meals: Regular tavern nights or potlucks
  • Skill practice: Ongoing training or craft work
  • Seasonal activities: Harvest, planting, celebrations
  • Personal rituals: Journaling, prayer, creative work

GM usage:

  • Establish 2-3 recurring routine scenes
  • Use routines to deliver information and hooks naturally
  • Show time passing through seasonal routine changes
  • Let players describe their character’s routines for depth
  • Skip routines during crisis; return to them for relief

Soft Failure Costs

Cozy campaigns need consequences without cruelty. Failures create complications, obligations, and embarrassment rather than injury, death, or irreversible loss.

Failure Types for Cozy Play

Reframe traditional failure costs into cozy-appropriate consequences. The pattern: something goes wrong, but it can be addressed or accepted.

Cozy failure costs:

  • Obligation: You succeed, but now you owe someone
  • Complication: Problem isn’t solved, it’s different now
  • Delay: Solution takes longer than expected
  • Embarrassment: Social cost, not mechanical penalty
  • Resource cost: Spend money, time, or material
  • Relationship strain: Someone is disappointed, not betrayed
  • Limited success: Get what you need, not what you want

Example failure resolutions:

Traditional: You fail to pick the lock. The door stays shut. (dead end)

Cozy: You can’t pick the lock, but the innkeeper’s daughter saw you trying. She’ll let you in if you help her with a delivery tomorrow. (obligation)

Traditional: You fail to convince the merchant. She refuses your offer. (negotiation ends)

Cozy: She’s not convinced, but she respects the attempt. She’ll think about it overnight, giving you time to find a sweetener or address her real concern. (delay + new information)

Partial Successes in Cozy Games

Partial successes (typically 5-6 on outcome rolls) work perfectly for cozy play. They deliver success with complication, creating interesting choices without harsh consequences.

Partial success patterns:

  • Success with cost: You get what you want but spend more resources
  • Success with obligation: You succeed but create future commitment
  • Success with complication: You solve this problem but create another
  • Success with revelation: You succeed but learn something concerning
  • Limited success: You get part of what you want

Example partial successes (harvest festival planning):

  • Roll to organize volunteers: Partial success. Everyone agrees to help, but the wealthier families expect recognition in the program. Do you honor that or risk offense?
  • Roll to secure musicians: Partial success. The traveling band will perform, but they need lodging and meals for a week. Can the community absorb the cost?
  • Roll to prepare feast: Partial success. You gather enough food, but the centerpiece dish requires an ingredient you don’t have. Do you adapt or send someone on a side quest?

GM usage:

  • Treat partials as “yes, but” rather than “yes, and”
  • Make complications interesting, not punishing
  • Give players choice in how they address complications
  • Use complications to create new story hooks
  • Let some complications resolve naturally over time

Embracing Failure as Story

Cozy campaigns can embrace failure as story fuel. Things going wrong creates memorable moments, character development, and community bonding.

Failure as story techniques:

  • Comedic failure: Disaster played for humor, not trauma
  • Learning moment: Failure teaches skill or humility
  • Bonding opportunity: Shared struggle brings people together
  • Pivot point: Failure opens new, better path
  • Character moment: How PCs handle failure reveals character

Example failure as story:

The PCs try to bake a cake for the elder’s birthday. They fail spectacularly: the cake collapses, the kitchen is covered in flour, and they’re running out of time. Instead of tragedy, this becomes comedy: they improvise a “deconstructed cake” dessert, the elder finds it hilarious, and the community remembers this as the year of “the disaster cake.” Future baking attempts become running gags.

GM usage:

  • Signal when failure will be comedic vs serious
  • Give PCs room to roleplay response to failure
  • Let community NPCs react with support, not judgment
  • Use failures to create inside jokes and callbacks
  • Make spectacular failures more memorable than successes

Social and Exploration Emphasis

Cozy campaigns shift mechanical focus from combat to social and exploration challenges. Rebalance difficulty, rewards, and progression to support this focus.

Rebalancing Difficulty

If combat is rare, social and exploration challenges need to carry equivalent weight. Make them mechanically interesting and meaningfully consequential.

Rebalancing techniques:

  • Increase social challenge complexity (opposed rolls, multi-party negotiations)
  • Make exploration reveal information and resources, not just terrain
  • Use success tiers to add texture to social outcomes
  • Create social “set pieces” equivalent to combat encounters
  • Reward social and exploration creativity mechanically

Example social set piece (mediation):

The feuding baker and miller (from earlier) require structured negotiation. Each has a position clock (stubbornness) and an opening clock (willingness to compromise). The PCs make social rolls to advance opening clocks while preventing position clocks from filling. Success means resolution; failure means escalation. Complications might require side quests or creative solutions.

Mechanical structure:

  • Baker position clock: 4 segments (fills if he refuses compromise)
  • Baker opening clock: 6 segments (fills when he’s willing to negotiate)
  • Miller position clock: 4 segments
  • Miller opening clock: 6 segments
  • PC actions: Social rolls to advance opening or reduce position
  • Complications: Each failed roll advances one position clock
  • Resolution: Both opening clocks fill before either position clock

Exploration Rewards

Make exploration inherently rewarding. Discovery provides community benefits, personal satisfaction, and story hooks.

Exploration reward types:

  • Resources: Find useful materials or food sources
  • Knowledge: Learn history, geography, or natural patterns
  • Relationships: Meet new NPCs or communities
  • Beauty: Experience aesthetic wonder (valid reward in cozy games)
  • Secrets: Discover hidden places or forgotten knowledge

Example exploration reward:

The PCs explore the old forest to gather mushrooms. They succeed and find enough mushrooms for the week. As a bonus (or on a critical success), they discover a grove of ancient trees with unusual properties. The trees produce edible bark with medicinal qualities. This discovery:

  • Provides new resource for community
  • Creates trade opportunity with distant communities
  • Raises questions about who planted the trees
  • Offers quiet, beautiful location for future scenes
  • Suggests deeper mysteries if PCs want to investigate

GM usage:

  • Reward exploration with tangible and aesthetic benefits
  • Let discoveries create new options and story hooks
  • Make exploration feel productive, not risky
  • Use exploration downtime to develop character
  • Balance guided and open exploration

Social Progression

In combat-focused games, PCs progress by defeating threats. In cozy games, PCs progress through relationships, reputation, and community standing.

Social progression mechanics:

  • Reputation tracks by faction or community
  • Relationship bonds that unlock new abilities or knowledge
  • Community roles that provide authority and responsibility
  • Social conditions that represent standing (Respected, Beloved, Trusted Expert)
  • Favor banks that track obligations and debts

Example social progression:

A PC focuses on cooking and hospitality. Over time, they:

  • Gain reputation as skilled cook (mechanical bonus to cooking rolls)
  • Develop bonds with NPCs who share recipes and ingredients
  • Take on role of feast coordinator (authority to make decisions)
  • Earn condition Beloved Host (advantage on hospitality social rolls)
  • Build favor bank (many NPCs owe them meals or help)

These social advancements create mechanical benefits and story opportunities equivalent to combat-focused progression.

Celebration and Ritual

Cozy campaigns thrive on celebration. Festivals, meals, and rituals provide structure, emotional peaks, and community bonding. They’re both content and reward.

Festivals and Events

Festivals mark time, create stakes, and showcase community. They’re natural story arcs: preparation, execution, and aftermath.

Festival structure:

  1. Announcement: Event is coming (2-4 sessions away)
  2. Preparation: Organize logistics, assign roles, gather resources
  3. Complication: Something threatens or challenges the event
  4. Execution: The event happens; PCs participate and adapt
  5. Aftermath: Celebrate success, address consequences, set up next arc

Festival types:

  • Seasonal: Harvest, planting, solstice, equinox
  • Commemorative: Founding day, historical event, memorial
  • Lifecycle: Wedding, coming-of-age, funeral, birth celebration
  • Competition: Cook-off, crafts fair, athletic games, talent show
  • Religious/spiritual: Blessing, pilgrimage, holy day, ritual

GM usage:

  • Plan one major festival per 4-6 sessions
  • Use festivals to introduce challenges naturally
  • Let PCs take leading roles in preparation and execution
  • Make festivals showcase PC skills and relationships
  • Use festival outcomes to shift campaign direction

Communal Meals

Meals are powerful cozy play tools. They gather characters, facilitate conversation, and mark important moments. Treat them as scenes, not transitions.

Meal scene structure:

  1. Preparation: Who cooks? What do they make? (opportunity for rolls)
  2. Gathering: Who attends? What’s the mood?
  3. Sharing: The meal itself; spotlight rotates for conversation
  4. Purpose: Information sharing, relationship development, or planning
  5. Ending: How does the meal conclude? What’s next?

Meal types:

  • Daily routine: Tavern breakfast, communal dinner
  • Celebration: Festival feast, wedding banquet, birthday dinner
  • Negotiation: Business meal, diplomatic dinner
  • Comfort: Meal after crisis, grief support, welcome-home dinner
  • Impromptu: Picnic, field meal, travel rest

Example meal scene:

The community gathers for a harvest feast. The PCs helped prepare and are guests of honor. During the meal:

  • The elder toasts the PCs and shares a story about past harvests
  • The merchant privately mentions trade opportunities
  • The blacksmith’s child shows the PCs a drawing of them
  • Someone starts singing, and the table joins in
  • The innkeeper offers free lodging for the winter

The scene accomplishes: recognition, information sharing, relationship development, emotional satisfaction, and arc setup.

GM usage:

  • Use meals to deliver information naturally
  • Let food quality and presentation matter mechanically
  • Make shared meals create or strengthen bonds
  • Use meal scenes for pacing relief
  • Spotlight different PCs through meal conversations

Seasonal Rhythms

Seasons provide natural structure for cozy campaigns. Each season brings activities, challenges, and tone shifts.

Seasonal framework:

Spring:

  • Activities: Planting, cleaning, building
  • Tone: Hopeful, energetic, new beginnings
  • Challenges: Preparation, resource allocation, planning

Summer:

  • Activities: Tending, traveling, gathering
  • Tone: Abundant, social, adventurous
  • Challenges: Maintenance, weather, competition

Autumn:

  • Activities: Harvesting, preserving, celebrating
  • Tone: Grateful, busy, reflective
  • Challenges: Time pressure, resource management, preparation

Winter:

  • Activities: Crafting, storytelling, planning
  • Tone: Cozy, intimate, patient
  • Challenges: Scarcity, isolation, endurance

GM usage:

  • Mark season changes with significant events
  • Let seasonal activities drive session content
  • Use seasonal tone to vary pacing and mood
  • Create seasonal traditions that recur each year
  • Track time through seasonal cycles rather than calendar days

Worked Example: Harvest Festival Crisis

This example demonstrates cozy conflict, project play, and community focus across a three-session arc.

Scenario Setup

The village of Millbrook holds an annual harvest festival. This year, the PCs are helping organize it. Three days before the festival, the main barn collapses in a storm. The barn stored decorations, competition supplies, and feast preparations. The community must decide: cancel the festival, scale it down, or find creative solutions.

Cozy elements:

  • Stakes: Community morale and tradition, not survival
  • Conflict: Resource scarcity and time pressure, not violence
  • Resolution: Collaboration, creativity, and adaptation

Session 1: Crisis and Planning

Frame: The morning after the storm, the PCs survey the damage. The barn is unsafe, its contents destroyed or damaged. The village gathers to decide what to do.

Initial community mood: Demoralized. Many villagers suggest canceling. The elder remains silent, leaving the decision to the community.

PC opportunity: The PCs can advocate for continuing the festival. This requires a social challenge (convince the community) or a practical challenge (present viable alternative plan).

Example player approaches:

  • Inspiring speech: Social roll to rally community spirits
  • Practical solution: Present alternative location and scaled plan
  • Emotional appeal: Invoke tradition and past festivals
  • Collaborative brainstorm: Facilitate planning session

Outcome (assuming Partial success on social rally):

The community agrees to try, but enthusiasm is low. Several key volunteers are skeptical. The PCs must deliver on their promise or lose community trust.

This introduces two clocks:

  • Festival preparation clock: 8 segments (must fill by festival day)
  • Community enthusiasm clock: 6 segments (must maintain above 3)

Planning session:

The community identifies needs:

  • Alternative venue for feast and competitions
  • Replacement decorations and supplies
  • Revised event schedule
  • Coordinated volunteer effort
  • Managing expectations (this year will be different)

The PCs assign themselves tasks and recruit help. This creates structure for the next two sessions.

Session 2: Preparation and Complications

The PCs spend this session executing their plan. Each PC focuses on one major task, creating opportunities for varied gameplay.

Example PC tasks:

PC 1: Secure alternative venue (the old mill)

  • Challenge: The mill needs cleaning and basic repairs
  • Approach: Physical work with Investigation/Craft rolls
  • Success: Mill becomes charming rustic venue
  • Partial: Mill works but lacks capacity; need outdoor backup
  • Failure: Mill unusable; must find another solution
  • Advance festival prep clock: 2 segments on success, 1 on partial

PC 2: Gather replacement decorations (forest gathering)

  • Challenge: Need natural decorations quickly
  • Approach: Exploration and Craft rolls
  • Success: Gather beautiful autumn materials
  • Partial: Materials sufficient but need community help crafting
  • Failure: Materials inadequate; must buy expensive replacements
  • Advance festival prep clock: 2 segments on success, 1 on partial

PC 3: Organize volunteers (social coordination)

  • Challenge: Convince skeptical volunteers to commit
  • Approach: Social rolls with different NPCs
  • Success: Volunteers commit enthusiastically
  • Partial: Volunteers commit but need constant encouragement
  • Failure: Volunteers agree reluctantly; risk of no-shows
  • Advance enthusiasm clock: 2 segments on success, 1 on partial

PC 4: Secure feast supplies (economic/trade challenge)

  • Challenge: Replace lost food stores
  • Approach: Negotiation and resource management
  • Success: Gather supplies within budget
  • Partial: Gather supplies but create obligations
  • Failure: Must scale down feast significantly
  • Advance festival prep clock: 2 segments on success, 1 on partial

Complication (mid-session):

The blacksmith, a key volunteer, sprains his wrist and can’t help with the competition equipment. This threatens both clocks: festival prep and community enthusiasm.

PC response options:

  • Find replacement help (social challenge)
  • Modify competitions to need less equipment (creative solution)
  • PCs take on equipment work personally (skill challenge)
  • Convince blacksmith’s apprentice to step up (mentorship moment)

Resolution depends on PC approach. Success preserves both clocks. Partial advances festival prep but reduces enthusiasm. Failure reduces enthusiasm significantly.

Session 2 ends with most tasks complete but both clocks incomplete. Festival is tomorrow.

Session 3: Festival Day

The festival arrives. The prep clock is at 6/8 segments (not perfect), and enthusiasm is at 4/6 (cautiously optimistic). The day proceeds in phases.

Morning: Setup

The PCs coordinate final preparations. This is a montage with one group check to advance the prep clock to 8/8.

Success: Everything comes together beautifully. Enthusiasm advances to 5/6. Partial: Most things work; one area is improvised. Enthusiasm stays at 4/6. Failure: Setup is rough; festival starts with visible problems. Enthusiasm drops to 3/6.

Midday: Competition and Crisis

The competitions begin (baking, crafts, children’s games). This is the dramatic high point. Introduce one final complication:

Crisis: The traveling merchant arrives with her wagon, expecting to sell at the festival. But no one budgeted space or coordinated with her. She’s a regular vendor, and refusing her damages the village’s trade reputation.

PC response:

  • Negotiate with merchant (find compromise)
  • Make space quickly (improvise solution)
  • Turn crisis into opportunity (special merchant competition category)
  • Handle social fallout (apologize and promise future inclusion)

This final challenge determines enthusiasm clock final state and merchant relationship outcome.

Afternoon: Feast and Resolution

The feast begins at the mill. The PCs have honored roles (toasts, serving, storytelling). This scene showcases success.

Feast scene beats:

  • Elder thanks community for resilience and PCs for leadership
  • Villagers share favorite moments from the day
  • Someone toasts “the festival we almost didn’t have”
  • Music and dancing (optional PC participation)
  • Quiet moment where PCs see community joy restored

Enthusiasm clock resolution:

  • 5-6 segments: Complete success; community is revitalized; PCs gain Beloved standing
  • 3-4 segments: Mixed success; festival happened but tradition strain; PCs gain Respected standing
  • 1-2 segments: Partial success; festival was awkward but meaningful; PCs must rebuild trust

Aftermath and Hooks

The arc concludes, but consequences ripple forward:

Immediate outcomes:

  • PCs gain reputation (mechanical bonus to future social rolls)
  • Villagers remember specific PC contributions
  • Community bonds strengthen or strain based on outcomes
  • The mill becomes new festival venue (environmental change)

Future hooks planted:

  • Merchant relationship (trade opportunity or obligation)
  • Barn rebuilding project (next arc possibility)
  • Villagers ask PCs to lead spring planting festival
  • Traveling performers mention festival success in distant town
  • Someone suggests making this “the year we changed” a tradition

GM usage:

This arc demonstrates:

  • Cozy stakes (tradition and morale, not survival)
  • Soft failure costs (obligation, complication, not tragedy)
  • Project structure with clocks
  • Community focus with ensemble participation
  • Celebration as content and reward
  • Layered challenges (social, practical, creative)
  • Meaningful success without violence

Adapt this structure for other cozy arcs: construction project, community crisis, seasonal preparation, or relationship-focused story.
