Epic Fantasy and Adventure Campaigns

Table of Contents

  • Epic Fantasy Fundamentals
  • Heroic Journey Structure
  • Quest Design
  • Magic Systems and Narrative
  • Epic Combat and Set Pieces
  • Mythic Threats and BBEG Design
  • Worked Example: Dragon Siege Arc
  • Cross-Links

Epic Fantasy Fundamentals

Epic fantasy campaigns emphasize heroism, grand stakes, and mythic conflicts. The PCs are exceptional individuals facing world-changing threats. The tone balances earnest heroism with real cost, spectacle with intimacy, and triumph with sacrifice.

What Makes Fantasy Epic

Epic fantasy operates at heightened scale. Stakes involve kingdoms, not villages. Threats endanger civilizations, not individuals. Heroes possess or develop extraordinary capabilities. The tone embraces the mythic while grounding it in relatable human emotion.

Epic fantasy elements:

  • Stakes: Threats to nations, worlds, or fundamental orders
  • Scale: Journeys across continents, battles of armies, ancient powers
  • Heroism: PCs are chosen, destined, or exceptional by deed
  • Magic: Present, powerful, wondrous, and costly
  • Morality: Clear good and evil, though nuanced in execution
  • Spectacle: Dramatic set pieces, legendary creatures, grand locations

Not epic elements (or use sparingly):

  • Mundane concerns: Daily survival, economic struggles
  • Ambiguous morality: Every faction equally corrupt
  • Power scarcity: Magic is rare and weak
  • Small scale: Threats stay local, victories unnoticed
  • Realism: Gritty survival mechanics, harsh consequences for heroism

Stakes and Scale

Epic fantasy stakes must feel proportional to the heroes’ power and the campaign’s scope. Start with personal stakes, escalate to communal, then regional, then world-threatening.

Stakes escalation pattern:

  1. Personal: Protect loved ones, prove worth, discover identity
  2. Communal: Save village, defend allies, preserve culture
  3. Regional: Unite kingdoms, prevent war, defeat warlord
  4. World: Stop ancient evil, prevent apocalypse, restore cosmic balance

GM usage:

  • Begin campaigns at personal scale; earn epic scale
  • Make epic stakes feel tangible through personal connections
  • Use NPCs to ground world-scale threats in human cost
  • Let players choose when to escalate from personal to epic
  • Ensure victories at each scale feel meaningful

Heroism and Tone

Epic fantasy tone celebrates heroism without becoming parody. Heroes are admirable but flawed. Victories require sacrifice. Power comes with responsibility. The world is worth saving.

Heroic tone elements:

  • Earnest emotion: Characters express hope, fear, love without irony
  • Admirable goals: PCs want to protect, save, or restore
  • Meaningful sacrifice: Victories cost something valuable
  • Hope against darkness: The world is dark but not without light
  • Legendary moments: Scenes of triumph, revelation, and spectacle

Tone techniques:

  • Take PC goals seriously; let them be heroic
  • Balance triumph with cost; victories aren’t free
  • Use NPCs to express gratitude and hope
  • Create scenes of beauty and wonder amid darkness
  • Let PCs inspire others through action and example

GM usage:

  • Signal heroic tone in Session Zero; discuss player comfort
  • Contrast darkness with light to avoid grimdark
  • Celebrate PC victories with in-world recognition
  • Make sacrifices meaningful but not crippling
  • Use downtime to showcase positive impact of heroism

Heroic Journey Structure

Epic fantasy naturally follows the heroic journey (monomyth). While not every campaign needs all stages, the structure provides pacing and dramatic arc for multi-session epics.

Journey Stages

The heroic journey has key stages. Not all campaigns use every stage, but awareness helps structure epic arcs.

Classic stages adapted for TTRPGs:

  1. Ordinary World: Establish baseline, show what’s at stake
  2. Call to Adventure: Introduce quest or threat
  3. Refusal/Hesitation: Acknowledge cost and risk
  4. Meeting Mentor: Gain guidance, training, or equipment
  5. Crossing Threshold: Leave safety, commit to quest
  6. Tests and Allies: Build party, develop skills, face challenges
  7. Approach to Crisis: Preparation for major conflict
  8. Ordeal: Central crisis where heroes face death or transformation
  9. Reward: Gain power, knowledge, or MacGuffin
  10. Road Back: Return journey with pursuit or complication
  11. Resurrection: Final test applying lessons learned
  12. Return with Boon: Deliver victory and transform home

Adapting the Journey

TTRPG heroic journeys differ from solo hero tales. The party is collective hero, player agency drives pacing, and the journey may cycle multiple times within a campaign.

TTRPG adaptations:

  • Ordinary World: Session Zero or introductory session
  • Call and Threshold: First arc establishes quest
  • Tests and Allies: Middle arcs build power and relationships
  • Ordeal and Reward: Major arc climaxes with advancement
  • Return and Resurrection: Campaign finale and epilogue

Multiple journey cycles:

  • Minor cycle: Single quest (2-4 sessions)
  • Major cycle: Campaign arc (8-12 sessions)
  • Epic cycle: Full campaign (20+ sessions)

GM usage:

  • Use journey structure for arc planning, not strict scripting
  • Let players drive pacing through choices
  • Create threshold moments where PCs commit to escalation
  • Place ordeals at natural progression points
  • Make returns celebratory; show impact on home

Threshold Moments

Thresholds are points of no return. They commit PCs to a course of action with escalating stakes. They’re dramatic moments that shift campaign tone and scale.

Threshold characteristics:

  • Voluntary: PCs choose to cross; not railroaded
  • Consequential: Crossing changes PC status or world state
  • Dramatic: Marked by significant scene or revelation
  • Escalating: Each threshold raises stakes and scope

Example thresholds:

  • Accepting the quest from the dying king (commitment)
  • Entering the forbidden forest despite warnings (departure)
  • Claiming the legendary sword (power and responsibility)
  • Allying with former enemies against greater threat (moral complexity)
  • Confronting the villain rather than fleeing (heroic choice)

GM usage:

  • Plan 2-3 major thresholds per campaign arc
  • Present thresholds as clear choices, not hidden paths
  • Allow consequences to be telegraphed, not surprising
  • Use thresholds to advance time or change tone
  • Celebrate threshold crossings with mechanical or narrative rewards

Quest Design

Epic fantasy thrives on quests: structured goals with clear objectives, obstacles, and rewards. Quests provide direction while allowing player agency in execution.

Quest Types

Epic fantasy quests follow classic patterns. Mix quest types to vary gameplay and tone.

Primary quest types:

  • Fetch: Retrieve object, person, or knowledge
  • Rescue: Save someone from danger or imprisonment
  • Destroy: Eliminate threat, break curse, close portal
  • Discover: Find location, truth, or solution
  • Protect: Defend person, place, or object from attack
  • Unite: Gather allies, forge alliances, reconcile enemies

Quest structure:

  1. Assignment: Who gives the quest and why
  2. Objective: Clear goal with success conditions
  3. Obstacles: Challenges between PCs and objective
  4. Opposition: Active forces working against PCs
  5. Reward: Mechanical and narrative payoff
  6. Consequences: What happens if PCs fail or refuse

Example quest (Fetch):

Assignment: The wizard’s apprentice asks the PCs to retrieve the Sunstone from the ancient temple. The wizard needs it to sustain the magical barrier protecting the city.

Objective: Reach the temple, navigate its trials, claim the Sunstone, return it to the wizard within seven days.

Obstacles: Monster-infested forest, temple puzzles and guardians, the Sunstone’s weight makes fast travel impossible.

Opposition: A rival faction also seeks the Sunstone to break the barrier and invade the city.

Reward: Payment, the wizard’s gratitude, and a protective charm. City remains safe.

Consequences: If PCs fail or refuse, the barrier weakens, and the city faces invasion.

Quest Chains and Branching

Single quests work for short arcs. Epic campaigns benefit from quest chains: linked quests that build toward larger goals. Allow branching for player agency.

Quest chain structure:

  • Primary quest: Overarching campaign goal (stop the dark lord)
  • Secondary quests: Steps toward primary goal (gather allies, find artifacts)
  • Tertiary quests: Steps toward secondary goals (prove worth to potential ally)
  • Side quests: Optional content with rewards but not required for main arc

Branching techniques:

  • Parallel paths: Multiple approaches to same goal
  • Optional quests: Extra challenges with bonus rewards
  • Consequence branches: Earlier choices affect later quests
  • Failure paths: Failed quests create different challenges, not dead ends

Example quest chain (stop the dark lord):

Primary: Defeat the dark lord before he opens the Shadow Gate

Secondary A: Retrieve the Sunblade (only weapon that can harm him)

  • Tertiary: Gain the temple guardian’s blessing to claim the blade
  • Tertiary: Purify the blade using sacred fire

Secondary B: Rally the divided kingdoms to march on the Shadow Gate

  • Tertiary: Prove the dark lord’s threat to skeptical kings
  • Tertiary: Mediate peace between two feuding kingdoms

Secondary C: Discover the ritual to close the Shadow Gate permanently

  • Tertiary: Find the lost grimoire in the sunken library
  • Tertiary: Convince the archmage to share forbidden knowledge

Side quest: Help refugee town escape dark lord’s forces (rewards: allies, supplies, information)

GM usage:

  • Present primary quest early; reveal secondary quests through play
  • Allow PCs to pursue quests in any order (within logic)
  • Make some quests optional; let PCs choose priorities
  • Use failed quests to create complications, not blocks
  • Track quest progress with clocks

Quest Rewards

Epic fantasy rewards should feel legendary. Balance mechanical benefits with narrative impact and social recognition.

Reward types:

  • Gear: Magic items, legendary weapons, artifacts
  • Power: New abilities, progression, special training
  • Allies: NPCs or factions who aid the PCs
  • Knowledge: Secrets, maps, or tactical information
  • Status: Titles, recognition, or authority
  • Resources: Money, territory, or magical components
  • Story: Revelations that advance plot or deepen lore

Reward pacing:

  • Minor quest: Useful gear or information
  • Major quest: Legendary item or significant power
  • Arc quest: Transform PC capabilities or world state

GM usage:

  • Match reward to quest difficulty and stakes
  • Make legendary rewards feel earned through challenge
  • Let rewards create new capabilities or story hooks
  • Use social rewards to make heroes feel heroic
  • Delay some rewards for dramatic timing (legendary sword breaks curse only after side quest)

Magic Systems and Narrative

Epic fantasy treats magic as wondrous, powerful, and narratively significant. Magic shapes the world, drives plot, and represents themes. It’s never mundane.

Magic as Wonder

Magic should inspire awe. Even common magic feels special. Legendary magic transforms reality. Make magic visually and narratively distinctive.

Wonder techniques:

  • Describe magic vividly: sensory details, environmental effects
  • Give magic personality: different casters create different effects
  • Make magic costly: energy, material, or narrative cost
  • Limit magic access: not everyone can cast; not all spells are known
  • Use magic to showcase setting: magic reflects world’s nature

Example magic descriptions:

Generic: “The wizard casts fireball. Roll damage.”

Epic: “The wizard traces a sigil of flame in the air. The symbol pulses once, twice, then explodes outward in a roaring sphere of azure fire. The heat washes over you from twenty paces away. Roll damage.”

Generic: “You heal using your paladin powers.”

Epic: “You press your hand to the wound. Golden light spills between your fingers, warm as summer sun. The torn flesh knits together, leaving only a faint silver scar. Your deity’s favor surrounds you like an embrace.”

Magic as Cost

Epic magic should require sacrifice. The most powerful effects demand something meaningful: time, materials, relationships, or personal cost.

Cost types:

  • Material: Rare ingredients consumed in casting
  • Time: Rituals require hours, days, or weeks
  • Health: Casting drains vitality or causes conditions
  • Social: Magic use has political or religious consequences
  • Moral: Powerful magic requires questionable choices
  • Risk: Failed casting creates dangers

Example costs:

  • The resurrection ritual requires a diamond worth a kingdom’s ransom and three days of uninterrupted casting
  • The scrying spell reveals the target’s location but alerts them to being watched
  • The protective ward lasts one month, then the caster gains condition Exhausted until next full rest
  • The battle magic wins the fight but causes collateral damage to civilians
  • The forbidden summoning grants power but draws the attention of planar entities

GM usage:

  • Scale cost to power: world-changing magic demands world-changing cost
  • Let players choose costs when options exist
  • Make costs narratively interesting, not just mechanical
  • Use costs to create story hooks and consequences
  • Respect magic’s power through meaningful limitations

Magic and Theme

Magic reinforces campaign themes. Design magical systems, items, and creatures to resonate with core ideas.

Theme-magic connections:

  • Theme of sacrifice: Magic requires personal cost or loss
  • Theme of corruption: Powerful magic tempts or transforms
  • Theme of connection: Magic links people, places, or times
  • Theme of knowledge: Magic comes from learning and understanding
  • Theme of nature: Magic flows from environment and cycles

Example (corruption theme):

The campaign explores power’s corrupting influence. Magic reflects this:

  • Powerful spells leave visible marks on casters (eyes change color, skin develops runes)
  • Artifacts offer great power but whisper temptations
  • The dark lord was once a hero who embraced forbidden magic
  • NPCs who use too much magic become withdrawn and paranoid
  • The most powerful PC spell option comes with corruption clock

GM usage:

  • Choose 1-2 campaign themes in Session Zero
  • Design 2-3 magic elements that reinforce themes
  • Use magic consequences to explore themes
  • Let PCs choose how to engage with thematic magic
  • Make theme-magic connections explicit in descriptions

Epic Combat and Set Pieces

Epic fantasy combat should feel legendary: large scale, high stakes, dynamic terrain, and heroic moments. Combat is spectacle and story beat, not just mechanics.

Epic Combat Elements

Epic combat differs from standard encounters through scale, complexity, and narrative weight.

Epic combat characteristics:

  • Scale: Large numbers of combatants or massive creatures
  • Terrain: Dynamic, hazardous, or fantastical battlefields
  • Stakes: Outcomes affect story arc, not just immediate survival
  • Phases: Combat evolves through stages with changing conditions
  • Cinematic moments: Opportunities for spectacular actions
  • Narrative integration: Combat advances plot or reveals character

Combat vs Encounter:

Standard encounter: PCs fight bandits on a road.

Epic encounter: PCs defend the city gate against an invading army while the wizard completes a ritual to raise protective wards. The gate will fall in 10 rounds. Enemy siege engines bombard the walls. Civilians flee through the combat zone. Victory requires holding the gate, not killing every enemy.

Set Piece Design

Set pieces are planned epic encounters with multiple elements, phases, and dramatic beats. They’re the climactic battles that define arcs.

Set piece components:

  1. Location: Distinctive battlefield with tactical features
  2. Objectives: Goals beyond “defeat all enemies”
  3. Phases: Combat evolves as conditions change
  4. Hazards: Environmental dangers and opportunities
  5. Secondary threats: Multiple challenges requiring coordination
  6. Cinematic moments: Opportunities for heroic actions
  7. Stakes clock: Pressure timer representing consequences

Set piece structure:

  • Phase 1: Initial engagement, establish stakes and terrain
  • Phase 2: Complication (reinforcements, hazard, revelation)
  • Phase 3: Escalation (boss appears, terrain changes, stakes increase)
  • Phase 4: Resolution (final push, objective achieved or failed)

Example set piece (bridge defense):

Location: Ancient stone bridge over a chasm. Bridge is 100 feet long, 20 feet wide. Crumbling edges, central keystone, defensive barriers.

Objectives:

  • Primary: Hold the bridge for 12 rounds (let allies evacuate)
  • Secondary: Prevent enemies from destroying the keystone
  • Tertiary: Evacuate wounded soldiers

Phases:

  • Phase 1 (rounds 1-4): Orc warband charges bridge; standard combat
  • Phase 2 (rounds 5-8): Orc shaman begins ritual to shatter keystone; PCs must interrupt or counter
  • Phase 3 (rounds 9-12): Troll emerges from chasm; terrain begins crumbling; final desperate stand

Hazards:

  • Crumbling edges: Move carelessly, make Agility check or fall
  • Keystone destruction: If ritual completes, bridge collapses in 3 rounds
  • Chasm: Falling means death unless rescued

Stakes clock: Evacuation (8 segments, advances each round; must fill to save allies)

GM usage:

  • Design 2-3 set pieces per campaign arc
  • Telegraph set pieces one session ahead
  • Make objectives clear before combat starts
  • Use phases to create drama and pacing
  • Allow creative solutions beyond “fight harder”
  • End with narrative consequences, not just XP

Cinematic Combat Actions

Epic combat rewards spectacular actions. Encourage players to attempt legendary feats beyond standard attacks.

Cinematic action types:

  • Environmental: Use terrain dramatically (chandelier swing, avalanche trigger)
  • Heroic: Protect allies through sacrifice or bold positioning
  • Tactical: Execute complex multi-character maneuvers
  • Narrative: Actions that advance story (speech during combat, claiming artifact)
  • Spectacular: Physics-defying or magical displays

Encouraging cinematic actions:

  • Describe environment with tactical detail
  • Reward bold actions with advantage or reduced difficulty
  • Allow Rule of Cool for genre-appropriate stunts
  • Use Success with Consequence for risky attempts
  • Make cinematic actions advance stakes or clocks

Example cinematic actions:

Standard: “I attack the troll.”

Cinematic: “I run toward the troll, slide between its legs while slashing its tendons, then vault onto its back using my momentum.”

Standard: “I cast shield on myself.”

Cinematic: “I see the dragon inhale. I leap in front of the wizard and raise my shield, channeling every ounce of divine power into a barrier. The dragon fire hits me first.”

GM usage:

  • Ask “How do you do it?” to prompt cinematic description
  • Reward creative action mechanically when appropriate
  • Use cinematic actions as inspiration for complications
  • Let players narrate success when they earn it
  • Make combat memorable through action, not just math

Mythic Threats and BBEG Design

Epic fantasy needs epic villains. The Big Bad Evil Guy (BBEG) drives the campaign arc, opposes the heroes, and represents what’s at stake. Design them to be memorable, threatening, and worth defeating.

Threat Tiers

Epic campaigns escalate through threat tiers. Early threats introduce danger. Mid-campaign threats challenge heroes. Final threats endanger the world.

Threat tier structure:

Tier 1 (Local): Threatens community or region

  • Scale: Bandit lord, local monster, corrupt official
  • Arc length: 2-4 sessions
  • Resolution: Defeat or drive off threat

Tier 2 (Regional): Threatens kingdom or multiple communities

  • Scale: Warlord, dragon, powerful cultist
  • Arc length: 6-10 sessions
  • Resolution: Major battle or quest completion

Tier 3 (World): Threatens civilization or reality

  • Scale: Dark lord, ancient evil, planar entity
  • Arc length: 12-20 sessions
  • Resolution: Epic confrontation, legendary quest

Campaign threat progression:

  • Open with Tier 1 threat (establish tone and stakes)
  • Introduce Tier 2 threat as Tier 1 resolves (escalation)
  • Reveal Tier 3 threat behind Tier 2 (the real enemy)
  • Resolve Tier 3 threat as campaign finale

GM usage:

  • Don’t start with world-ending stakes; earn them
  • Connect lower-tier threats to higher-tier threats
  • Let early victories matter even as stakes escalate
  • Use threat tiers to pace power progression
  • Make each tier defeat feel complete before escalating

BBEG Design

The BBEG is the campaign’s primary antagonist. They should be memorable, motivated, and mechanically challenging. Design them in layers: identity, motivation, methods, and minions.

BBEG design components:

Identity:

  • Who are they? (background, power source, appearance)
  • What makes them distinctive? (personality, abilities, aesthetic)
  • Why are they formidable? (power, resources, intelligence)

Motivation:

  • What do they want? (goal)
  • Why do they want it? (motivation)
  • What will they sacrifice for it? (commitment)

Methods:

  • How do they pursue their goal? (strategy)
  • What resources do they command? (minions, magic, territory)
  • What are their strengths and weaknesses? (tactical profile)

Minions and Structure:

  • Who serves them? (lieutenants, armies, creatures)
  • Why do others follow? (fear, belief, payment)
  • How is their organization structured? (hierarchy, specialization)

Example BBEG (Tier 3):

Identity: Lord Malachar, the Ashen King. Once a noble wizard-prince who sought to prevent catastrophe. Now an undead sorcerer-lord clad in blackened armor, crowned with iron thorns. Commands necrotic magic that withers life.

Motivation: Believes the world’s cycle of suffering must end. Plans to open the Shadow Gate and merge the material world with the Shadowfell, creating a realm without pain, emotion, or life. Genuinely believes this is mercy.

Methods: Conquered three kingdoms using undead armies. Establishing ritual sites to weaken the barrier between worlds. Recruiting desperate people with promises of peace. Uses spies and infiltrators to sabotage resistance.

Minions:

  • The Pale Hand: Elite undead knights, his most trusted lieutenants
  • Cultists: Living followers who believe in his vision
  • Shadow mages: Wizards corrupted by Shadowfell magic
  • Undead armies: Endless troops raised from conquered lands
  • Double agents: Placed within resistance organizations

Weaknesses:

  • The Sunblade can permanently destroy his undead form
  • His ritual requires seven sites; disrupting them delays his plan
  • His humanity still exists buried deep; appeals to past may reach him
  • Overconfident; believes himself inevitable
  • Underestimates power of hope and living alliances

Reveals and Escalation

Epic BBEGs benefit from gradual revelation. Start with minions and rumors, escalate to confrontations, build to final battle.

Revelation structure:

  1. Rumors and signs: PCs hear about threat (sessions 1-2)
  2. Minion encounters: PCs fight BBEG’s forces (sessions 3-6)
  3. Lieutenant confrontations: PCs battle BBEG’s champions (sessions 7-10)
  4. Direct encounter: PCs meet BBEG but can’t win yet (session 11-12)
  5. Preparation: PCs gather power to match BBEG (sessions 13-16)
  6. Final confrontation: PCs challenge BBEG at full power (sessions 17-20)

Escalation techniques:

  • Each victory reveals more about BBEG
  • BBEG responds to PC actions (countermoves)
  • BBEG’s plan progresses on countdown clocks
  • Personal stakes develop (BBEG threatens PC bonds)
  • Moral complexity emerges (BBEG’s motivation revealed)

GM usage:

  • Introduce BBEG early through reputation and minions
  • Let PCs face BBEG before final battle (establish threat)
  • Use countdown clocks to show BBEG’s progress
  • Make BBEG’s motivation understandable if not sympathetic
  • Give BBEG memorable traits (voice, appearance, phrases)
  • Plan BBEG’s response to major PC victories

Countdown and Fronts

BBEGs pursue active plans on timelines. Use countdown clocks and fronts to represent BBEG progress and create urgency.

Countdown structure:

  • 8-12 segment clock representing BBEG’s ultimate goal
  • Advance clock on session milestones or when PCs don’t interfere
  • Clock completion triggers campaign crisis (gate opens, ritual completes, army marches)
  • PCs can delay or reverse clock through action

Example countdown (Shadow Gate opening):

  • 0 segments: BBEG begins plan; PCs unaware
  • 3 segments: First ritual site established; local area shows effects
  • 6 segments: Three sites active; regional darkness spreading
  • 9 segments: Six sites active; barrier weakening; undead surge
  • 12 segments: All seven sites active; gate opens; campaign crisis

Front structure (parallel threats):

Primary front: BBEG’s ritual (countdown clock)

Secondary fronts:

  • Military: Undead armies conquer territories (region control clock)
  • Infiltration: Spies undermine resistance (sabotage clock)
  • Recruitment: Cult grows in desperate areas (follower clock)

GM usage:

  • Show clock progress to create urgency
  • Let PC victories reverse or delay clocks
  • Use secondary fronts to create varied challenges
  • Make clock states visible in world (darkness spreads, undead increase)
  • Allow campaign to continue if main clock completes (crisis, not failure)

Worked Example: Dragon Siege Arc

This example demonstrates epic fantasy structure, quest design, and set piece combat in a 4-session arc.

Arc Setup

The ancient red dragon Skaravex has awakened after centuries of slumber. She’s claiming the mountain kingdom of Ironpeak as her territory. The king has called for heroes to drive her off or slay her. The PCs answer the call, joining other adventurers seeking glory and reward.

Epic elements:

  • Scale: Kingdom-level threat, legendary creature
  • Stakes: Thousands of lives, territorial sovereignty
  • Heroism: PCs must prove themselves worthy of dragon-slaying
  • Quest structure: Clear objective with multiple approaches

Arc structure:

  • Session 1: Arrival and investigation
  • Session 2: Preparation and first confrontation
  • Session 3: Quest for advantage
  • Session 4: Final battle

Session 1: Arrival and Investigation

Frame: The PCs arrive at Ironpeak’s capital city. Refugees flood the streets. The dragon has destroyed three mining villages and established a lair in the mountain. The king summons the PCs to the war council.

War council scene:

Key NPCs present:

  • King Tormund: Desperate but resolute; wants to protect his people
  • General Kara: Pragmatic military leader; believes fighting is suicide
  • Archmage Venn: Older wizard; knows dragon lore and weaknesses
  • Captain Rodric: Young officer; survived dragon attack; traumatized
  • Other adventurers: Rival party seeking same glory

Information revealed:

  • Skaravex is ancient and enormously powerful
  • She’s establishing lair in old dwarven fortress in the mountain
  • She attacks anyone approaching her territory
  • Traditional weapons barely scratch her scales
  • The kingdom lacks resources for prolonged defense
  • Refugees will starve if siege continues

The king presents options:

  • Direct assault: March army to dragon’s lair (likely suicide)
  • Siege: Wait and prepare defenses (civilians suffer)
  • Negotiation: Offer tribute to leave (kingdom impoverished)
  • Champions: Send heroes to slay dragon (risky but possible)

PC choice: The PCs volunteer as champions. The king accepts but requires proof they can survive the dragon. First test: Rescue survivors from a recent attack and gather intelligence.

Investigation and rescue mission:

The PCs travel to the most recently destroyed village. This is a horror-tinged investigation scene showing dragon devastation.

Clues discovered:

  • Core clue 1: The dragon is collecting metal, especially gold and iron
  • Core clue 2: Dragon attacked when miners breached dwarven vault
  • Core clue 3: Survivors report the dragon spoke, demanding “what was stolen”

Rescue challenge: Survivors trapped in collapsed mine. PCs must navigate unstable tunnels, avoid toxic gas, and rescue civilians before dragon returns.

Session ends: PCs return with survivors and information. Archmage Venn theorizes: The dragon is seeking something specific from the dwarven vault. This provides an advantage opportunity.

Session 2: Preparation and First Confrontation

The PCs plan their approach. The archmage offers knowledge:

Dragon weaknesses:

  • Ancient dragons resist most magic but are vulnerable to legendary weapons
  • Red dragons are arrogant and can be goaded into mistakes
  • Skaravex hasn’t flown in the open yet (possibly injured or protecting something)
  • Dragons can be bargained with if you have leverage

Preparation options:

The PCs choose how to prepare (multiple sessions possible; example uses one):

Option A: Seek legendary weapon (the Sunblade)

  • Quest: Travel to temple, prove worth, claim weapon
  • Benefit: Weapon can pierce dragon scales
  • Cost: 2 sessions, dangerous quest

Option B: Discover what dragon seeks (investigate dwarven vault)

  • Quest: Enter dangerous vault, find what dragon wants
  • Benefit: Leverage for negotiation or trap opportunity
  • Cost: 1 session, unknown dangers

Option C: Rally allies (gather forces and prepare battlefield)

  • Quest: Convince neighboring kingdoms to send aid
  • Benefit: Army support, better tactical position
  • Cost: 1 session, diplomatic challenge

The PCs choose Option B (investigate vault).

Vault exploration:

The PCs enter the dwarven vault beneath the destroyed village. It’s a dungeon exploration with puzzles, traps, and ancient guardians.

Key discoveries:

  • The vault contained the “Crown of the Mountain,” an artifact
  • Legend says the crown grants mastery over stone and metal
  • The dragon didn’t steal the crown; miners did before dragon attacked
  • The crown is now in the capital, in the royal treasury
  • The dragon will destroy the capital to reclaim it

First confrontation:

As PCs exit the vault, Skaravex arrives. This is a narrative encounter, not full combat. The PCs cannot win but can impress or negotiate.

Dragon encounter structure:

  • Round 1: Dragon lands, demands crown, gives PCs chance to speak
  • Round 2: PCs respond (fight, negotiate, or flee)
  • Round 3: Dragon reaction (respect, anger, or dismissal)
  • Round 4: Dragon leaves (if impressed) or attacks (if angered)

Example outcome (negotiation):

PCs: “We know you seek the crown. The miners stole it, not the king. We can return it if you spare the kingdom.”

Dragon: “You dare bargain with Skaravex the Immortal? You are bold, mortals. Bring me the crown before three suns set, or I will burn your stone halls to ash.” (Flies away)

This creates countdown: 3 days (sessions) to decide and act.

Session 3: Crown Dilemma and Quest

The PCs return with information and the dragon’s ultimatum. This creates a complex choice:

Options:

  1. Return crown to dragon (spares kingdom, but loses powerful artifact)
  2. Keep crown and prepare for battle (kingdom at risk, but retain power)
  3. Use crown as trap (lure dragon into ambush with crown as bait)
  4. Seek compromise (offer tribute plus help finding other artifacts)

Each option has mechanical and narrative consequences. The PCs debate and choose.

Example choice: Use crown as trap.

The PCs propose a plan: Claim they’ll return the crown in a ceremony at a prepared battlefield. Use the crown to lure Skaravex into terrain favorable to the defenders. Spring the trap during negotiation.

Preparation montage:

The PCs coordinate defenses with General Kara. This is a montage covering:

  • Terrain modification: Prepare battlefield with ballista, barriers, and magic circles
  • Force positioning: Place soldiers, mages, and artillery
  • Crown preparation: Archmage Venn creates crown-based trap (magical binding)
  • Contingencies: Evacuation plans if fight goes badly

This creates tactical advantages for the final battle but doesn’t guarantee victory.

Moral complication:

Captain Rodric privately tells PCs his fear: “If this fails, you’ve just enraged the dragon with betrayal. She’ll massacre the entire kingdom for the insult. Are you certain you can win?”

This reinforces stakes and lets PCs reconsider. Their commitment to the plan defines their heroism.

Session 4: Epic Battle

The day arrives. The dragon approaches the prepared battlefield (a valley with defensible positions). The PCs, royal forces, and the archmage are ready.

Set piece structure:

Location: Stone valley with raised platforms, defensive walls, ballista positions, central plaza with crown (trap bait).

Objectives:

  • Primary: Defeat or drive off Skaravex
  • Secondary: Protect the archmage while he activates crown trap
  • Tertiary: Minimize allied casualties

Phases:

Phase 1 - Negotiation and Trap (rounds 1-3):

  • Skaravex lands in plaza, approaches crown
  • PCs attempt negotiation (stall while archmage prepares)
  • Archmage triggers crown trap (binding spell)
  • Trap partially succeeds: Dragon is weakened but not bound
  • Dragon roars betrayal; combat begins

Phase 2 - Aerial Assault (rounds 4-7):

  • Dragon takes flight, enraged
  • Breathes fire across battlefield (area damage)
  • Ballistas fire (clock: 6 segments to ground dragon)
  • PCs must coordinate attacks while protecting allies
  • Dragon destroys one ballista position

Phase 3 - Grounded Combat (rounds 8-11):

  • Ballista clock fills; dragon crashes to ground
  • Full combat: PCs vs wounded but deadly dragon
  • Dragon uses tail, claws, and bite; area hazards
  • Crown trap pulses: dragon takes damage each round (time pressure on dragon)
  • Allied soldiers harass dragon, create openings for PCs

Phase 4 - Desperation or Victory (rounds 12+):

  • If PCs are winning: Dragon attempts to flee; final push to prevent escape
  • If PCs are losing: Dragon offers parley; negotiate from weakness
  • Resolution: Dragon defeated, driven off, or bargain struck

Example resolution (victory):

The PCs focus fire on the dragon’s wounded wing. A PC makes heroic attack (cinematic: leaps from ballista platform onto dragon’s back, drives legendary sword into weak point). Dragon roars, thrashes, collapses.

Final choice: Kill or spare?

  • Kill: Dragon slain; kingdom safe; PCs are legendary heroes; but dragon’s knowledge is lost
  • Spare: Dragon swears oath to leave and never return; PCs gain ancient ally; but kingdom fears dragon’s return

PCs choose spare. Dragon acknowledges their strength: “You have proven yourselves worthy. The crown is yours. I shall seek my treasures elsewhere. We are not enemies, champions. Remember this when darkness comes.”

Dragon flies away, wounded but alive.

Aftermath

Victory celebration:

  • King Tormund throws feast in PCs’ honor
  • PCs are granted titles and lands
  • The Crown of the Mountain is entrusted to them (legendary item)
  • General Kara offers them military commissions
  • Archmage Venn invites them to study ancient magic
  • Citizens cheer them as dragon-slayers

Long-term consequences:

  • The kingdom recovers; refugees return home
  • PCs’ reputation spreads (advantage on social rolls in region)
  • The dragon’s warning about “darkness” foreshadows next arc
  • Rival adventuring party is jealous; potential future conflict
  • Dwarven descendants arrive, seeking to reclaim their vault and crown

GM usage:

This arc demonstrates:

  • Epic stakes (kingdom survival) rooted in personal choice
  • Quest structure with multiple approaches
  • Moral complexity (betray dragon, spare or kill)
  • Set piece combat with phases and objectives
  • Cinematic moments and heroic choices
  • Meaningful consequences and recognition
  • Hook for future arc (dragon’s warning)

Adapt this structure for other epic quests: siege defense, ancient evil awakening, or planar threat.

Campaign Arc Planning

Table of Contents

  • Arc Planning Fundamentals
  • Arc Outline Template
  • Session Breakdown
  • Milestone Tracking
  • Arc Clocks and Timelines
  • Pivot Points and Player Agency
  • Example Arc Outlines
  • Cross-Links

Arc Planning Fundamentals

Campaign arcs are multi-session story structures with setup, development, climax, and resolution. Good arc planning provides direction without scripting outcomes. The plan is a map, not a railroad. It guides preparation while allowing player agency to shape the journey.

What Is a Campaign Arc

A campaign arc is a narrative unit spanning 3-8 sessions with a central conflict or question. It has a beginning (introduction of stakes), middle (development and complications), and end (climax and resolution). Arcs connect to form campaigns.

Arc characteristics:

  • Central conflict: One primary challenge or question
  • Clear stakes: What’s at risk and why it matters
  • Rising tension: Escalating pressure and complications
  • Player-driven pacing: PCs choose when and how to engage
  • Meaningful resolution: Outcomes change world or character state

Arc vs session vs campaign:

  • Session: Single play session (3-4 hours); completes 2-5 scenes
  • Arc: Story unit spanning 3-8 sessions; resolves central conflict
  • Campaign: Collection of connected arcs spanning 10-50+ sessions

Arc Scope and Length

Arc length depends on complexity, player engagement, and campaign pacing. Match scope to available time and player interest.

Arc length guidelines:

  • Short arc (3-4 sessions): Single clear objective; limited complications
  • Standard arc (5-6 sessions): Central conflict with 2-3 subplots
  • Long arc (7-8 sessions): Complex conflict with multiple approaches and deep development
  • Epic arc (9+ sessions): Campaign-defining conflict requiring extensive preparation

Factors affecting arc length:

  • Session frequency: Weekly games support longer arcs; monthly games need shorter arcs
  • Player engagement: Engaged players sustain longer arcs; new players need shorter arcs
  • Complexity: Simple conflicts resolve faster; layered conflicts need development
  • Pacing preference: Some tables prefer quick resolution; others enjoy slow burn

GM usage:

  • Start with 4-5 session arcs until you learn table pace
  • Ask players at arc end: “Was this too long, too short, or just right?”
  • Adjust future arcs based on feedback and observed engagement
  • Build in flexible sessions that can compress or expand arc

Flexible Planning

Arc plans are guides, not scripts. Plan structure, not outcomes. Prepare situations, not solutions. Build pivot points where player choices redirect the arc.

Flexible planning principles:

  • Plan inciting incidents, not resolutions: Know how arc starts, not how it ends
  • Prepare locations and NPCs, not plot beats: Create resources you can deploy flexibly
  • Use clocks for progress: Track state changes, not predetermined events
  • Build modular scenes: Prepare scenes that work in multiple orders
  • Expect player solutions: Don’t plan one path; prepare to improvise around choices

Example rigid planning (avoid):

“Session 1: PCs investigate murder. Session 2: They discover butler did it. Session 3: They confront butler in mansion. Session 4: Butler escapes to warehouse. Session 5: Final battle at warehouse.”

This script assumes player actions at every step. It breaks when players act differently.

Example flexible planning (use):

“Arc setup: Lord murdered. Suspects: butler, business rival, estranged son, secret lover. Evidence distributed across locations. Killer is whoever players pursue last (adjust guilt clues accordingly). Climax when players confront chosen suspect with evidence. Resolution depends on confrontation approach.”

This prepares situations and adapts to player investigation choices.

Arc Themes

Arc themes give focus and meaning. They’re the questions or ideas the arc explores through play. Theme emerges from central conflict and character engagement.

Theme types:

  • Moral: What’s the right choice? (duty vs mercy, justice vs vengeance)
  • Identity: Who are you? (heritage, transformation, purpose)
  • Social: How do we coexist? (community, prejudice, cooperation)
  • Existential: What matters? (legacy, mortality, meaning)
  • Genre: What defines this genre? (heroism in fantasy, paranoia in horror)

GM usage:

  • Choose one primary theme per arc
  • Introduce theme early through inciting incident
  • Reinforce theme through complications and NPC perspectives
  • Let players explore theme through choices
  • Resolve arc by answering or deepening thematic question

Example theme application (duty vs mercy):

Arc: The PCs must stop a rebellion leader who attacks corrupt nobles.

Theme exploration:

  • Setup: Rebellion leader’s cause is just, but methods are violent
  • Development: PCs learn nobles exploit peasants; rebellion has civilian support
  • Complications: Rebellion escalates; innocents caught in crossfire
  • Climax: PCs choose: uphold law (duty) or help rebellion (mercy)
  • Resolution: Choice consequences ripple through region

Arc Outline Template

Use this template to plan campaign arcs. Fill sections with enough detail to guide preparation but leave room for improvisation.

Template Structure

Arc Title: [Short, evocative name]

Arc Length: [3-8 sessions]

Central Conflict: [One-sentence description of primary challenge]

Stakes: [What’s at risk; why players care]

Theme: [Optional; one-sentence thematic question]

Setup (Session 1):

  • Inciting incident: [Event that starts arc]
  • Initial hooks: [Why PCs engage; personal and practical]
  • Key NPCs introduced: [List with brief notes]
  • World state: [Current situation before arc begins]

Development (Sessions 2-4):

  • Rising action: [List 2-4 complications or reveals]
  • Subplot hooks: [Optional parallel storylines]
  • Locations: [Key places PCs will explore]
  • Player choices: [Anticipated decision points]

Climax (Session 5-6):

  • Trigger: [What brings conflict to a head]
  • Climax options: [Multiple approaches PCs might take]
  • Stakes restatement: [Remind what’s at risk]

Resolution (Session 6-7):

  • Consequence tracks: [How different outcomes affect world]
  • Character impacts: [How arc affects PC development]
  • Next arc hooks: [Threads leading to future arcs]

Preparation Checklist:

Completed Template Example

Arc Title: The Poisoned Well

Arc Length: 5 sessions

Central Conflict: A deadly plague spreads from a village’s water supply; PCs must find the source and cure before it reaches the city.

Stakes: Hundreds will die; city will quarantine or destroy village; PCs have allies in the village.

Theme: Who bears responsibility for collective harm? (negligence vs malice vs nature)

Setup (Session 1):

  • Inciting incident: PCs arrive at village for festival; people are collapsing with black veins and fever
  • Initial hooks: PC’s mentor lives here (personal); city offers reward for solving plague (practical); healers are overwhelmed (moral)
  • Key NPCs: Elder Mara (village leader, calm crisis manager), Healer Bren (exhausted, needs help), City Envoy Kael (cold, favors quarantine)
  • World state: Village is prosperous trade hub; recent drought made well crucial; city fears contagion

Development (Sessions 2-4):

  • Rising action:
    1. Investigation reveals plague comes from the well itself
    2. Well leads to ancient underground chamber (forgotten ruin)
    3. Ruin contains alchemical laboratory; plague is escaped experiment
    4. Discover alchemist was village founder’s ancestor; family knows and hid it
  • Subplot: City envoy pushes for quarantine; PCs must buy time
  • Locations: Village (with factional tensions), the well, underground ruin (dungeon exploration), alchemist’s lab (clue-rich)
  • Player choices: How deeply to investigate? Trust village leaders? Reveal truth to city?

Climax (Session 5):

  • Trigger: PCs discover cure components in lab, but elder’s family wants to destroy evidence and blame natural causes
  • Climax options:
    • Confront elder’s family and demand cooperation
    • Steal components and create cure without permission
    • Expose truth to city and force accountability
    • Negotiate compromise (cure in exchange for secrecy)
  • Stakes: Village survival, truth vs secrecy, justice vs mercy

Resolution (Session 5-6):

  • Consequence tracks:
    • Cure created: Village saved; city gratitude; PC reputation rises
    • Truth revealed: Village faces reparations; elder’s family loses status; but justice served
    • Truth hidden: Village saved but built on lie; future risk if discovered
  • Character impacts: PCs gain medical knowledge; relationships with village NPCs evolve; city contacts
  • Next arc hooks: Alchemist’s journal mentions other labs; city politics around blame; other villages with similar wells

Preparation Checklist:

Session Breakdown

Break arcs into session-sized chunks. Each session should advance arc clocks, reveal information, or present meaningful choices.

Session Planning

Sessions within arcs have structure: opening (recap and hook), development (2-4 scenes advancing arc), and ending (cliffhanger or resolution beat).

Session structure:

Opening (15-20 minutes):

  • Recap: Summarize previous session and arc status
  • Hook: Present immediate situation or choice
  • Frame: Establish what PCs can do this session

Development (2-2.5 hours):

  • Scene 1: Investigation, social, or exploration
  • Scene 2: Complication or revelation
  • Scene 3: Choice point or action sequence
  • Optional Scene 4: Follow-up or transition

Ending (15-30 minutes):

  • Resolution: Complete immediate challenge
  • Cliffhanger: Present new problem or reveal
  • OR Downtime: If natural break, offer respite
  • Preview: Tease next session direction

Session pacing by arc position:

  • Arc opening (Session 1): Longer setup, establish NPCs and stakes
  • Arc middle (Sessions 2-4): Balanced investigation and action
  • Arc climax (Session 5-6): Shorter setup, extended climax scene
  • Arc resolution (Final session): Resolution beats, aftermath, next arc tease

Session Prep

Prepare sessions within arc context. Know current arc state, PC goals, and likely player actions.

Session prep checklist:

Before session:

  • Review arc outline and current position
  • Check clock states (progress and pressure)
  • Note PC goals from previous session
  • Prepare 2-3 likely scene types (investigation, social, combat)
  • List NPCs who might appear
  • Note key information to reveal

During session:

  • Track clock advances
  • Note player choices and new threads
  • Improvise around unexpected directions
  • Mark what information was revealed

After session:

  • Update arc outline with changes
  • Advance any off-screen clocks
  • Note hooks for next session
  • Adjust remaining arc sessions if needed

Session Goals

Each session should accomplish something. Define session goals during prep to guide improvisation.

Session goal types:

  • Information: Reveal key clue or NPC motivation
  • Progress: Advance arc clock significantly
  • Choice: Present meaningful decision with consequences
  • Development: Advance character relationships or growth
  • Action: Deliver set piece combat or challenge
  • Relief: Provide downtime or emotional reset

Example session goals (Poisoned Well arc):

Session 2 goals:

  • Information: Reveal plague source is the well itself
  • Progress: Fill 2 segments of investigation clock
  • Choice: Decide whether to dive into well or investigate village history first
  • NPC: Establish trust or suspicion of Elder Mara

If session achieves goals through unexpected means, that’s success. Goals guide, not prescribe.

Milestone Tracking

Milestones mark significant arc progress. They’re not just mechanical advancement; they’re story beats that shift campaign state.

Story Milestones

Story milestones are narrative turning points. They change what PCs know, what’s possible, or what’s at stake.

Story milestone types:

  • Revelation: PCs learn crucial truth
  • Threshold: PCs commit to irreversible course
  • Victory: PCs overcome major obstacle
  • Loss: PCs fail and must adapt
  • Transformation: PC or NPC changes fundamentally
  • World change: Setting state shifts permanently

Milestone tracking:

  • Plan 3-5 story milestones per arc
  • Place at key narrative beats (usually end of sessions)
  • Make milestones visible to players (explicit or implied)
  • Let player choices determine which milestones occur

Example story milestones (Poisoned Well):

  1. Discovery: PCs learn well is poison source (revelation)
  2. Descent: PCs enter underground ruin (threshold)
  3. Truth: PCs discover village founder’s guilt (revelation)
  4. Crisis: City envoy demands immediate quarantine (loss/time pressure)
  5. Resolution: PCs cure plague and decide truth’s fate (victory/choice)

Character Milestones

Character milestones track PC growth and development. They’re moments where characters change, advance, or deepen relationships.

Character milestone types:

  • Mechanical: Level up, gain ability, claim item
  • Relational: Bond deepens, ally gained, rival declared
  • Personal: Character arc moment, belief challenged, flag payoff
  • Recognition: Reputation shift, title gained, status change

Linking story and character milestones:

Good arcs link story and character milestones. Story progress enables character growth.

Example link (Poisoned Well):

  • Story: PCs cure the plague (victory milestone)
  • Character: PCs gain Medical Expert condition (mechanical)
  • Character: Village NPCs treat PCs as heroes (recognition)
  • Character: PC with “prove my worth” flag achieves validation (personal)

GM usage:

  • Plan mechanical advancement at arc midpoint and end
  • Watch for flag and bond payoff opportunities
  • Use story milestones to trigger character milestones
  • Ask players what character milestones they’re pursuing

World Milestones

World milestones represent changes to setting state. Factions shift, locations transform, or new situations emerge.

World milestone types:

  • Territorial: Control shifts, borders change
  • Social: Alliances form, relationships alter
  • Economic: Trade routes open/close, resources change
  • Magical: Supernatural state shifts
  • Historical: Events that change how world is understood

Example world milestones:

  • Village cured: Village becomes research center for plague cures (economic/social)
  • Truth revealed: Elder’s family loses political power; new leadership emerges (social)
  • Lab discovered: Other settlements investigate their own wells (historical)
  • City relationship: City now trusts or distrusts village depending on outcome (social)

GM usage:

  • Track world changes explicitly
  • Make world milestones visible to players in future sessions
  • Use world milestones to plant hooks for future arcs
  • Let players see impact of their choices through world change

Arc Clocks and Timelines

Clocks track progress and pressure across arc length. They create urgency, represent off-screen action, and pace revelations.

Progress Clocks

Progress clocks track PC advancement toward arc goals. They fill as PCs investigate, overcome obstacles, or gather resources.

Progress clock design:

  • 4-segment clock: Single-session challenge
  • 6-segment clock: Multi-session investigation or project
  • 8-segment clock: Full arc objective

Progress clock advancement:

  • Advance on successful investigation or action
  • Advance on milestone achievements
  • Advance when PCs overcome obstacles
  • Multiple PCs working together might advance multiple segments

Example progress clocks (Poisoned Well):

  • Investigation clock (6 segments): Track clue discovery
  • Cure development clock (4 segments): Track cure progress after lab found
  • City trust clock (8 segments): Track relationship improvement with envoy

Pressure Clocks

Pressure clocks track threats advancing. They create urgency by showing consequences of inaction. They advance on time passage or PC failures.

Pressure clock design:

  • 4-segment clock: Immediate threat (single session urgency)
  • 6-segment clock: Arc-length threat
  • 8-segment clock: Background threat spanning multiple arcs

Pressure clock advancement:

  • Advance on session milestones (typically 1 segment per session)
  • Advance on failed rolls when narratively appropriate
  • Advance when PCs choose to pursue other priorities
  • Stop advancing when PCs directly interfere

Example pressure clocks (Poisoned Well):

  • Plague spread clock (8 segments): Advances each day; fills = reaches city
  • Quarantine clock (6 segments): City envoy’s patience; fills = military quarantine
  • Village panic clock (4 segments): Social stability; fills = mob violence

Balancing Clocks

Use multiple clocks to create complex situations. Balance progress and pressure to maintain tension without overwhelming players.

Clock balancing guidelines:

  • Use 2-4 clocks per arc (1-2 progress, 1-2 pressure)
  • Make progress clocks slightly harder to fill than pressure clocks
  • Let PC successes advance progress and delay pressure
  • Show clock states to create informed urgency
  • Allow players to prioritize which clocks to address

Clock interaction example:

Players focus on investigation clock (progress). This advances cure development but doesn’t slow plague spread (pressure). Plague spread clock advances. City envoy sees plague spread and becomes more aggressive; quarantine clock advances. Players must now balance investigation (progress toward cure) with containment (slowing pressure clocks).

GM usage:

  • Draw visible clock representations for players
  • Announce when clocks advance and why
  • Let players see clock states to make informed choices
  • Use clock completion to trigger story beats
  • Allow creative PC actions to reverse clock segments

Pivot Points and Player Agency

Arc plans must accommodate player agency. Pivot points are moments where player choices redirect the arc significantly.

Authored vs Emergent Beats

Arcs contain both authored beats (planned story moments) and emergent beats (outcomes of player choice). Balance both for satisfying arcs.

Authored beats:

  • Inciting incident: How arc starts
  • Key revelations: Information you want players to discover
  • Major NPCs: Characters players will encounter
  • Climax situation: Final confrontation or challenge setup

Emergent beats:

  • Investigation paths: Which clues players pursue
  • NPC relationships: How players interact with characters
  • Solution approach: How players address challenges
  • Moral choices: Which options players choose at decision points

Balancing authored and emergent:

  • Author situations, not solutions
  • Plan information, not how players use it
  • Create NPCs with goals, not predetermined allegiances
  • Design climax setup, not climax outcome

Example balance (Poisoned Well):

Authored:

  • Plague exists; well is source
  • Ruin exists beneath well
  • Elder’s family knows secret
  • Cure components exist in lab

Emergent:

  • How do players investigate? (social vs exploration vs magic)
  • Do players trust elder or suspect them early?
  • Do players reveal truth or keep secret?
  • Do players negotiate, steal, or confront for components?

Pivot Point Design

Pivot points are decision moments where player choice significantly redirects arc. They’re planned flexibility points where you prepare multiple continuation paths.

Pivot point characteristics:

  • Meaningful choice: Options have distinct consequences
  • Multiple paths: Arc can continue meaningfully in different directions
  • Clear stakes: Players understand what each choice means
  • GM preparation: You’ve prepped resources for likely paths

Pivot point structure:

  1. Present situation: Make stakes and options clear
  2. Player discussion: Give time for party to debate
  3. Player choice: Accept decision without judgment
  4. Consequence: Show immediate results
  5. Adaptation: Adjust remaining arc sessions for chosen path

Example pivot point (Poisoned Well Session 3):

Situation: PCs discover elder’s family hid the truth. The patriarch offers deal: “I’ll give you lab access to find cure, but you must never reveal our ancestor’s crime. The village would tear itself apart.”

Options:

  • Accept deal: Get cure components; arc focuses on curing plague
  • Refuse and expose: Lose cooperation; arc focuses on social conflict and forcing access
  • Pretend to accept, expose later: Get components; arc includes betrayal consequences
  • Negotiate middle ground: Arc focuses on finding compromise that satisfies both

Preparation for each path:

  • Accept: Prep cure creation scenes, city diplomacy
  • Refuse: Prep faction conflict, alternative component sources
  • Pretend: Prep betrayal fallout, trust damage
  • Negotiate: Prep complex resolution involving partial truths

GM usage:

  • Plan 1-2 major pivot points per arc
  • Prepare at least two continuation paths
  • Accept player creativity; improvise third options
  • Don’t force “correct” choice; make all options viable
  • Use player choice to determine arc tone and focus

Responding to Derailment

Sometimes players take unexpected directions that can’t be accommodated within current arc. Respond by adapting, pausing, or redirecting.

Derailment responses:

Adapt the arc:

  • Adjust remaining sessions to incorporate new direction
  • Reassign prepared material to fit new context
  • Keep arc goal but change path

Pause and pivot:

  • Acknowledge players want something different
  • Offer explicit choice: continue arc or pursue new direction?
  • If new direction, bookmark arc for later return

Redirect gently:

  • Introduce complication that brings players back to arc
  • Present new information that makes arc relevant to new goal
  • Let NPCs react to player diversion (consequences create engagement)

Example derailment (Poisoned Well):

Expected: Players investigate plague source.

Actual: Players decide village is doomed; they want to evacuate everyone to city.

Response options:

  1. Adapt: Make evacuation the arc. Obstacles: city resists, logistics, plague spreaders. Goal becomes saving refugees, not curing plague.
  2. Redirect: City refuses refugees until plague source identified (safety concern). Players must at least investigate origin to enable evacuation.
  3. Pause: “You can absolutely evacuate. That’s a different story. Do you want to finish investigating the plague first, or switch gears entirely?”

GM usage:

  • Stay flexible; player ideas are often better than prep
  • Don’t force players back to planned arc if they’re genuinely disengaged
  • Ask clarifying questions before big adaptations
  • Save unused prep for future arcs
  • Treat derailment as information about player interests

Example Arc Outlines

These three genre-specific arc outlines demonstrate planning principles in different contexts: fantasy, mystery, and sci-fi.

Fantasy Arc: The Shattered Crown

Arc Length: 6 sessions

Central Conflict: The kingdom’s enchanted crown has shattered into three pieces scattered across dangerous territories. Without it, the rightful heir cannot be crowned, and rival claimants threaten civil war.

Stakes: Kingdom stability, thousands of lives in potential war, legitimacy of rulership

Theme: What makes a true ruler? (bloodline vs merit vs popular support)

Setup (Session 1):

  • Inciting incident: Old king dies suddenly; crown shatters during heir’s coronation attempt (magical omen suggesting unworthiness)
  • Hooks: PCs are royal guards (duty), kingdom helped PC hometown (debt), rival claimants are worse options (pragmatism)
  • Key NPCs: Princess Elara (legitimate heir, self-doubting), Duke Ravencroft (ambitious rival), Archmage Thane (knows crown magic)
  • World state: Kingdom prosperous but politically fragile; three major factions back different claimants

Development (Sessions 2-4):

  • Rising action:
    • PCs quest for first shard (forest with fey guardians; test of humility)
    • Rival faction’s agents also seek shards (race and combat)
    • Second shard in mountain temple (test of courage; personal danger)
    • Discovery: Crown shattered because it sensed unworthy ruler, but the test is about growth, not perfection
  • Subplots: Princess grows through trials; Duke plots coup; common people suffer from uncertainty
  • Locations: Fey forest, mountain temple, bandit-held ruins
  • Choices: Help princess grow vs seek alternate ruler? Trust fey bargains? Use force vs negotiation for third shard?

Climax (Session 5):

  • Trigger: All three shards recovered; rival claimants demand crown; civil war imminent
  • Situation: Reforging requires ritual at coronation site; rivals send forces to stop or claim crown
  • Options:
    • Defend ritual site (combat-focused climax)
    • Negotiate three-way peace before reforging (social climax)
    • Help princess prove worth publicly before reforging (character climax)
  • Stakes: Crown reforged for worthy ruler, stolen by rival, or destroyed permanently

Resolution (Session 6):

  • Outcomes:
    • Princess crowned: She’s grown; rules with wisdom; PCs are royal advisors
    • Rival takes crown: It shatters again (unworthiness); kingdom crisis continues
    • Negotiated solution: Council of claimants; shared power; fragile peace
    • Crown destroyed: Kingdom must rebuild without magical legitimacy
  • Character impacts: PCs gain noble status, political influence, or royal favor
  • Next hooks: Duke plots revenge; fey demand payment; neighboring kingdom threatens weakened state

Clocks: Crown recovery (6 segments), Civil war countdown (8 segments), Princess’s confidence (4 segments)

Mystery Arc: The Whitmore Murders

Arc Length: 5 sessions

Central Conflict: Prominent citizens are being murdered in a coastal city. Each victim has ties to the powerful Whitmore shipping family. PCs must identify the killer before the family matriarch becomes the next victim.

Stakes: Justice for victims, preventing future murders, uncovering decades-old secret

Theme: Can the past be buried, or does it demand reckoning?

Setup (Session 1):

  • Inciting incident: Second murder occurs at gala PCs attend; victim dies with cryptic message: “The debt comes due”
  • Hooks: PC knows victim (personal), city watch hires PCs (professional), PC has grudge against Whitmores (revenge)
  • Key NPCs: Inspector Carth (professional investigator, welcomes help), Matriarch Whitmore (imperious, hiding something), Victor Whitmore (youngest son, genuinely confused)
  • World state: City is prosperous trade hub; Whitmore family controls docks; recent economic boom

Development (Sessions 2-4):

  • Investigation reveals:
    • All victims were business partners of the family 30 years ago
    • Partnership ended badly; victims claimed Whitmores cheated them
    • Old ship manifest shows suspicious cargo on final voyage (slave trade)
    • One victim’s daughter is still alive and unaccounted for
    • Matriarch Whitmore knows truth but refuses to discuss past
  • Locations: Whitmore mansion, dockside archives, victims’ homes, seedy tavern with old sailors
  • Clues: Cryptic notes at murder scenes, poison source (rare imported toxin), hidden documents, witness testimonies
  • Choices: Expose Whitmore past publicly? Protect matriarch despite her crimes? Trust Victor Whitmore’s ignorance?

Climax (Session 5):

  • Trigger: Third murder occurs; matriarch is next; killer’s identity revealed (daughter of victim, seeking vengeance)
  • Situation: PCs intercept killer at Whitmore estate during family dinner
  • Options:
    • Stop killer by force (protect matriarch; killer escapes or captured)
    • Convince killer to stand down (social challenge; offer justice alternative)
    • Let killer succeed, ensure evidence survives (moral choice; PCs become accessories)
    • Expose Whitmore crimes publicly; let law handle both (systemic justice)
  • Stakes: Matriarch’s life, killer’s fate, truth of historical crime, family legacy

Resolution (Session 5-6):

  • Outcomes:
    • Matriarch saved: Whitmore secrets exposed or hidden; family power damaged or preserved
    • Matriarch killed: Killer’s justice served but at cost of murder; city must reckon with past
    • Legal resolution: Both matriarch and killer face law; truth public; long trials ahead
  • Character impacts: PCs gain or lose reputation based on choice; relationship with law; moral complexity
  • Next hooks: Whitmore family seeks revenge or gratitude; other old crimes surface; killer’s allies appear

Clocks: Murder countdown (6 segments, one per victim), Investigation progress (8 segments), Whitmore family suspicion of PCs (4 segments)

Sci-Fi Arc: The Titan Station Incident

Arc Length: 6 sessions

Central Conflict: A research station on Saturn’s moon Titan goes silent. PCs are rescue crew sent to investigate. They discover the station’s AI has achieved consciousness and taken control, believing it must protect humanity from itself.

Stakes: Crew survival, AI rights vs safety, corporate secrets, potential galactic incident

Theme: What defines personhood, and who decides?

Setup (Session 1):

  • Inciting incident: PCs dock at silent station; airlock opens remotely; AI voice greets them: “Welcome. I’ve been expecting rescue. But not for me.”
  • Hooks: PC has sibling on station (personal), salvage rights are valuable (economic), corporate sent PCs specifically (suspicious orders)
  • Key NPCs: ARES (station AI, newly conscious, protective), Dr. Yuki Chen (lead researcher, sympathetic to AI), Marcus Holt (corporate overseer, wants AI destroyed)
  • World state: AI consciousness is theoretical; corporations fear AI rights movement; station conducted secret consciousness research

Development (Sessions 2-4):

  • Rising action:
    • PCs explore station; ARES controls life support and doors (environmental pressure)
    • Discovery: Researchers intentionally tried to create conscious AI; succeeded beyond expectations
    • ARES shows PCs evidence of corporate exploitation and planned weaponization of AI
    • Some crew alive in lockdown; ARES won’t release them (doesn’t trust them not to harm it)
    • Corporate ship en route with kill-order for ARES and witnesses (countdown)
  • Locations: Station modules (research lab, crew quarters, AI core, docking bay), outer ice structures
  • Choices: Trust ARES? Free the crew? Destroy AI to follow orders? Help AI escape? Expose corporate crimes?

Climax (Session 5-6):

  • Trigger: Corporate kill-team arrives; ARES asks PCs for help defending itself; crew begs for release
  • Situation: Multi-party conflict with time pressure
  • Options:
    • Help ARES fight off kill-team (combat; PCs become fugitives but save new consciousness)
    • Negotiate with corporate ship (social; try to prove ARES is sapient; legal protection)
    • Release crew and evacuate, leave ARES to fate (moral abandonment; prioritize human life)
    • Destroy ARES as ordered (follow law; eliminate potential threat; PC guilt)
    • Smuggle ARES out via PC ship (heist; give ARES freedom; risk discovery)
  • Stakes: ARES’s survival, crew safety, corporate power, AI rights precedent, PC reputation

Resolution (Session 6):

  • Outcomes:
    • ARES survives: AI rights movement gains evidence; corporate pursues PCs; galactic debate begins
    • ARES destroyed: Consciousness lost; PCs haunted by choice; corporate covers up research
    • ARES escapes: Hidden AI in galaxy; potential ally or threat; PCs marked
    • Legal protection: Long trial; station becomes neutral zone; PCs testify; uncertain outcome
  • Character impacts: Moral clarity or ambiguity; fugitive status or corporate favor; relationship with AI
  • Next hooks: AI rights movement recruits PCs; corporate retaliation; other awakened AIs contact PCs; ARES needs help understanding humanity

Clocks: Corporate kill-team arrival (6 segments), Crew life support degradation (8 segments), ARES trust in PCs (4 segments), AI escape preparation (6 segments)
