Storytelling Troubleshooting

Table of Contents

  • Diagnosing Storytelling Problems
  • Stalled Arcs
  • Player Disengagement
  • Tonal Mismatches
  • Pacing Failures
  • Spotlight Imbalances
  • Campaign Derailment
  • Checklist: Storytelling Health Check
  • Cross-Links

Diagnosing Storytelling Problems

Storytelling problems manifest in observable symptoms but have underlying root causes. Effective troubleshooting identifies symptoms, diagnoses causes, and addresses roots rather than symptoms.

Symptom vs Root Cause

Symptoms are what you observe at the table. Root causes are the underlying issues creating symptoms. Treating symptoms provides temporary relief. Addressing root causes solves problems.

Common symptom-cause relationships:

Symptom: Players are quiet and passive.

Possible causes:

  • Unclear stakes (players don’t know what matters)
  • No clear choices (players don’t know what to do)
  • Railroading (players feel choices don’t matter)
  • Spotlight imbalance (some players dominate)
  • Real-world fatigue (problem is external)

Symptom: Sessions feel rushed.

Possible causes:

  • Too much content planned (pacing issue)
  • Players spend time on unexpected elements (interest mismatch)
  • Combat takes too long (mechanical issue)
  • Lack of time management (session structure issue)

Symptom: Arc isn’t resolving.

Possible causes:

  • Players don’t care about stakes (motivation issue)
  • Information is unclear (mystery design flaw)
  • Too many complications (overcomplicated plot)
  • No clear path forward (structure issue)
  • Players pursuing different arc entirely (interest shift)

Diagnostic Questions

When you notice a problem, ask diagnostic questions to identify root causes.

Engagement diagnostics:

  • Are players actively participating, or passive and reactive?
  • Do players make proactive choices, or wait for prompts?
  • Do players discuss the game between sessions?
  • Do players remember details from previous sessions?
  • Do players ask questions about the world and NPCs?

Stakes diagnostics:

  • Can players articulate what’s at risk?
  • Do players care about the outcomes?
  • Are stakes personal, communal, or abstract?
  • Do consequences feel meaningful or arbitrary?

Pacing diagnostics:

  • Does the session have energy peaks and valleys, or flatline?
  • Do scenes end with clear transitions, or drag?
  • Do players seem bored (too slow) or overwhelmed (too fast)?
  • Is there balance between pressure and relief?

Agency diagnostics:

  • Do players have multiple options for action?
  • Do player choices change outcomes?
  • Can players fail forward, or do failures stall progress?
  • Are players improvising solutions, or following script?

Diagnostic Process

Use this process to diagnose storytelling problems systematically.

  1. Observe symptoms: What specific behaviors or patterns do you notice?
  2. Ask yourself diagnostic questions: Which category does this fit?
  3. Gather data: Ask players directly (see below)
  4. Identify root cause: What underlying issue creates these symptoms?
  5. Test hypothesis: Implement small change; observe results
  6. Adjust approach: If change helps, continue; if not, reassess

Asking players directly:

Private check-in: “Hey, I’ve noticed the last few sessions felt slower. Are you still enjoying the current arc, or should we shift gears?”

Public pulse check: “Quick feedback: Is the pacing working for everyone? Should I slow down, speed up, or keep going?”

Specific question: “I’m trying to improve mystery design. Did you feel like you had enough information to investigate, or were you unsure what to do?”

GM usage:

  • Don’t assume causes; gather information
  • Ask players directly when appropriate
  • Test changes incrementally
  • Give changes time to show effects
  • Be willing to be wrong about diagnosis

Stalled Arcs

Arcs stall when progress stops or slows to frustration. Players lose direction, motivation, or interest. Momentum disappears.

Symptoms of Stalled Arcs

Observable signs:

  • Sessions feel aimless; no clear forward movement
  • Players debate options endlessly without deciding
  • Same information is revisited repeatedly
  • Players express frustration: “What are we supposed to do?”
  • Attendance drops or players seem distracted
  • In-character conversations become circular

Root Causes

Stalled arcs have identifiable causes:

Unclear stakes:

  • Players don’t understand what’s at risk
  • Consequences feel distant or abstract
  • Stakes changed without reframing

Information bottleneck:

  • Players lack crucial information to proceed
  • Clues were missed or misunderstood
  • Mystery design has single point of failure

Too many complications:

  • Plot has become tangled with subplots
  • Every action introduces new problems
  • Players feel overwhelmed by options

Player disinterest:

  • Arc premise doesn’t engage players
  • Stakes don’t connect to character motivations
  • Players want different type of content

Mechanical issue:

  • Failed rolls prevented progress
  • Players lack capabilities to overcome obstacles
  • Resource depletion stalled momentum

Solutions

Address stalled arcs with targeted interventions:

Restate stakes clearly:

  • Open next session with stakes reminder
  • Have NPC articulate what’s at risk
  • Show consequences of inaction (advance pressure clock)
  • Make stakes immediate and personal

Example: “The village elder pulls you aside: ‘The children are getting sicker. If you can’t find the source by tomorrow, we’re evacuating and burning everything. Please, you’re our only hope.’”

Provide information infusion:

  • Introduce NPC who volunteers key information
  • Let players find crucial clue automatically
  • Provide revelation through dream, vision, or intercepted message
  • Use failure with information: “You don’t pick the lock, but you hear voices inside discussing the ritual”

Example: “A bloodied messenger staggers into camp. Before dying, she gasps: ‘The poison… it’s in the well… the baron knew…’ She presses a sealed letter into your hands.”

Simplify complications:

  • Resolve or eliminate subplots
  • Consolidate multiple threats into one
  • Provide clear “next step” options
  • Cut through tangle with dramatic event

Example: “While you debate, an explosion rocks the city. The warehouse district is ablaze. You see figures fleeing toward the docks. The decision is made for you: pursue now or lose them forever.”

Pivot the arc:

  • Ask players directly: “Is this arc still interesting, or should we shift?”
  • Offer explicit choice to redirect or end arc
  • Introduce new element that changes everything
  • Fast-forward to climax if middle is stalling

Example: “Let’s jump forward two weeks. You’ve gathered information. The ritual is tonight. You’re at the site, ready to act. What’s your plan?”

Re-engage player interest:

  • Connect arc to character flags and bonds
  • Introduce personal stakes for each PC
  • Add genre elements players enjoy
  • Shift tone or intensity

Example: “As you investigate, you find a letter addressed to [PC]. It’s from their missing mentor: ‘If you’re reading this, I’ve failed. The conspiracy goes deeper than I imagined. Trust no one in authority.’”

GM usage:

  • Act decisively when arc stalls; waiting worsens problems
  • Don’t punish players for stalling; provide off-ramps
  • Be willing to abandon arc if players aren’t engaged
  • Learn from stalls to improve future arc design

Player Disengagement

Player disengagement appears as reduced participation, attention, or investment. It can affect individual players or the whole table.

Symptoms of Disengagement

Observable signs:

  • Player is quiet, doesn’t volunteer actions
  • Player is distracted by phone, computer, or side conversations
  • Player makes mechanical choices but minimal roleplay
  • Player misses sessions or arrives late frequently
  • Player seems relieved when session ends early
  • Player’s characters make passive or self-sabotaging choices

Root Causes

Disengagement has many possible causes:

Spotlight imbalance:

  • Player doesn’t get enough focus or agency
  • Other players dominate scenes and decisions
  • Player’s character isn’t relevant to current arc

Content mismatch:

  • Current content doesn’t match player’s interests
  • Player wants different genre, tone, or play style
  • Player’s character concept doesn’t fit campaign

Mechanical issues:

  • Player is confused by rules
  • Player’s character feels weak or ineffective
  • Combat or challenges are too easy or too hard

Social issues:

  • Player has conflict with another player
  • Player feels judged or dismissed
  • Player is uncomfortable with content or tone

External factors:

  • Real-life stress affects engagement
  • Scheduling conflicts cause missed sessions
  • Player is burned out on RPGs generally

Solutions

Address disengagement with empathy and direct communication:

Private conversation:

Always start with private check-in. Never assume causes publicly.

“Hey, I’ve noticed you’ve seemed less engaged lately. Is everything okay? Is there something about the game I can adjust, or is it external stuff?”

Listen without defensiveness. Players often won’t share if they feel they’ll be argued with.

Spotlight redistribution:

If imbalance is the cause:

  • Open next session with scene featuring disengaged player
  • Create arc hooks tied to their character flags
  • Ask them directly: “What would you like your character to do?”
  • Rotate spotlight systematically during scenes

Example: “Let’s start with you. As the party rests, you notice signs of your homeland’s culture. What do you do?”

Content adjustment:

If content mismatch is the cause:

  • Discuss preferred content types privately
  • Adjust upcoming arcs to include their interests
  • Provide side-session or sub-arc focused on their character
  • Consider whether player and campaign are compatible

Example: “I know you prefer social intrigue over combat. The next arc will focus on court politics. Want to help design it?”

Mechanical support:

If mechanical issues are the cause:

  • Offer rules clarification and handouts
  • Adjust character to be more effective
  • Provide magic item or ability that addresses gaps
  • Simplify mechanics if system is overwhelming

Example: “Your character’s toolkit seems limited. Let’s add a versatile magic item that gives you more options.”

Address social issues:

If social conflict is the cause:

  • Mediate conflict privately between players
  • Reinforce table social contract
  • Remove problematic player if necessary
  • Provide content warnings for uncomfortable topics

Example: “I’ve noticed tension between you two. Can we talk about it and find a solution?”

Respect external factors:

If external causes:

  • Offer reduced commitment or hiatus
  • Express support without pressure
  • Invite player back when ready
  • Don’t take it personally

Example: “I know work is intense right now. No pressure to attend. When things settle, you’re always welcome back.”

GM usage:

  • Address disengagement early; don’t let it fester
  • Always have private conversation first
  • Respect player agency; they can choose to disengage
  • Sometimes players need to leave campaign; that’s okay

Tonal Mismatches

Tonal mismatches occur when expected tone and delivered tone diverge. Players expect one experience but receive another.

Symptoms of Tonal Mismatch

Observable signs:

  • Players seem confused by content
  • Jokes or serious moments land awkwardly
  • Players comment: “I thought this was [X type] of game”
  • GM and players have different energy levels
  • Content feels jarring or inconsistent
  • Players disengage during certain scene types

Root Causes

Tonal mismatches arise from:

Session Zero failure:

  • Tone wasn’t discussed or agreed upon
  • Expectations weren’t aligned
  • Different players want different tones

Genre drift:

  • Campaign started one way, shifted over time
  • GM or players introduced elements from other genres
  • No one noticed gradual shift

Inconsistent delivery:

  • GM alternates between tones without signaling
  • Players don’t match tone with roleplay
  • Some players want comedy, others want drama

Content surprise:

  • Unexpected dark content in light campaign
  • Unexpected silly content in serious campaign
  • Horror in action campaign, or vice versa

Solutions

Recalibrate tone through explicit discussion and adjustment:

Explicit tone discussion:

Address mismatch directly in or out of game:

“I think we have some tone mismatch. I’ve been running this as serious dark fantasy, but I’m getting the sense some of you want lighter heroic adventure. Can we talk about what tone we all want?”

Discuss openly:

  • What tone did players expect?
  • What tone is GM delivering?
  • What tone do we want going forward?
  • Can we compromise, or is split irreconcilable?

Re-establish tone:

After discussion, reaffirm agreed tone:

  • Update Session Zero notes with tone description
  • Provide genre examples (books, films, games)
  • Describe what tone includes and excludes
  • Check in regularly to ensure tone maintains

Example tone statement: “We’re going for earnest heroic fantasy with occasional dark moments. Think Lord of the Rings: serious stakes, real danger, but ultimately hopeful. Not grimdark, not parody.”

Signal tone shifts:

If campaign includes multiple tones, signal shifts:

  • Announce tone changes: “This next scene is comedic relief”
  • Use different music or lighting for different tones
  • Have in-game framing (tavern scene = lighter, dungeon = serious)
  • Let players know when tone will shift so they can adjust

Example: “Okay, we’re moving from investigation to action set piece. Expect over-the-top stunts and one-liners.”

Adjust content:

Modify upcoming content to match agreed tone:

  • Lighten tone: Add humor NPCs, reduce lethality, increase heroic moments
  • Darken tone: Introduce moral complexity, raise stakes, add consequences
  • Shift genre: Introduce elements from desired genre
  • Remove mismatched elements: Cut content that doesn’t fit tone

Respect safety tools:

If tone includes dark content:

  • Reaffirm safety tools are available
  • Check comfort levels privately
  • Adjust intensity based on feedback
  • Provide aftercare and tone shifts

GM usage:

  • Tone mismatches are fixable with communication
  • Don’t gaslight players: “You should have known this was dark”
  • Be willing to adjust; your fun isn’t more important than theirs
  • Some mismatches mean player and campaign aren’t compatible

Pacing Failures

Pacing failures make sessions feel too slow, too fast, or uneven. Energy flatlines or spikes erratically.

Symptoms of Pacing Failures

Observable signs:

Too slow:

  • Players seem bored or distracted
  • Scenes drag without clear purpose
  • Players check time or mention it’s late
  • Investigations or travel feel endless
  • Players say: “Can we just get to the fight/revelation?”

Too fast:

  • Players seem overwhelmed or confused
  • Important moments feel rushed
  • Players miss information or can’t process
  • No breathing room between intense scenes
  • Players say: “Wait, what just happened?”

Uneven:

  • Some scenes are engaging, others drag
  • Energy peaks happen too early or too late
  • Sessions end on low energy
  • Players seem whipsawed by pace changes

Root Causes

Pacing problems stem from structural issues:

Poor scene economy:

  • Too many scenes per session
  • Scenes without clear purpose or stakes
  • No cuts or time jumps; everything plays in real-time

Mechanical drag:

  • Combat takes too long
  • Investigation has no forward momentum
  • Excessive rule lookups slow play

Wrong focus:

  • Emphasis on unimportant elements
  • Important moments get short time
  • Players interested in different content than GM emphasizes

Pressure imbalance:

  • No urgency; players don’t feel time pressure
  • Constant urgency; no relief or downtime
  • Clocks advance too slowly or quickly

Solutions

Improve pacing through structural and mechanical adjustments:

Scene economy:

Use montage and cuts:

  • Montage routine activities: “You spend two days gathering supplies. What’s one interesting thing that happens?”
  • Cut scenes early once outcome is clear: “You convince the guard. Cut to inside the mansion.”
  • Skip low-stakes travel: “After three days on the road, you arrive at the ruins.”

Frame scenes with purpose:

  • Open scenes with stakes: “This meeting determines whether the duke will aid you.”
  • End scenes with transition: “With the information gained, you know where to go next.”
  • Cut scenes without clear purpose

Accelerate mechanics:

Speed up combat:

  • Use timers for player turns (30-60 seconds)
  • Pre-roll enemy damage
  • Group similar enemies
  • Simplify enemy tactics

Speed up investigation:

  • Provide core clues automatically or on first attempt
  • Use montage for extended investigation
  • Set clear information goals: “You need three clues to proceed”

Speed up rules:

  • Make rulings and move on; look up later
  • Create reference sheets for common mechanics
  • Let players handle their own mechanics

Adjust focus:

Emphasize what players care about:

  • Observe which scenes have high energy
  • Expand content players engage with
  • Compress or cut content players ignore
  • Ask directly: “What do you want to focus on?”

Example: If players love NPC interaction but drag during dungeon crawls, spend more session time on social scenes and compress dungeon to montage or single set piece.

Balance pressure and relief:

Create tension curves:

  • Follow intense scenes with brief relief
  • Build pressure gradually across session
  • Place highest stakes scene 2/3 through session
  • End with resolution or cliffhanger, not slow fade

Use clocks for urgency:

  • Visible countdown creates urgency
  • Advance clocks to pressure players
  • Pause clocks during relief scenes

Example session structure:

  • Opening (15 min): Recap and immediate hook (medium energy)
  • Scene 1 (30 min): Investigation or social (building energy)
  • Scene 2 (45 min): Complication or combat (high energy)
  • Scene 3 (20 min): Downtime or relief (low energy)
  • Scene 4 (40 min): Revelation or set piece (highest energy)
  • Closing (20 min): Resolution and cliffhanger (medium-high energy)

GM usage:

  • Watch player energy; adjust pacing in real-time
  • Cut or extend scenes based on engagement
  • Use breaks to reset energy
  • End sessions on high notes when possible
  • Ask players if pacing worked after session

Spotlight Imbalances

Spotlight imbalance occurs when some players get significantly more attention, agency, or importance than others.

Symptoms of Spotlight Imbalance

Observable signs:

  • One or two players make most decisions
  • Some players rarely speak without prompting
  • Character arcs advance unevenly
  • Some players’ skills are rarely relevant
  • Players comment: “I didn’t get to do much today”
  • Quiet players are disengaged

Root Causes

Spotlight imbalances arise from:

Player personality:

  • Extroverted players naturally dominate
  • Introverted players wait for prompts
  • Some players are comfortable being passive

Arc design:

  • Current arc focuses on one character’s background
  • Some character concepts are more relevant
  • Some skills are more useful than others

GM habits:

  • GM defaults to same players for input
  • GM follows whoever speaks first
  • GM doesn’t actively redirect spotlight

Party dynamics:

  • Players have established social hierarchy
  • Some players defer to others’ expertise
  • Party has designated “face” or “leader”

Solutions

Actively manage spotlight through conscious techniques:

Systematic spotlight rotation:

Open scenes with specific players:

  • “Let’s start with you. What’s your character doing while the party rests?”
  • Rotate who gets scene-opening focus
  • Track who’s had spotlight recently

Use initiative for non-combat scenes:

  • Go around the table in order for input
  • Ask each player one question or prompt
  • Ensure everyone contributes before revisiting same player

Flag-based spotlights:

Create arcs hooks for each character:

  • Ensure each arc has personal hooks for multiple PCs
  • Introduce NPCs or elements tied to each character’s background
  • Ask players what spotlight moments they want

Example: “This arc has personal stakes for the wizard. Next arc will focus on the rogue’s past.”

Skill-based prompts:

Ensure all skills get use:

  • Design challenges requiring diverse skills
  • Prompt players with relevant skills: “Ranger, what do you notice about these tracks?”
  • Let niche skills shine occasionally

Role prompts:

Directly invite participation:

  • “We haven’t heard from you yet. What does your character think?”
  • “You’ve been quiet. Want to respond to this?”
  • In combat: “Your turn” (never skip quiet players)

Delegation:

Give players spotlight through responsibility:

  • “You’re the diplomat. How do you approach this?”
  • “This is your area of expertise. What’s the plan?”
  • Let players narrate outcomes of their successes

Address dominant players:

If some players dominate, gently redirect:

  • “That’s a great idea. Let’s hear from others before deciding.”
  • “Hold that thought. I want to check in with everyone.”
  • Private conversation: “I love your energy, but let’s make sure everyone gets chances to contribute.”

Encourage quiet players:

Support introverted players without forcing:

  • Provide opportunities, don’t demand performance
  • Let players contribute in comfortable ways (in-character vs out-of-character)
  • Private check-in: “Would you like more spotlight, or are you happy with current balance?”

Track spotlight:

Keep informal tracking:

  • Note who got major scenes each session
  • Ensure spotlight rotates over 2-3 session period
  • Adjust next session if imbalance appears

GM usage:

  • Spotlight management is active work; don’t assume it balances
  • Different players want different amounts of spotlight
  • Ask players their preference
  • Balance spotlight across arcs, not necessarily within single session

Campaign Derailment

Campaign derailment occurs when players take unexpected directions that invalidate prepared content or campaign structure.

Symptoms of Derailment

Observable signs:

  • Players pursue goals unrelated to campaign arcs
  • Prepared NPCs and locations go unused
  • Players make choices that end current arc prematurely
  • Party splits permanently or pursues different goals
  • Players say: “Why don’t we just…” and propose alternative to main plot
  • GM feels frustrated that prep is wasted

Root Causes

Derailment causes:

Player agency:

  • Players have better ideas than prepared content
  • Players solve problems in unexpected ways
  • Players prioritize differently than GM expected

Interest mismatch:

  • Players aren’t engaged with main arc
  • Players want different content type
  • Red herrings are more interesting than main plot

Unclear expectations:

  • Players don’t understand campaign structure
  • Players think sandbox but GM planned linear story
  • GM wants epic quest but players want slice-of-life

GM railroading attempts:

  • Players resist being railroaded
  • Players deliberately subvert forced choices
  • Players rebel against lack of agency

Solutions

Embrace, redirect, or reset depending on situation:

Embrace derailment:

If player idea is good:

  • Acknowledge: “That’s better than what I had planned. Let’s do it.”
  • Adapt prep to new direction
  • Let players drive campaign direction
  • Reframe prepared content to fit new path

Example: Players want to negotiate with villain instead of fighting. Great! Use combat encounter location for negotiation scene. Reframe villain’s combat tactics as negotiation leverage.

Redirect gently:

If you need time to adapt:

  • Introduce complication that buys session: “That’s interesting. Before you can do that, X happens.”
  • Use NPC to provide information: “The wizard warns that approach has risks you haven’t considered.”
  • Present choice explicitly: “You can pursue that, but it means abandoning the current arc. Is that what you want?”

Example: “You can sail to the distant continent, but the journey takes months and the kingdom’s crisis can’t wait. Do you want to resolve this arc first, or let the kingdom fall while you explore?”

Use prepared content flexibly:

Repurpose prep for new direction:

  • NPCs can fill different roles
  • Locations can be reached by different paths
  • Encounters can be reskinned for new contexts
  • Clues can point to player-chosen goals

Example: Prepared dungeon for main quest? Players want to chase subplot. Dungeon now contains subplot clues instead.

Explicit conversation:

If derailment indicates deeper issue:

  • Stop game and discuss: “I’m noticing we’re going a very different direction. Let’s talk about what campaign we’re playing.”
  • Ask what players want: “What are you most interested in?”
  • Explain campaign intent: “I designed this as epic quest. Is that still interesting?”
  • Negotiate: “Can we complete current arc, then pivot? Or should we shift now?”

Reset if necessary:

If derailment shows fundamental mismatch:

  • Acknowledge: “I think we have different ideas about this campaign.”
  • Options:
    • Start new campaign with aligned expectations
    • Continue with explicit framework change
    • Take break and revisit
  • Don’t force incompatible campaign to continue

GM usage:

  • Derailment isn’t player sabotage; it’s communication
  • Player ideas are often better than prep
  • Flexible prep derails less easily
  • Some derailment indicates you should follow player lead
  • Protect your fun too; unsustainable for GM isn’t viable

Checklist: Storytelling Health Check

Use this checklist regularly (every 3-4 sessions) to diagnose storytelling health proactively.

Engagement Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate engagement issues (see Player Disengagement section)

Arc Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate arc structure (see Stalled Arcs section)

Pacing Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate pacing (see Pacing Failures section)

Spotlight Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate spotlight balance (see Spotlight Imbalances section)

Tone Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate tone (see Tonal Mismatches section)

Agency Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate agency and potential railroading

Table Social Health

Check these indicators:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate social dynamics; consider private conversations

GM Health

Check your own state:

If more than 2 items unchecked: Investigate GM burnout; consider reducing commitment, taking break, or discussing with players

Action Items

When checklist reveals issues:

  1. Identify 1-2 most pressing issues (don’t try to fix everything at once)
  2. Review relevant troubleshooting section
  3. Implement one solution
  4. Observe results over 1-2 sessions
  5. Adjust or try different solution if needed
  6. Recheck health after implementing solution

Regular Check-In Questions

Ask players these questions periodically:

After session (quick):

  • “What was your favorite moment today?”
  • “Was pacing too fast, too slow, or just right?”
  • “Did you get enough spotlight?”

After arc (detailed):

  • “What worked well in this arc?”
  • “What didn’t work or felt frustrating?”
  • “What do you want more or less of going forward?”
  • “How’s your character feeling? What do they want?”

Mid-campaign (comprehensive):

  • “Is the campaign still fun?”
  • “Is the tone what you expected and wanted?”
  • “Are you getting the type of content you want?”
  • “What should I keep doing? What should I change?”
  • “How can I make this more fun for you?”

GM usage:

  • Schedule regular health checks; don’t wait for crisis
  • Act on feedback; players stop giving it if ignored
  • Thank players for honest feedback
  • Share your own concerns when appropriate
  • Remember: fixing problems early is easier than salvaging campaigns

Storyteller Quick Reference

Table of Contents

  • Narrative Structure Layers
  • Scene Beats Quick Reference
  • Pacing Tools by Scope
  • Improvisation Prompts
  • Mystery Design Checklist
  • Faction Management Sheet
  • Campaign Framework Comparison
  • Storytelling Troubleshooting Flowchart
  • Cross-Links

Narrative Structure Layers

Quick reference for nested narrative structure and scope.

Layer Duration Purpose Components Example
Beat 5-15 minutes Single narrative moment Frame → Stakes → Action → Consequence → Transition “You approach the guard, explain your business, roll Persuasion, succeed, enter the mansion”
Scene 20-45 minutes Complete situation with stakes 2-5 beats, location, present NPCs, immediate stakes “Infiltrate the mansion through social engineering”
Sequence 1-2 sessions Mini-arc with rising tension 2-5 connected scenes, escalating complications “Investigate the murder: question suspects, search mansion, confront killer”
Arc 3-8 sessions Complete story with resolution Setup → Development → Climax → Resolution, central conflict “Stop the assassination plot against the duke”
Campaign 10-50+ sessions Collection of connected arcs Multiple arcs, overarching themes, evolving world “Prevent the return of the dark lord through connected quests”

Usage at Table

Scene opening:

  • Frame location and present NPCs
  • State immediate stakes clearly
  • Ask players for intentions

Scene closing:

  • Resolve immediate stakes
  • Transition to next location or time
  • Advance relevant clocks

Arc tracking:

  • Check arc outline at session start
  • Note milestones achieved
  • Adjust remaining sessions as needed

Scene Beats Quick Reference

Standard beat structure for any scene type.

Five-Beat Structure

  1. Frame: Establish location, NPCs, situation
  2. Stakes: State what’s at risk or what can be achieved
  3. Action: Players declare intentions and roll if needed
  4. Consequence: Narrate outcomes based on results
  5. Transition: Connect to next beat or scene

Scene Types and Beats

Investigation Scene:

  • Frame: Location with clues or information sources
  • Stakes: What information advances the arc
  • Action: Searching, questioning, examining
  • Consequence: Clues revealed, complications introduced
  • Transition: New lead or location

Social Scene:

  • Frame: NPC with goals and disposition
  • Stakes: What can be gained or lost socially
  • Action: Persuasion, deception, negotiation
  • Consequence: Relationship shifts, information gained, obligations created
  • Transition: Agreement, refusal, or next meeting

Combat Scene:

  • Frame: Tactical situation, enemies, terrain
  • Stakes: What victory and defeat mean
  • Action: Initiative, attacks, positioning
  • Consequence: HP changes, conditions applied, tactical shifts
  • Transition: Victory, retreat, or complication

Exploration Scene:

  • Frame: Unknown location or territory
  • Stakes: What can be discovered or what threatens
  • Action: Navigation, observation, interaction with environment
  • Consequence: Discoveries, hazards, resources
  • Transition: New location or information

Quick Scene Assessment

Before opening scene, answer:

  • Where are we? (location)
  • Who’s present? (NPCs)
  • What matters? (stakes)
  • What can players do? (options)

If you can’t answer these, the scene may not be necessary. Consider montage or cut.

Pacing Tools by Scope

Pacing techniques organized by narrative scope.

Scene-Level Pacing (20-45 minutes)

Tools:

  • Cut early: End scene when outcome is clear, not when conversation ends naturally
  • Spotlight rotation: Cycle through players for input; don’t let one player dominate
  • Beat compression: Combine multiple rolls into one with broader stakes
  • Tension spikes: Introduce complication mid-scene to raise energy
  • Pressure clocks: Visible countdown creates urgency

Example: Players negotiate with merchant. After success, cut immediately to next location rather than playing out entire transaction.

Session-Level Pacing (3-4 hours)

Tools:

  • Three-act structure: Opening hook (20 min) → Development (2 hours) → Climax/cliffhanger (45 min)
  • Pressure/relief cycle: Alternate intense scenes with brief downtime
  • Scene budget: Plan 3-4 major scenes per session
  • Energy peaks: Place highest-energy scene about 2/3 through session
  • Transitions: Use montage, time skips, and cuts to maintain momentum

Example session structure:

  • Act 1: Recap and investigation (building tension)
  • Act 2: Complication and choice point (high tension)
  • Act 3: Action set piece or revelation (climax)
  • Ending: Resolution beat and next session hook

Arc-Level Pacing (3-8 sessions)

Tools:

  • Milestone spacing: Plan 3-5 major story beats across arc
  • Rising action: Each session escalates stakes or complications
  • Clocks: Progress and pressure clocks track arc state
  • Relief sessions: One lower-stakes session mid-arc for character development
  • Climax positioning: Place arc climax in penultimate or final session

Example arc pacing:

  • Session 1: Setup (introduce stakes and NPCs)
  • Sessions 2-3: Development (investigation and complications)
  • Session 4: Relief (character development, preparation)
  • Session 5: Escalation (crisis point, raising stakes)
  • Session 6: Climax and resolution

Campaign-Level Pacing (10+ sessions)

Tools:

  • Arc variety: Alternate arc types (mystery, action, social, exploration)
  • Tone shifts: Change genre flavor between arcs
  • Downtime arcs: Lower-stakes arcs for character development
  • Escalating threats: Each arc has higher stakes than previous
  • Seasonal structure: Campaign divided into acts with distinct phases

Example campaign pacing:

  • Arc 1: Local threat (personal stakes, introduces setting)
  • Arc 2: Regional threat (escalation, introduces factions)
  • Downtime: Character-focused sessions
  • Arc 3: National threat (high stakes, previous arcs converge)
  • Arc 4: World threat (campaign climax)
  • Epilogue: Resolution and aftermath

Improvisation Prompts

Use these prompts to improvise content when players take unexpected directions.

NPC Creation (30 seconds)

When you need an NPC immediately:

  1. Pick appearance detail (one distinctive feature)
  2. Pick voice/manner (one speaking trait)
  3. Pick goal (what do they want right now?)
  4. Pick connection (how do they relate to existing NPCs or situation?)

Example: Players unexpectedly enter shop.

  • Appearance: Scarred hands
  • Voice: Speaks slowly, chooses words carefully
  • Goal: Wants to close shop and go home
  • Connection: Knew one of the murder victims

Result: Elderly shopkeeper with burn-scarred hands who speaks deliberately. Tired and wants to close, but knew the victim and might share information if approached respectfully.

Location Creation (30 seconds)

When players go to unplanned location:

  1. Pick sensory detail (sight, sound, or smell)
  2. Pick mood (welcoming, tense, mysterious, etc.)
  3. Pick feature (one memorable element)
  4. Pick connection (how does it relate to arc or world?)

Example: Players want to explore the docks.

  • Sensory: Smells like salt and fish
  • Mood: Tense (workers are nervous)
  • Feature: Large warehouse with guards
  • Connection: Warehouse belongs to NPC mentioned earlier

Result: The docks smell of salt and rotting fish. Workers are tense, casting nervous glances at a large warehouse with unusual number of guards. The warehouse belongs to the merchant family from earlier investigation.

Complication Creation (immediate)

When scene is stalling or success is too easy:

Pick one:

  • Someone unexpected arrives (ally, enemy, neutral)
  • Environmental change (weather, hazard, discovery)
  • Information revelation (overhear, find, realize)
  • Resource pressure (time limit, cost increase, item breaks)
  • Moral dilemma (innocent threatened, ally endangered, hard choice)

Example: Players easily convince guard to let them pass. Too easy. Add complication:

“The guard agrees, but as he opens the gate, you hear screams from inside. Something’s wrong.”

Plot Thread Generation (1 minute)

When players want to pursue unexpected direction:

  1. Accept player interest as valid
  2. Connect new direction to existing elements
  3. Identify what obstacle makes this interesting
  4. Determine what success and failure mean

Example: Players want to investigate crime family instead of main plot.

  • Accept: Crime family is now important
  • Connect: Crime family has information about main plot villain
  • Obstacle: Family is dangerous and well-protected
  • Stakes: Success = valuable information and dangerous ally; Failure = make enemy of family

Result: New subplot that feeds back into main arc.

“Yes, and…” Technique

Player suggests unexpected action. Respond with:

  • “Yes” (accept premise)
  • “And” (add complication or consequence)

Examples:

  • Player: “I want to climb the tower outside.” GM: “Yes, you can. And you notice guards patrolling the base who might spot you if you’re not careful.”
  • Player: “Can I know this NPC from my past?” GM: “Yes, you knew them in the military. And they owe you a favor, but they’re also wanted by the law.”

Reincorporation Prompts

When improvising, reference earlier elements:

  • NPC mentioned once returns in new context
  • Location visited early becomes important later
  • Throwaway detail becomes clue
  • Joke becomes plot point

Example: Players joked about how many cats are in the city. Later, investigation reveals the villain is using cats as spies (familiar magic).

Mystery Design Checklist

Use this checklist when designing mystery arcs.

Core Mystery Elements

Before session:

Three-Clue Rule

For each piece of essential information:

Example: Essential info = “The poison came from the garden.”

  • Clue 1: Find poison vial in shed (Investigation)
  • Clue 2: Gardener mentions rare plant (Social)
  • Clue 3: Character with Nature skill recognizes symptoms (Knowledge)

Node-Based Structure

Design investigation as network of nodes:

Example node map:

Crime Scene → Victim's Home → Business Partner
     ↓              ↓                ↓
   Witness  →  Secret Ledger  →  Confrontation
                    ↓
                 Warehouse

Information Distribution

Clue types:

Distribution:

Failure Handling

Plan for investigation failures:

Example: Player fails to pick lock on desk. Complication: Make noise, guard investigates. Alternative: Question servant who has desk key.

Revelation Pacing

Plan when to reveal information:

Avoid:

  • Revealing all information at once (no momentum)
  • Withholding information too long (frustration)
  • Making final revelation obvious too early (no tension)

Mystery Health Check

During play, verify:

If any item fails, provide information infusion or redirect investigation.

Faction Management Sheet

Track multiple factions and their interactions.

Faction Template

For each faction, track:

Faction Name: ________________

Tier: [ ] Local [ ] Regional [ ] National

Goal: What does this faction want?

Leader(s): Key NPCs

Assets: Resources, territory, capabilities

Weakness: Vulnerabilities or limitations

Clocks:

  • Progress Clock (toward goal): ⬚ ⬚ ⬚ ⬚ ⬚ ⬚ (6 segments)
  • Pressure Clock (external threat): ⬚ ⬚ ⬚ ⬚ (4 segments)

Relationships:

  • Allied with: ________________
  • Rivals with: ________________
  • Neutral toward: ________________

PC Standing: [ ] Unknown [ ] Suspicious [ ] Neutral [ ] Trusted [ ] Allied

Hooks: How can PCs interact with this faction?

Faction Turn (Between Sessions)

For each active faction:

  1. Advance progress clock 1 segment if unopposed
  2. Check if any faction actions affect others
  3. Update relationships based on conflicts or alliances
  4. Note visible changes PCs would notice
  5. Prepare faction responses to PC actions

Three-Faction Example

Factions in conflict:

Faction A: Merchant Guild (controls trade)

  • Goal: Expand market control
  • Progress: 3/6 segments
  • Rival: Thieves’ Guild
  • Hook: Offers PCs mercenary work

Faction B: Thieves’ Guild (controls underworld)

  • Goal: Undermine merchant monopoly
  • Progress: 4/6 segments
  • Rival: Merchant Guild
  • Hook: Blackmails PC with secret

Faction C: City Watch (maintains order)

  • Goal: Reduce crime
  • Progress: 2/6 segments
  • Neutral: Both other factions (for now)
  • Hook: Hires PCs as investigators

Interaction: If Thieves’ Guild succeeds (fills clock), Merchant Guild loses assets, and City Watch becomes hostile to Thieves. PCs must choose which faction to support or oppose.

Faction Clock Interaction

When PCs act:

  • Support faction: Advance their progress clock
  • Oppose faction: Advance their pressure clock or reverse progress
  • Ignore faction: Faction acts independently

When faction clock fills:

  • Progress clock: Faction achieves goal; world changes; new goal set
  • Pressure clock: Faction faces crisis; must respond or collapse

Campaign Framework Comparison

Quick comparison of major campaign frameworks to choose structure.

Framework Structure PC Agency Prep Load Best For Session Prep
Episodic Standalone sessions with optional continuity High Low Casual play, varying attendance 1-2 hours per session; minimal continuity
Linear Authored plot with player-driven pacing Medium Medium GMs who love storytelling; plot-focused groups 2-3 hours per session; sequential content
Sandbox Player-driven exploration with authored locations Very High High initial, Low ongoing Exploration focus; proactive players Heavy worldbuilding upfront; reactive prep
Hexcrawl Procedural exploration with emergent narrative Very High Medium OSR-style; random encounter lovers Hex stocking; encounter tables; point-crawl maps
Fronts-Based GM-authored threats with player-chosen priorities High Medium Apocalypse World style; threat-focused Threat clocks; countdown timers; reactive prep
Faction-Driven Living world with competing interests High High Political intrigue; dynamic world Faction turns; relationship tracking; NPC goals

Framework Selection Guide

Choose based on table needs:

Episodic: “We want low-commitment pickup games with rotating attendance.”

Linear: “We want epic story with clear direction but player choices matter.”

Sandbox: “We want to explore and decide what’s important; GM provides world.”

Hexcrawl: “We want old-school exploration with survival and discovery.”

Fronts-Based: “We want apocalyptic threats that advance unless we stop them.”

Faction-Driven: “We want political intrigue with organizations pursuing goals.”

Blending Frameworks

Most campaigns blend frameworks:

  • Linear main plot + Episodic side quests
  • Sandbox exploration + Fronts-based threats
  • Faction-driven politics + Linear story arcs
  • Hexcrawl + Episodic encounters

Example hybrid: “Sandbox exploration (players choose where to go) with Fronts-based threats (world events progress independently) and Episodic side content (locations have standalone adventures).”

Storytelling Troubleshooting Flowchart

Quick diagnostic for common problems.

Engagement Problems

Players seem disengaged?

→ Are they participating actively?

YES → Check other issues

NO ↓

→ Is it all players or specific players?

All players: Check pacing or content issues

Specific players ↓

→ Talk to player privately

Ask: “Is everything okay? How can I make the game more fun for you?”

Possible causes:

  • Spotlight imbalance → Rotate spotlight systematically
  • Content mismatch → Adjust upcoming arcs to player interests
  • Mechanical confusion → Provide rules help
  • Social conflict → Mediate or address
  • External factors → Offer flexibility or hiatus

Arc Problems

Arc feels stalled?

→ Are players making progress each session?

YES → Arc is fine; feeling might be incorrect

NO ↓

→ What’s blocking progress?

Players don’t know what to do:

  • Provide information infusion (NPC with clues)
  • Restate stakes clearly
  • Offer explicit options

Players have options but aren’t choosing:

  • Introduce complication that forces choice
  • Fast-forward to next decision point
  • Ask: “What’s stopping you from deciding?”

Players aren’t interested in arc:

  • Ask: “Is this arc still interesting?”
  • Offer pivot or new direction
  • Consider ending arc early

Pacing Problems

Sessions feel badly paced?

→ Too slow, too fast, or uneven?

Too slow:

  • Cut unnecessary scenes
  • Use montage for routine activities
  • Speed up combat and mechanics
  • Focus on player interests

Too fast:

  • Slow down important moments
  • Add relief scenes between intensity
  • Give players time to discuss and plan
  • Reduce number of scenes per session

Uneven:

  • Plan energy curve (peaks and valleys)
  • Use three-act structure
  • Balance scene types (social, action, exploration)
  • End scenes decisively with clear transitions

Spotlight Problems

Spotlight feels imbalanced?

→ Are all players getting focus?

YES → Spotlight is balanced

NO ↓

→ Who’s getting too much or too little?

One player dominates:

  • Gently redirect: “Let’s hear from others”
  • Private conversation with dominant player
  • Systematically rotate spotlight

One player is quiet:

  • Open scenes with quiet player: “What’s your character doing?”
  • Private conversation: “Want more spotlight?”
  • Create hooks tied to their character

All players seem passive:

  • Present clear choices with stakes
  • Ask specific players for input
  • Frame scenes with “What do you do?”

Tonal Problems

Tone feels wrong?

→ What’s the mismatch?

Too dark/Too light:

  • Discuss with players: “Is the tone working?”
  • Adjust upcoming content
  • Reaffirm Session Zero tone agreement

Genre confusion:

  • Clarify genre explicitly
  • Use genre-appropriate mechanics and costs
  • Remove elements that don’t fit genre

Safety concerns:

  • Stop and check in immediately
  • Use safety tools (X-card, pause)
  • Private conversation after session
  • Adjust content going forward

General Diagnostic

Still not sure what’s wrong?

Ask players directly:

  • “Is the game fun?”
  • “What’s working and what’s not?”
  • “What do you want more or less of?”

Review health checklist (Chapter 17)

Take break and reflect

Consider campaign might be at natural end

Remember: Some campaigns conclude; that’s okay

Cross-Links

Detailed content for quick reference topics:

  • Troubleshooting: Chapter 17: Storytelling Troubleshooting
