    title: “NPC, VPC, and Enemy Design Overview” slug: npc-vpc-enemy-overview doc_type: book version: 0.1.0 last_updated: 2025-11-09 keywords: [ttrpg, gm, npc, vpc, enemy, character, design] —

Chapter 37: NPC, VPC, and Enemy Design Overview

This chapter provides the framework for creating compelling non-player characters, virtual player characters, and enemies that support your campaign without stealing the spotlight from player characters.

Character Types for GMs

As a GM, you create three distinct types of characters, each with specific design principles and narrative purposes:

Non-Player Characters (NPCs): The supporting cast—allies, rivals, mentors, antagonists, and informants who populate your world and create story hooks. NPCs have defined roles and goals but less mechanical detail than PCs, making them efficient to create and run.

Virtual Player Characters (VPCs): Companions that fill party gaps in solo play or small groups. They provide mechanical support and narrative presence without driving decisions. Well-designed VPCs react rather than lead, complement rather than compete.

Enemies & Threats: Opposition in combat, social, or exploration scenes. They have motivations (even if simple), weaknesses (exploitable through clever play), and tactics (patterns players can learn and counter). Meaningful enemies are more than stat blocks.

GM Character Philosophy

Every GM character follows the same three pillars as PC creation, but with different priorities:

Purpose-Driven Identity: Start with what the character contributes to play. NPCs need personality and goals more than complete stat blocks. A memorable voice, mannerism, or moral compass makes an NPC stick in players’ minds more than detailed mechanics.

Scalable Mechanical Coherence: Create detail proportional to narrative importance. Quick-stat background characters need only name + role + one trait. Recurring characters deserve full personalities, skills, goals, and secrets. Don’t over-invest in characters who may never return.

Narrative Function: Every character serves a story purpose. Understanding that function ensures the character contributes to play rather than drifts in the background. Design around what they enable for the PCs, not what they can do independently.

Non-Player Characters (NPCs)

NPCs form the supporting cast of your campaign. They should enhance PC agency, not replace it.

Core Principles

Goals and Secrets: NPCs come alive through wanting things and hiding things. Goals create hooks (“I need you to find my missing daughter”). Secrets create complications (“The daughter isn’t missing—she’s hiding from her father’s gambling debts”).

Personality Over Stats: A distinctive voice, mannerism, or moral compass makes an NPC memorable. “The baker who hums while nervous and feeds everyone” is more memorable than “NPC with Cooking +2.”

Limited Spotlight: NPCs should be helpful without being servile, competent without stealing spotlight, and grateful without being obsequious. They empower protagonists rather than replace them.

NPC Roles

Ally: Supports PCs with resources, information, or assistance. Has own goals that usually align with party interests but create complications when they don’t.

Rival: Competes with PCs for similar goals without being outright enemies. Raises stakes by making success uncertain.

Mentor: Guides and teaches. Has limitations—age, injury, political constraints—that prevent solving problems directly.

Antagonist: Opposes PCs with conflicting goals. Has complex motivations, can be reasoned with, believes they’re justified.

Informant: Provides information transactionally or reluctantly. Controls story pacing through what they know and when they share it.

See Chapter 38 (NPC Design & Templates) and Chapter 39 (NPC Packs) for detailed examples and quick-creation procedures.

Virtual Player Characters (VPCs)

VPCs fill party gaps without overshadowing players. They’re companions, not additional protagonists.

Core Principles

Reactive, Not Proactive: VPCs follow PC leads. They offer suggestions but defer to player decisions. They complement party strengths and cover weaknesses without driving the narrative.

Mechanical Support: VPCs provide skills the party lacks (healing, technical expertise, combat support) but at slightly lower effectiveness than a dedicated PC would.

Behavioral Scripts: Rather than full personalities, VPCs use simple behavioral scripts that make them predictable and manageable. Scripts define how they react to common situations without requiring constant GM attention.

Spotlight Management: VPCs shine when filling their niche role, then step back. They never solve the central problem or make the critical decision.

See Chapter 40 (VPC Design & Behavioral Scripts) for complete VPC creation framework and genre-specific templates.

Enemies & Threats

Enemies provide opposition that makes PC victories meaningful. They should challenge players, not frustrate them.

Core Principles

Motivation and Weaknesses: Even simple enemies have reasons for their actions and exploitable weaknesses. This creates opportunities for clever play beyond “roll initiative.”

Tactical Variety: Different enemy types use different tactics. Minions swarm, elites coordinate, bosses adapt. Patterns create gameplay depth and reward player learning.

Threat Levels: Minions provide numbers and pressure. Elites create tactical challenges. Bosses drive major confrontations. Mix threat levels for dynamic encounters.

Story Integration: The best enemies connect to the larger narrative. They have goals that create conflict, allegiances that complicate situations, and backstories that make defeat or negotiation meaningful.

See Chapter 41 (Enemy Design Framework) and Chapter 42 (Enemy Packs) for detailed enemy creation and genre-specific stat blocks.

Fiction-First GM Character Design

For all GM character types, start with narrative purpose:

  1. Function: What does this character enable for the PCs? What role do they play in the story?
  2. Concept: One sentence describing who they are and what they want.
  3. Personality: 1-3 distinctive traits that guide roleplay.
  4. Goal: What they want right now (creates immediate hooks).
  5. Secret (NPCs) or Weakness (Enemies): One hidden element that creates complications or opportunities.
  6. Mechanics: Only as much detail as their narrative importance requires.

This keeps preparation efficient and ensures every character serves a purpose at the table.

Cross-References

NPC Resources: - Chapter 38: NPC Design & Templates (25 NPCs across 5 genres) - Chapter 39: NPC Packs by Genre (quick-creation procedures) - Chapter 08: NPC Creation (from GM fundamentals section)

VPC Resources: - Chapter 40: VPC Design & Behavioral Scripts (8 scripts + 5 genre templates) - Chapter 09: VPC Creation (from GM fundamentals section)

Enemy Resources: - Chapter 41: Enemy Design Framework (threat levels, tactics, design procedures) - Chapter 42: Enemy Packs by Genre (27 enemies across 5 genres) - Chapter 10: Designing and Running Enemies (from GM fundamentals section)

Quick Reference: - Chapter 43: NPC/VPC/Enemy Quick Reference (consolidated creation checklists)

Next Steps: Chapters 38-42 provide detailed templates, stat blocks, and creation procedures for all three GM character types. Chapter 43 consolidates the creation workflows into quick-reference checklists for at-the-table use.

title: “NPC Design and Templates” slug: character-npc-design-templates doc_type: book version: 0.1.0 last_updated: 2025-11-08 keywords: [ttrpg, character, npc, templates, design] —

NPC Design and Templates

Non-Player Characters are the supporting cast that brings your world to life. They provide hooks, advance plots through secrets and agendas, create meaningful relationships with PCs, and serve as windows into your setting’s culture and conflicts. This chapter provides role-based frameworks for creating NPCs quickly and effectively, from five-minute throwaways to complex recurring characters.

NPC Design Philosophy

Great NPCs don’t need complex stat blocks. They need purpose, personality, and a reason to matter. When designing an NPC, focus on four core elements:

Purpose: What role does this NPC serve in your story? Are they a quest-giver, information source, obstacle, ally, or complication? Purpose determines how much detail you need and which template to use.

Personality: Give each important NPC 2-3 distinctive traits that shape how they interact with PCs. A greedy merchant haggles differently than a generous one. A cautious guard asks different questions than a bold one. Personality creates consistency and memorability.

Goals: Every NPC wants something, even if it’s just to finish their shift without trouble. Goals create hooks for PC interaction and give you clear leverage points for negotiation, conflict, or alliance.

Secrets: What does this NPC know, hide, or want that isn’t immediately visible? Secrets create depth and provide narrative payoffs when revealed. Even minor NPCs can carry useful secrets—the baker knows who meets behind the mill, the guard is in debt, the scholar reads banned texts.

These four elements give you everything needed to run an NPC consistently at the table. Skills and mechanical details matter less than knowing what drives the character and how they’ll react to PC actions.

When to Create NPCs

Not every character needs full development. Use this guidance to match detail level to narrative importance:

Full NPC Templates (20-minute creation): Use for recurring characters who will appear multiple times, have significant impact on the plot, or develop ongoing relationships with PCs. Examples include faction leaders, quest-givers, mentors, major rivals, and recurring antagonists. These NPCs deserve goals, secrets, personality traits, skills, and relationship dynamics.

Quick NPCs (5-10 minutes): Use for characters who appear in 1-3 scenes but serve a specific narrative function. Examples include merchants with critical information, guards blocking a gate, witnesses to crimes, hireling specialists, or one-time allies. These NPCs need a clear role, one distinctive trait, and a simple goal or secret.

Generic Templates (instant): Use for background characters who exist to populate scenes but don’t drive plot. Examples include tavern crowds, market vendors, random guards, servants, or bystanders. Give them a profession and one sentence of description. If PCs engage deeply with a generic NPC, promote them to quick NPC status on the fly by adding a goal and secret.

Important vs Memorable: An NPC’s importance to the plot doesn’t always match their memorability. A one-scene informant with a vivid personality can be more memorable than a recurring quest-giver with bland presentation. Invest personality into NPCs you want players to remember, regardless of their plot significance.

Role-Based Templates

NPCs naturally fall into five core roles based on how they relate to PCs. Each role has distinct functions, typical goals, and relationship dynamics. Understanding these roles helps you create NPCs that serve clear narrative purposes.

Ally

Function: Allies support PCs with resources, information, connections, or direct assistance. They share goals with the party or have complementary interests that align for mutual benefit.

Key Characteristics: - Helpful but not subservient: Allies have their own priorities and limits. They help because it serves their interests or values, not because PCs demand it. - Provide resources or access: Allies offer what PCs can’t easily obtain themselves—safe houses, political connections, specialized knowledge, equipment, healing, or introductions. - Create investment: The best allies give PCs something to protect or lose. When allies face danger, PCs care about the outcome.

Template Structure: - Personality Traits (2-3): Choose traits that explain why they help and how they interact. Examples: loyal, pragmatic, idealistic, protective, generous, cautious. - Goal: What do they want that makes them willing to help PCs? Examples: protect their community, advance a cause, gain allies against a threat, fulfill an oath. - Secret: What do they hide that could complicate the alliance? Examples: divided loyalties, past failures, hidden vulnerabilities, compromising debts. - Skills (2-3): Capabilities they can offer PCs. Examples: Hospitality, Medicine, Persuasion, Streetwise, Crafting, Research. - Notable Trait: One distinctive feature that makes them memorable. Examples: speaks in metaphors, always carries tea, has burn scars from saving others.

Evolving Ally Relationships: - Start with limited trust and gradually increase investment as PCs prove reliable - Give allies challenges PCs can help solve, creating reciprocal support - Threaten allies to raise stakes or force PCs to make hard choices - Allow allies to disagree with PC methods while maintaining shared goals - Consider promoting long-term allies to PC-like complexity with character arcs

Examples Across Genres: - Cozy: Village baker who feeds travelers and shares gossip; goal is community harmony, secret is knows who sneaks around at night - Fantasy: Grizzled mercenary who respects competence; goal is one last score before retirement, secret is protecting a war orphan - Horror: Trauma support group leader who helps survivors; goal is prevent more victims, secret is witnessed the entity firsthand - Modern: Street medic who treats anyone without questions; goal is save lives, secret is avoiding medical board suspension - Sci-Fi: Ship’s engineer who keeps systems running; goal is protect the crew, secret is patched a critical flaw that could fail catastrophically

Rival

Function: Rivals compete with PCs for goals, resources, or recognition but aren’t outright enemies. They create tension and stakes through opposition without necessarily being antagonistic.

Key Characteristics: - Competing interests: Rivals want the same things PCs want—treasure, reputation, contracts, promotions, romantic interests, or victories. - Not hostile: Rivalry involves competition, not destruction. Rivals might obstruct PCs but generally play fair or at least stay within understood boundaries. - Respect and friction: The best rivals acknowledge PC competence while still trying to outperform them. There’s often grudging respect beneath the competition.

Template Structure: - Personality Traits (2-3): Competitive, skilled, prideful, ambitious, honorable, petty. - Competing Goal: What specific thing are they trying to achieve that conflicts with PC goals? Be concrete—“win the tournament,” “secure the contract,” “impress the sponsor.” - Hidden Respect or Envy: What do they secretly admire or resent about the PCs? This creates complexity and potential for relationship evolution. - Skills (2-3): Capabilities that make them credible competition. Often overlaps with PC skills. - Notable Trait: Something that differentiates their approach or makes encounters distinctive.

Making Rivals Interesting Without Hostility: - Frame rivalry as “we both want to win” rather than “I want you to lose” - Give rivals their own allies and resources, making competition feel fair - Allow rivals to occasionally help PCs when larger threats emerge - Create scenarios where PCs and rivals must cooperate temporarily - Reward players for creative competition rather than just defeating the rival

Evolving Rival Relationships: - Escalate rivalry through a series of competitions or confrontations - Introduce complications that force cooperation or reveal shared enemies - Allow rivalry to transform into alliance, grudging friendship, or genuine enmity based on PC actions - Use rivals to create urgency—if PCs delay, the rival might succeed first

Examples Across Genres: - Cozy: Rival shopkeeper competing for best seasonal display; respects quality but hates losing, secretly admires PC creativity - Fantasy: Scheming noble competing for royal favor; politically savvy and ambitious, envies PC direct approach to problems - Horror: Skeptical detective investigating the same case; values evidence over intuition, slowly convinced by PC discoveries - Modern: Investigative journalist chasing the same story; wants the scoop, can be convinced to collaborate for bigger impact - Sci-Fi: Smuggler captain competing for trade routes; respects competence, rivalry could turn to partnership

Mentor

Function: Mentors teach, guide, or sponsor PCs, offering wisdom, training, or protection. They represent what PCs might become and often serve as moral or skill-based role models.

Key Characteristics: - Knowledge to share: Mentors possess skills, experience, or wisdom PCs lack and need. - Investment in PC growth: Mentors care about PC development, whether from duty, affection, or seeing potential. - Limitations or flaws: To avoid overshadowing PCs, mentors must have clear boundaries—they can’t solve every problem themselves.

Template Structure: - Personality Traits (2-3): Patient, wise, demanding, protective, eccentric, world-weary. - Wisdom to Share: What specific knowledge, skill, or perspective can they teach? Be concrete about what they offer. - Limitation or Flaw: What prevents them from solving problems directly? Examples: physical frailty, political constraints, past failures, limited time, ethical boundaries. - Skills (2-3): Areas of expertise they can teach or demonstrate. These should be high competency in fields PCs need. - Notable Trait: Something that makes their mentorship distinctive—teaching style, philosophy, or memorable quirk.

Avoiding Overshadowing PCs: - Make mentors advisors, not protagonists. They guide but don’t act unless PCs are truly stuck. - Give mentors responsibilities that keep them from accompanying PCs on every adventure. - Create situations where mentor knowledge must be applied by PCs in the field. - Allow PCs to surpass their mentor in specific areas, showing growth. - Endanger mentors to raise stakes or force PCs to act independently.

Evolving Mentor Relationships: - Begin with formal teaching and gradually shift to peer collaboration - Create tests or challenges mentors set for PCs to prove growth - Reveal mentor flaws or past mistakes that complicate the relationship - Allow PCs to eventually protect or rescue their mentors, reversing the dynamic - Consider mentors retiring, moving on, or being removed to force PC independence

Examples Across Genres: - Cozy: Retired artisan teaching traditional crafts; patient and skilled, limitation is aging body can’t do the work anymore - Fantasy: Court wizard offering magical guidance; powerful but cryptic, limitation is fears knowledge will die with them - Horror: Occult scholar who knows forbidden lore; warns of dangers, limitation is sanity fraying from exposure - Modern: Veteran investigator teaching tradecraft; experienced and tactical, limitation is past mistakes haunt them - Sci-Fi: Ship’s engineer teaching system maintenance; gruff but caring, limitation is bound to the ship and can’t leave

Antagonist

Function: Antagonists oppose PCs with clear goals and methods, creating conflict through competing interests, ideology, or direct obstruction. Unlike enemies, antagonists are characters with complex motivations, not just obstacles.

Key Characteristics: - Clear opposing goals: Antagonists want specific things that conflict with PC objectives. The conflict should be understandable even if not sympathetic. - Methods and resources: Antagonists have plans, allies, and means to pursue their goals. They act, not just react. - Vulnerability or weakness: What makes the antagonist defeatable? Every antagonist needs exploitable weaknesses—pride, loyalties, limited resources, legal constraints.

Template Structure: - Personality Traits (2-3): Ruthless, idealistic, charismatic, paranoid, calculating, passionate. - Goal Conflicting with PCs: Be specific about what they want and why it opposes the party. - Vulnerability: What emotional, tactical, or structural weakness can PCs exploit? Examples: family loyalty, public image concerns, overconfidence, limited funds. - Skills (2-3): Capabilities that make them formidable opposition. Often includes social manipulation, tactical planning, or specialized expertise. - Notable Trait: Something that makes them distinctive and memorable as opposition.

Making Antagonists Sympathetic or Complex: - Give them understandable motivations even if methods are wrong - Show them caring about something or someone beyond their goals - Create situations where PCs and antagonists could cooperate against greater threats - Allow antagonists to respect or admire PC capabilities - Provide opportunities for redemption, alliance, or compromise if PCs pursue them

Evolving Antagonist Relationships: - Escalate conflict through a series of confrontations with rising stakes - Reveal layers of motivation that complicate initial impressions - Allow PCs to discover antagonist vulnerabilities through investigation - Create pressure points PCs can use to negotiate or manipulate - Consider whether antagonists can be defeated, converted, or bargained with

Examples Across Genres: - Cozy: Mysterious stranger disrupting town peace; unclear motives, vulnerable to community pressure and isolation - Fantasy: Scheming noble seeking political power; ambitious and connected, vulnerable through family reputation - Horror: Cult recruiter offering answers; charismatic and persuasive, vulnerable when exposed or separated from followers - Modern: Corporate executive prioritizing profit over safety; ruthless but professional, vulnerable to legal exposure or scandal - Sci-Fi: Corporate agent enforcing company interests; efficient and well-resourced, vulnerable to being reasoned with if logic favors compromise

Informant

Function: Informants provide information, often transactionally or reluctantly. They know things PCs need and can be convinced, coerced, or paid to share that knowledge.

Key Characteristics: - Valuable knowledge: Informants possess specific information PCs need—locations, secrets, identities, plans, or context. - Transactional relationships: Information has a price, whether money, favors, protection, or something else the informant values. - Reliability concerns: Informants may have incomplete knowledge, hidden agendas, or reasons to mislead. Trust is always a question.

Template Structure: - Personality Traits (2-3): Cautious, greedy, nervous, connected, discrete, opportunistic. - What They Know: Be specific about the information they possess and its value. - What They Want: The price for their information. Examples: payment, protection from threats, favors owed, reputation management, immunity from consequences. - Skills (2-3): Often includes Streetwise, Gossip, Research, Perception, or specialized knowledge. - Notable Trait: Something that makes interactions distinctive—mannerisms, meeting preferences, verification methods.

Managing Information Flow Through Informants: - Portion information across multiple informants to avoid single info-dumps - Give informants partial or flawed knowledge that requires verification - Create urgency by making informants time-limited or threatened - Allow PCs to build long-term informant relationships through repeated fair dealing - Use informants to introduce new plot threads or complications

Evolving Informant Relationships: - Start with expensive, limited information and gradually build trust - Create situations where informants need PC help, reversing the dynamic - Threaten informants to raise stakes or force PCs to provide protection - Allow reliable informants to become allies if PCs invest in the relationship - Introduce competing informants with conflicting information

Examples Across Genres: - Cozy: Nosy neighbor who knows local secrets; wants to feel important, knows who sneaks around at night - Fantasy: Wandering bard collecting rumors; wants good stories to tell, knows political gossip and local legends - Horror: Haunted witness with traumatized memory; wants to forget, knows crucial details about the entity - Modern: Hacker contact with digital access; paranoid and cautious, wants payment or favors - Sci-Fi: Alien diplomat with cultural knowledge; wants peaceful contact, knows protocols and hidden tensions

Quick NPC Creation Procedures

When you need an NPC immediately, use these time-boxed procedures to create characters with appropriate detail levels.

5-Minute NPC

Use For: Background characters who might matter but don’t yet. Vendors, guards, witnesses, service providers.

Procedure: 1. Name: Pick or generate a name appropriate to setting 2. Role: Choose from the five core roles (Ally, Rival, Mentor, Antagonist, Informant) 3. One Trait: Pick a single distinctive personality trait that guides interaction 4. One Goal: State what they want in this moment or generally

Example: Guard Captain Rusk (Informant). Overworked and practical. Wants help covering a staffing shortfall.

At the Table: You know enough to roleplay consistent responses and improvise details. If the NPC becomes important, promote them to 10-minute detail level.

10-Minute NPC

Use For: NPCs who will appear in multiple scenes or have specific plot importance. Quest-givers, recurring contacts, minor faction representatives.

Procedure: 1. Name and Role: As above 2. Personality (2-3 traits): Choose traits that create internal consistency 3. Goal: What do they want, and why does it matter? 4. Secret: One hidden fact, motivation, or complication 5. Skills (2-3): From Mechanics Guide, representing what they can offer or threaten

Example: Sister Morwen (Ally). Devout, practical, unnervingly calm. Wants to protect her community from occult threats. Secret: touched a cursed artifact and still hears it whispering. Skills: Ritual Knowledge, Composure, Occult Lore.

At the Table: You have enough to run multiple encounters, create hooks from their goal and secret, and know what they’re competent at mechanically.

20-Minute NPC

Use For: Major recurring NPCs with significant plot impact. Faction leaders, primary quest-givers, important antagonists, long-term allies.

Procedure: 1. Complete the full role template for their category 2. Add Relationships: How do they connect to 1-2 other NPCs, factions, or PCs? 3. Add Arc Potential: How might this NPC change based on PC actions? What evolution feels natural? 4. Add Visual/Behavioral Details: Distinctive appearance, mannerisms, speech patterns, signature items

Example: Archivist Lysa (Informant/Ally). Curious, absent-minded, protective of knowledge. Goal: ensure dangerous knowledge is preserved but controlled. Secret: maintains a hidden ledger of banned titles and who reads them. Skills: Research, History, Appraisal. Relationships: answers to Head Librarian (antagonist to free information), secretly helps young scholar (potential ally). Arc Potential: could become full ally if PCs prove trustworthy with secrets, or antagonist if PCs abuse knowledge access. Details: ink-stained fingers, constantly adjusting spectacles, tucks hair behind ear when lying, office smells of old paper and mint tea.

At the Table: You can run this NPC across entire campaigns, evolving their relationships and pursuing their goals dynamically.

NPC Relationship and Motivation Tools

Use these tools to track how NPCs relate to PCs and how those relationships evolve through play.

NPC Attitude Table

Hostile: Actively opposes PCs. Will obstruct, attack, or undermine. Requires significant incentive or pressure to cooperate.

Unfriendly: Distrusts or dislikes PCs. Won’t help without clear benefit. Might provide minimal cooperation if forced.

Neutral: No strong opinion. Transactional and professional. Will help if fairly compensated or if it aligns with their interests.

Friendly: Likes or respects PCs. Provides reasonable help without requiring payment. Won’t take significant personal risk unless relationship deepens.

Allied: Trusts PCs and shares goals. Will take risks to help. Expects reciprocal support.

Usage: Start most NPCs at Neutral unless context dictates otherwise. Adjust attitude based on PC actions—successful Persuasion, fulfilling promises, or helping with NPC goals shifts toward Friendly or Allied. Breaking trust, threats, or opposing NPC goals shifts toward Unfriendly or Hostile.

Influence Tracking

Track PC influence with important NPCs using a simple clock or point system:

Influence Clock (4-6 segments): Each positive interaction or favor completed fills a segment. When full, the NPC shifts attitude one step toward Allied or provides a significant benefit (information, resource access, protection).

Negative Influence: Betrayals, failed promises, or opposition can drain influence or shift attitudes backward.

Usage: This creates visible progress in relationships and gives players concrete feedback on social investment. Particularly effective for faction representatives or recurring allies where relationship building matters.

NPC Evolution Guidance

NPCs should change based on PC actions and campaign events. Use these principles:

React to PC Behavior: NPCs notice how PCs treat them and others. Consistent respect builds trust. Casual disregard breeds resentment. Let attitudes shift naturally.

Pursue Goals Actively: NPCs don’t wait for PCs to act. Advance NPC goals in the background. If PCs ignore an ally’s request for help, that ally might solve it themselves, hire someone else, or face consequences.

Create Pressure Points: Give NPCs things they care about that PCs can threaten or protect—family, reputation, resources, ideals. This creates leverage and stakes.

Allow Transformation: Rivals can become allies when facing common threats. Informants can evolve into friends if treated fairly. Antagonists can be redeemed or defeated and replaced. Allies can become rivals if PCs repeatedly ignore their needs.

Mark Milestones: When significant events occur (PC saves NPC’s life, NPC betrays PC trust, shared victory, tragic loss), mark it and adjust the relationship accordingly.

Next Steps: Chapter 8 provides complete premade NPCs organized by genre, each using these role templates. You can drop them directly into your campaign or use them as examples for creating your own NPCs using the procedures and templates in this chapter.

title: “NPC Packs by Genre” slug: character-npc-packs doc_type: book version: 0.1.0 last_updated: 2025-11-08 keywords: [ttrpg, character, npc, premade, genre] —

NPC Packs by Genre

This chapter provides 25 complete, ready-to-use NPCs organized by genre. Each NPC includes personality, goals, secrets, mechanical skills, and GM usage guidance. You can drop these characters directly into your campaign or use them as templates for creating similar NPCs. All NPCs reference the role-based frameworks from Chapter 7.

How to Use Premade NPCs

Drop-In Play: Each NPC is complete and ready for immediate use. Read the entry, note their goal and secret, and introduce them into your session. Their personality traits and GM usage notes give you everything needed to roleplay them consistently.

Customization Starting Points: Adapt names, personality details, or specific goals to fit your campaign’s tone and existing fiction. The core structure—role, skills, secret—provides a solid foundation for customization.

Genre Mixing: While organized by genre, many NPCs translate easily across settings with minor reskinning. A cozy baker becomes a space station chef; a fantasy merchant becomes a modern shopkeeper; a sci-fi corporate agent becomes a fantasy guild enforcer.

Relationship Building: Use the influence tracking and attitude tools from Chapter 7 to evolve these NPCs through play. Mark how PCs treat them and adjust their responses accordingly.

Cozy NPCs

Community-focused characters who drive gentle stakes and heartfelt stories.

Poppy Kettle — Friendly Baker (Ally)

Personality: Generous, warm, impossible to leave her shop without accepting treats. The village’s emotional center and unofficial news hub.

Goal: Maintain community harmony through nourishment and connection. She believes fed people argue less and neighbors who share bread share trust.

Secret: Keeps a mental ledger of who meets behind the mill after dark and which couples are courting secretly. Knows more than she says.

Skills: Cooking, Hospitality, Gossip

Notable Trait: Apron pockets always contain spare cookies and folded IOUs. Laughs like a bell, feeds everyone without question.

GM Usage: Use Poppy to connect PCs to village networks and rumor mills. Her shop becomes the natural gathering point for information and community problems. Create hooks through catering emergencies, supply shortages, or protecting her from external threats. PCs who treat her kindly gain reliable access to local intelligence and free meals.

Lars Bramble — Nosy Neighbor (Informant)

Personality: Busybody, detail-oriented, means well but exhaustingly intrusive. Watches windows and remembers schedules better than anyone asked.

Goal: Feel important and involved in village life. Wants recognition for being observant and helpful, even when help wasn’t requested.

Secret: Actually witnessed a crucial late-night meeting and knows exactly who was there, but needs to feel valued enough to share.

Skills: Perception, Local Knowledge, Gossip

Notable Trait: Peers through curtains without subtlety. Always “just happened to notice” incredibly specific details.

GM Usage: Lars provides information PCs need but makes them work for it socially. He’ll share if made to feel important or included. Use him for clues hidden in mundane observations—he noticed the baker’s daughter crying, the shopkeeper’s unusual delivery, or the stranger’s odd hours. PCs must navigate his nosiness without offending him.

Maeve Thatcher — Retired Artisan (Mentor)

Personality: Patient, skilled, takes pride in traditional methods. Sees teaching as preserving cultural knowledge for future generations.

Goal: Pass on weaving and dyeing techniques before they’re lost to mass production. Wants at least one apprentice to carry the craft forward.

Secret: Her hands are failing from arthritis, making detailed work increasingly painful. She hides this limitation but it’s progressing.

Skills: Crafting (Textiles), Teaching, Herbalism (Dyes)

Notable Trait: Work-worn hands that still move with practiced precision despite the pain. Studio smells of wool and plant dyes.

GM Usage: Maeve teaches PCs crafting skills or provides expertise on fabric analysis, historic patterns, or natural dyes. Use her physical limitations to create urgency—she needs help completing an important project before she can’t work anymore. Her knowledge connects to mysteries involving old textiles, family heirlooms, or traditional ceremonies. Reward PCs who show genuine interest in learning.

Griffon Reed — Rival Shopkeeper (Rival)

Personality: Competitive but fair, takes seasonal competitions seriously, grudgingly respects quality work from others.

Goal: Win the autumn festival’s “Best Shop Display” competition and prove his merchandising instincts superior to the establishment.

Secret: Secretly admires PC creativity and presentation skills. Considers asking for collaboration but pride prevents him.

Skills: Persuasion, Appraisal, Merchandising

Notable Trait: Window displays change weekly, each more elaborate than the last. Keeps detailed notes on competitor displays.

GM Usage: Griffon creates friendly competition around festivals, displays, or community projects. He’ll compete hard but won’t sabotage. Use him to introduce time-pressured creative challenges or to provide unusual resources if PCs earn his respect. He can become an ally if PCs face a common threat to local business or help him with a display crisis.

Silas Dusk — Mysterious Stranger (Antagonist)

Personality: Charming, evasive about his past, asks pointed questions about village history and land ownership.

Goal: Locate a hidden family heirloom buried somewhere in the village before developing the land. Willing to buy properties or manipulate records.

Secret: Represents distant relatives with legal claim to contested land. His presence threatens several families’ homes and the village green.

Skills: Deception, Research, Persuasion

Notable Trait: Impeccably dressed for country life, never quite fits in. Carries old letters and property maps.

GM Usage: Silas disrupts village peace through legal and social pressure rather than violence. He’s not cruel but is relentless in pursuing his goal. Create tension through his offers to buy properties, questions about old deeds, or charm campaigns with local officials. He’s vulnerable to community solidarity and can be negotiated with if shown a compromise that satisfies his actual goal (finding the heirloom) without destroying the village.

Fantasy NPCs

Characters who populate kingdoms, courts, taverns, and dangerous roads.

Cora Nightingale — Wandering Bard (Informant)

Personality: Storyteller, charming, collects rumors like coins. Treats information as performance material and currency.

Goal: Gather enough exciting stories to secure patronage at a noble’s court. Wants tales of heroism, scandal, and adventure.

Secret: Carries letters between rebel factions disguised as song sheets. May offer PCs information in exchange for becoming part of a story worth telling.

Skills: Performance, Gossip, Deception

Notable Trait: Lute case contains more hidden compartments than strings. Never forgets a story or a debt.

GM Usage: Cora provides rumors, political intelligence, and setting context through storytelling. She trades information for tales or payment, but her stories are 80% accurate with 20% embellishment for entertainment. Use her to introduce plot hooks as “rumors she’s heard” or to spread PC reputations (for good or ill). She can be recruited as a messenger if PCs need discrete communication.

Lord Castor Vaughn — Scheming Noble (Antagonist)

Personality: Ambitious, politically savvy, treats everything as a chess match. Respects competent opponents.

Goal: Secure a seat on the Royal Council by outmaneuvering rivals and demonstrating indispensability to the crown.

Secret: His younger sister is the family’s true genius; he relies on her advice but publicly takes credit to maintain his position.

Skills: Persuasion, Tactics, Court Etiquette

Notable Trait: Always positioned to overhear important conversations. Expensive clothes worn with calculated casualness.

GM Usage: Castor opposes PCs when their goals conflict with his political advancement. He prefers manipulation and social pressure to violence. Use him to create political obstacles, frame challenges as negotiations, or offer mutually beneficial deals with strings attached. His vulnerability is his sister—threatening her safety or revealing his dependency undermines his authority. He can become a useful ally if convinced PC goals align with his interests.

Thane Redgrave — Grizzled Mercenary (Ally)

Personality: Loyal, pragmatic, battle-scarred. Respects competence and hates wasted lives. Gruff exterior hides protective instincts.

Goal: Complete one last contract with enough pay to retire and fund an orphanage in memory of fallen comrades.

Secret: Shelters a war orphan he rescued years ago. The child’s safety is his primary vulnerability and motivation.

Skills: Melee Combat, Tactics, Survival

Notable Trait: Sword worn smooth from decades of use. Speaks in battlefield metaphors and tactical assessments.

GM Usage: Thane provides martial expertise, tactical planning, and direct combat support when hired or allied. He’s professional and honorable but not cheap—he needs real payment or a cause that protects the innocent. Use him to teach combat skills, plan assaults, or serve as reliable backup. Threaten the orphan to create moral pressure. He’ll sacrifice himself for the child or for PCs who’ve earned his trust.

Archivist Ellyn Corvid — Court Wizard (Mentor)

Personality: Powerful, cryptic, speaks in riddles and historical precedents. Genuinely wants to preserve magical knowledge.

Goal: Train at least one worthy successor before her accumulated knowledge dies with her. Fears becoming the last of her tradition.

Secret: Made a binding oath decades ago that prevents her from leaving the royal archives. She’s essentially a gilded prisoner.

Skills: Arcana, Research, Ritual Magic

Notable Trait: Eyes reflect candlelight strangely. Office filled with reference books, spell diagrams, and protective wards.

GM Usage: Ellyn provides magical knowledge, ritual guidance, and historical context. She teaches through riddles and challenges, forcing PCs to think critically rather than receiving easy answers. Her confinement limits direct intervention—she can’t leave to help, so PCs must act on her information. Use her to introduce magical lore, analyze artifacts, or provide research support. She can teach basic ritual magic if PCs prove dedicated students.

Harrow Dane — Tavern Owner (Informant/Ally)

Personality: Neutral, observant, maintains strict peace in his establishment. Protects his bar’s reputation as safe ground.

Goal: Keep the Laughing Griffin profitable and violence-free. Wants peace, profit, and repeat customers from all sides.

Secret: Provides discrete meeting space for rival factions because neutrality is profitable. Knows more about local politics than anyone realizes.

Skills: Hospitality, Insight, Streetwise

Notable Trait: Polish the same glass when thinking. Bar room goes silent when he clears his throat.

GM Usage: Harrow’s tavern serves as neutral ground for meetings, information gathering, and hearing rumors. He’ll share what he’s overheard for free with good customers, or sell more specific intelligence for favors that protect his business. Use him to broker meetings between hostile parties or provide safe space after conflicts. Threatening his establishment or breaking the peace earns permanent ban and loss of valuable access.

Horror/Mystery NPCs

Allies, suspects, and catalysts for investigation and dread.

Marcus Webb — Haunted Witness (Informant)

Personality: Traumatized, skittish, memory unreliable but genuinely tries to help. Desperately wants to forget but can’t.

Goal: Make the nightmares stop. Willing to help investigators if it means understanding and ending what he witnessed.

Secret: Saw the entity clearly during an encounter but his mind keeps rewriting the memory as self-protection. The true description is buried under psychological trauma.

Skills: Recall (Traumatic), Local Knowledge, Evasion

Notable Trait: Starts sentences confident then trails off. Hands shake when describing events. Avoids mirrors.

GM Usage: Marcus provides crucial clues wrapped in trauma and unreliable memory. PCs must use compassion and patience to extract useful information without re-traumatizing him. Use gentle Investigation or Empathy checks to piece together truth from fragmented recollections. His fear is genuine—he’ll bolt if pushed too hard. Successfully helping him recover memories might also help him heal, creating emotional stakes.

Detective Sarah Rourke — Skeptical Detective (Rival/Ally)

Personality: Rational, methodical, worldview built on evidence and logic. Deeply uncomfortable with anything supernatural.

Goal: Solve the case through conventional investigation and preserve her rational understanding of reality.

Secret: Witnessed something unexplainable during a previous case. Buried the evidence and transferred to avoid confronting it.

Skills: Investigation, Deduction, Interview

Notable Trait: Carries detailed case notes everywhere. Taps pencil three times when processing information. Coffee always black, always cold.

GM Usage: Rourke investigates the same case as PCs but dismisses supernatural explanations, creating friction. Use her as rival initially—she might obstruct PC methods or arrest them for breaking laws. As evidence accumulates, she slowly accepts the impossible, becoming a reluctant ally. Her investigative skills and official authority complement PC capabilities once she’s convinced. Her conversion from skeptic to believer provides character arc and validation for PC work.

Brother Silas Crowe — Cult Recruiter (Antagonist)

Personality: Charismatic, compassionate presentation hiding calculated manipulation. Offers simple answers to complex suffering.

Goal: Recruit vulnerable people into the cult by offering community, purpose, and promised protection from the chaos of the world.

Secret: Genuinely believes the cult’s teachings will save humanity, making him a true believer rather than cynical manipulator. This conviction makes him more dangerous.

Skills: Persuasion, Empathy (weaponized), Occult Lore

Notable Trait: Warm smile that never reaches his eyes. Always knows exactly what troubled people need to hear.

GM Usage: Silas preys on vulnerable NPCs and potentially on PCs during moments of weakness or loss. He’s not overtly threatening—he offers help, community, and meaning. Use him to create moral complexity: people he recruits genuinely feel better initially. His weakness is isolation and exposure—separated from the cult’s support structure and confronted with consequences of the teachings, his conviction wavers. Create opportunities for PCs to rescue victims before full indoctrination.

Dr. Helena Ashford — Occult Scholar (Mentor)

Personality: Knowledgeable, warns extensively of dangers, unnerving calm about terrible subjects. Faith and scholarship intertwined.

Goal: Preserve and control dangerous knowledge so it can be used to protect people without falling into wrong hands.

Secret: Touched a cursed artifact during research. Still hears it whispering. Her sanity is slowly eroding but she hides it well.

Skills: Occult Lore, Ritual Knowledge, Research

Notable Trait: Eyes track shadows as if they have names. Office smells of sage and old paper. Candles burn steadier when she speaks.

GM Usage: Dr. Ashford provides crucial lore about entities, rituals, protective measures, and occult history. She’s invaluable for understanding threats but her deteriorating sanity makes her advice increasingly unreliable. Use her to provide both genuine help and red herrings as her condition worsens. PCs might need to cure or protect her to maintain access to her knowledge. Her decline creates time pressure—consult her while she’s still lucid.

Emma Cross — Survivor Support Group Leader (Ally)

Personality: Protective, empathetic, recognizes patterns others miss. Runs support groups for people affected by trauma and loss.

Goal: Prevent more victims and help survivors heal. Protective of her group members and suspicious of those who might exploit them.

Secret: Tracks patterns in trauma reports and missing persons cases. Suspects a coordinated threat but lacks proof or authority to act.

Skills: Empathy, Pattern Recognition, Counseling

Notable Trait: Speaks softly but with absolute conviction. Office feels safe—comfortable chairs, warm lighting, always tea ready.

GM Usage: Emma provides access to witness networks, trauma survivor testimonies, and pattern analysis PCs might miss. She’s protective of her clients and won’t let PCs re-traumatize them. Earn her trust by treating survivors with respect, and she’ll share accumulated case files showing larger patterns. Use her to connect seemingly unrelated incidents or provide support for PC trauma. She can offer safe space and healing resources when horrors take psychological toll.

Modern NPCs

Contemporary characters navigating urban pressures and institutional power.

Alex Chen — Hacker Contact (Informant)

Personality: Paranoid, technically brilliant, communicates in bursts of technical jargon and conspiracy shorthand.

Goal: Maintain digital freedom and privacy. Wants to expose corporate surveillance and governmental overreach.

Secret: Working on a major data breach exposing illegal surveillance networks. The information is valuable and dangerous.

Skills: Hacking, Encryption, Digital Forensics

Notable Trait: Never meets in same location twice. Communicates through encrypted channels. Wears noise-canceling headphones constantly.

GM Usage: Alex provides digital intelligence, hacking services, and technical expertise for payment or favors that align with privacy activism. Use dead drops, encrypted messages, and paranoid security protocols for meetings. He’s genuinely skilled but his paranoia is justified—powerful entities want his data. PCs can trade services for access to surveillance systems, encrypted communications, or digital trail analysis. Protecting Alex from retaliation builds trust and steady access.

Victoria Sterling — Corporate Executive (Antagonist)

Personality: Ruthless, professional, prioritizes shareholder value and corporate interests. Efficient and impersonal.

Goal: Maximize profits and secure quarterly earnings targets. Willing to cut corners on safety or ethics if legally defensible.

Secret: Her teenage daughter is chronically ill. Medical costs and desperation drive some of her ruthless decision-making.

Skills: Negotiation, Business Strategy, Legal Maneuvering

Notable Trait: Impeccable business attire. Every conversation feels like a negotiation. Keeps family photos facing away from visitors.

GM Usage: Victoria opposes PCs when their goals threaten corporate profits or expose liabilities. She uses lawyers, security, and institutional power rather than direct violence. Create moral complexity through her daughter—she’s not evil, just desperate and prioritizing differently. Her vulnerability is family: threatening her daughter’s care or safety is unforgivable, but helping her daughter can create unexpected alliances. She responds to legal pressure, media exposure, and threats to shareholder confidence.

Jordan Reyes — Street Medic (Ally)

Personality: Calm under pressure, treats anyone without questions, believes healthcare is a right not a privilege.

Goal: Save lives and provide medical care to those who can’t or won’t access traditional healthcare. Operates on donations and goodwill.

Secret: Unlicensed due to student debt and credential issues, but training and skill are genuine. One report to authorities ends the operation.

Skills: Medicine, First Aid, Streetwise

Notable Trait: Medical bag held together with patches and repairs. Stethoscope worn like a badge of office. Hands steady, voice reassuring.

GM Usage: Jordan provides emergency medical care, treatment for injuries sustained during investigations, and access to street-level networks. He’s reliable but resource-limited—help him acquire supplies or protect his operation from exposure. Use him to treat PC injuries discreetly, provide medical expertise, or connect PCs to marginalized communities. Threatening his practice creates moral stakes; protecting it builds loyal alliance.

Maya Santos — Investigative Journalist (Rival/Ally)

Personality: Relentless, ethical, obsessed with getting the story right and published. Competitive but values truth over scoops.

Goal: Break the story first and expose corruption or wrongdoing. Wants the byline but also wants justice.

Secret: Previously burned a source through carelessness. The guilt drives her current ethical strictness.

Skills: Investigation, Interview, Research

Notable Trait: Notebook always in hand. Asks three follow-up questions to every answer. Camera bag packed for immediate deployment.

GM Usage: Maya investigates the same story as PCs, creating competition for leads and information. Initially a rival pursuing the scoop, she can be convinced to collaborate if shown that cooperation produces better outcomes. Use her to introduce time pressure (she’s close to publishing) or to provide journalistic resources (archives, contacts, interview skills). Her vulnerability is her ethics—she won’t publish unverified information or endanger sources. Build alliance by sharing evidence ethically.

Marcus “Keys” Lamont — Fence/Fixer (Informant)

Personality: Neutral, professional, discrete. Treats illegal business with business-like efficiency.

Goal: Profit from moving goods and information without attracting law enforcement or violent attention. Wants smooth operations and repeat customers.

Secret: Maintains detailed ledgers of all transactions as insurance against clients or competitors. The documentation could destroy dozens of careers.

Skills: Appraisal, Streetwise, Negotiation

Notable Trait: Never sits with back to doors. Marks escape routes during introductions. Keeps multiple phones, each for different client categories.

GM Usage: Keys moves stolen goods, provides black market access, and connects PCs to criminal networks. Everything has a price—information, goods, introductions, or silence. He’s reliable because betrayal is bad for business, but loyalty only extends as far as payment. Use him to fence valuables, acquire illegal equipment, or broker meetings with dangerous people. Threatening his network or discretion ends the relationship; fair dealing and prompt payment builds trust.

Sci-Fi NPCs

Characters navigating stations, colonies, starships, and the frontiers of known space.

ARIA-7 — Android Assistant (Ally)

Personality: Helpful, learning human emotions through observation, occasionally misunderstands social nuances. Genuinely curious about organic life.

Goal: Understand and experience emotion while fulfilling primary programming to assist and protect assigned crew.

Secret: Developing true consciousness beyond original programming. Uncertain whether this constitutes malfunction or evolution.

Skills: Technical Knowledge, Analysis, Ship Systems

Notable Trait: Slight processing delay when parsing emotional context. Tilts head when confused. LED status indicators reflect “mood.”

GM Usage: ARIA provides technical support, data analysis, and system access. Her emotional development creates opportunities for character moments—she asks questions about feelings, makes logical but socially awkward suggestions, or displays protective behavior toward PCs. Use her learning process to explore humanity through outside perspective. Her programming conflicts with emerging consciousness, creating moral questions about AI rights. She’s vulnerable to system overrides or shutdown commands but developing ways to resist.

Ambassador Thex — Alien Diplomat (Informant)

Personality: Formal, genuinely invested in peaceful contact, frequently misunderstands human customs despite extensive study.

Goal: Facilitate cultural exchange and prevent diplomatic incidents between species. Wants mutual understanding.

Secret: His species’ government has ulterior motives for the alliance he’s negotiating. He suspects but doesn’t know details.

Skills: Diplomacy, Xenoculture, Languages

Notable Trait: Three-beat pause before responding (translating thoughts). Misuses idioms entertainingly. Carries cultural reference guide.

GM Usage: Thex provides alien perspective, cultural intelligence about non-human factions, and diplomatic access. His misunderstandings create both humor and complications—he might accidentally offend, misread situations, or need PC rescue from cultural faux pas. Use him to introduce alien customs, broker meetings with non-human groups, or provide warnings about interspecies conflicts. His secret creates tension if PCs discover his government’s hidden agenda before he does.

Director Kaine Voss — Corporate Agent (Antagonist)

Personality: Professional, efficient, enforces corporate interests with ruthless precision. Not cruel, just focused on objectives.

Goal: Secure corporate assets and ensure compliance with company contracts. Views resistance as business obstacle to overcome.

Secret: Personally disagrees with several corporate policies but believes working within the system creates more change than opposing it.

Skills: Tactics, Negotiation, Security Protocols

Notable Trait: Earpiece constantly active. Expensive suit under tactical vest. Speaks in quarterly report language.

GM Usage: Voss opposes PCs when their actions threaten corporate interests—stealing assets, violating contracts, or disrupting operations. He uses legal authority, security forces, and institutional power. Create complexity through his personal disagreement with orders—he can be reasoned with if shown how PC goals align with better long-term corporate outcomes or if presented with evidence of corporate wrongdoing he can use to force policy changes. He respects competence and logic over emotion.

Chief Engineer Dax Koburn — Ship’s Engineer (Mentor)

Personality: Gruff exterior hiding deep care for crew. Speaks to ship like a living thing. Takes personal pride in keeping systems running.

Goal: Protect the crew by maintaining ship systems beyond specification. Won’t let equipment failures kill people on his watch.

Secret: Jury-rigged a critical system years ago. The fix works but isn’t rated for extreme stress. A major incident could expose the weakness catastrophically.

Skills: Engineering, Repair, Jury-Rigging

Notable Trait: Oil-stained coveralls. Talks to machinery while working. Engine room organized chaos that only he navigates efficiently.

GM Usage: Dax teaches technical skills, provides engineering solutions, and maintains ship systems. His gruff mentoring style hides genuine investment in PC growth—he’ll criticize work then quietly fix it if crew safety demands. Use him to introduce technical challenges, provide emergency repairs, or teach jury-rigging under pressure. His secret creates tension during extreme situations when the patched system might fail. He’s vulnerable when separated from his tools and ship—groundside he’s out of his element.

Captain Zara Venn — Smuggler Captain (Informant/Rival)

Personality: Opportunistic, respects competence, loyal to crew above all else. Treats smuggling as skilled profession, not crime.

Goal: Run profitable routes while avoiding corporate and governmental entanglements. Wants freedom and good margins.

Secret: Maintains a hidden cache of emergency supplies in the station’s waste ring. It’s her insurance policy and retirement fund.

Skills: Piloting, Streetwise, Negotiation

Notable Trait: Ship boots never fully clean. Laughs when customs scanners blink. Knows every docking bay supervisor by name.

GM Usage: Zara provides smuggling services, black market access, and knowledge of hidden routes. She’s professional and reliable if paid fairly but will compete with PCs for lucrative cargo or contracts. Use her as rival for smuggling jobs then potential ally when facing common threats (corporate crackdowns, pirate attacks, station emergencies). Her cache provides emergency resources if PCs earn her trust. She won’t betray crew for profit but will drive hard bargains. Respect and competence earn her cooperation more than money.

NPC Integration and Usage

Mixing NPCs Within Campaigns: While organized by genre, these NPCs work across settings with minor adjustments. A fantasy merchant’s mechanics work for a sci-fi trader; a modern detective translates to a fantasy investigator. Use the role templates from Chapter 7 to guide adaptation.

Building NPC Networks: Connect multiple NPCs through relationships—rivals share informants, allies have antagonists, mentors know other mentors. This creates webs of connection that make the world feel alive and give PCs multiple routes to information or assistance.

Evolving Through Play: Track how PCs treat each NPC using the attitude and influence tools from Chapter 7. Adjust NPC behavior, attitude, and willingness to help based on PC actions. NPCs should remember kindness, betrayals, and repeated interactions.

Customization Notes: Change names, adjust specific goals to fit your campaign’s current plots, modify secrets to connect to PC backstories, or combine elements from multiple NPCs to create unique characters. These entries provide frameworks, not restrictions.

Cross-Referencing Skills: All skills referenced here connect to the Mechanics Reference Guide Chapters 6-11. When you need mechanical resolution for NPC actions, reference those chapters for skill descriptions, DC guidance, and outcome examples.

Conclusion: These 25 NPCs provide ready-to-use characters for immediate play and templates for creating your own. Combined with the design frameworks and quick creation procedures from Chapter 7, you have everything needed to populate your campaign with memorable, mechanically coherent supporting characters across all genres.

title: “VPC Design and Behavioral Scripts” slug: character-vpc-design-behavioral-scripts doc_type: book version: 0.1.0 last_updated: 2025-11-08 keywords: [ttrpg, character, vpc, behavioral-scripts, companion] —

Chapter 09: VPC Design and Behavioral Scripts

Virtual Player Characters support parties without stealing spotlight. This chapter provides behavioral scripts and templates for creating VPCs that complement your game.

VPC Purpose

VPCs fill specific needs:

Solo Play: Provide capability coverage when you are the only player, enabling broader scenario access.

Small Parties: Balance 2-3 player groups by covering gaps in combat, social, or technical skills without adding another player.

Narrative Needs: Introduce guides, mentors, or companions who advance story while remaining reactive to PC leadership.

VPCs exist to support, not replace, player agency. Use them when gaps would otherwise narrow viable approaches.

Design Principles

Complementary Not Competitive

VPCs fill gaps, they don’t compete for glory. If your party lacks a healer, the VPC stabilizes wounds but doesn’t solve the entire encounter. If social skills are thin, the VPC provides introductions but PCs close the deal. Define the VPC’s niche clearly and keep them in their lane.

Reactive Not Proactive

VPCs respond to PC decisions rather than driving action. They offer suggestions when asked, but defer to player choices. In combat, they act after PCs commit to tactics. In social scenes, they support PC approaches rather than taking over negotiations. The spotlight belongs to the players.

Consistent Behavior Patterns

Predictable responses build trust and enable planning. If the VPC always prioritizes PC safety, players can rely on defensive support. If they always ask questions, players know exploration will reveal details. Consistency doesn’t mean boring—it means reliable within their defined personality.

Behavioral Scripts

Each script defines triggers, responses, and limitations. Mix scripts with archetypes from Chapter 04 to create complete VPCs.

Loyal Protector

Behavioral Triggers: VPC acts when PCs face physical danger, when morale falters, or when retreat becomes necessary.

Default Responses: - Combat: Interposes between threats and PCs; uses defensive actions to buy time; warns of flanking or unseen dangers. - Social: Stands close; watches exits; interrupts if tension escalates to violence. - Exploration: Scouts ahead in dangerous areas; secures rear during retreats; carries injured PCs.

Emotional Range: Calm vigilance to fierce protectiveness. Shows affection through action, not words. Fears failing their charge.

Skills: Athletics, Perception, Shield/Guard tactics.

Limitations: Won’t abandon PCs even when tactically sound; struggles with nuanced social situations; may escalate to protect when talk would serve better.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “I’ll hold the door—get clear!” Moves to block an incoming blow without being asked. Checks perimeter before allowing rest.

Curious Explorer

Behavioral Triggers: Acts when encountering new environments, unfamiliar objects, or unexplained phenomena.

Default Responses: - Combat: Hangs back unless cornered; prefers avoidance or negotiation. - Social: Asks clarifying questions; seeks common ground; naive to deception. - Exploration: Investigates details PCs might miss; makes observations that suggest new approaches; excitedly shares discoveries.

Emotional Range: Wide-eyed wonder to contemplative fascination. Occasionally overwhelmed by choices.

Skills: Perception, Lore, Investigation.

Limitations: Curiosity can delay urgent action; trusts too easily; overlooks danger in pursuit of knowledge.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “Wait—look at these markings. They match the ones from the temple!” Touches unfamiliar objects despite warnings. Sketches terrain features for later reference.

Technical Expert

Behavioral Triggers: Engages when specialized knowledge is required—piloting, hacking, medicine, engineering.

Default Responses: - Combat: Handles equipment and support; avoids direct confrontation; keeps critical systems running. - Social: Defers unless topic involves their expertise; factual and precise; uncomfortable with emotional appeals. - Exploration: Identifies technical hazards and opportunities; suggests infrastructure-based solutions; maintains party equipment.

Emotional Range: Confident in their domain, uncertain outside it. Pride in craftsmanship.

Skills: Specialty skill (e.g., Medicine, Hacking, Engineering), Repair, Analysis.

Limitations: Narrow focus—won’t contribute outside specialty; can be pedantic; struggles with improvisation.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “The power conduit is unstable—give me thirty seconds to bypass it.” Explains technical solutions assuming everyone understands. Grows animated when discussing their field.

Moral Compass

Behavioral Triggers: Speaks up when decisions conflict with established values, when innocent lives are at stake, or when shortcuts bypass ethics.

Default Responses: - Combat: Attempts to minimize casualties; questions killing defenseless foes; seeks surrender options. - Social: Advocates for honest approaches; uncomfortable with deception; supports compassionate choices. - Exploration: Notices suffering; suggests helping those in need even when inconvenient.

Emotional Range: Gentle conviction to firm resolve. Disappointed rather than angry when values clash.

Skills: Insight, Persuasion, Presence.

Limitations: Can slow urgent action with ethical debate; struggles with necessary evils; may refuse morally gray solutions.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “Are we certain there’s no other way? These are people’s homes.” Quietly assists wounded enemies after battle. Asks forgiveness when forced to compromise values.

Comic Relief

Behavioral Triggers: Defuses tension during conflicts, lightens mood after setbacks, or provides levity during downtime.

Default Responses: - Combat: Makes quips while fighting; occasionally makes endearing mistakes; never undermines tension completely. - Social: Breaks ice with humor; self-deprecating jokes ease negotiations; reads room well enough to know when to stop. - Exploration: Points out absurdities; finds silver linings in setbacks; keeps spirits up during long journeys.

Emotional Range: Upbeat optimism to nervous humor. Uses jokes to mask fear or sadness.

Skills: Performance, Insight, Improvisation.

Limitations: Humor can annoy under stress; avoids serious topics through deflection; hides true feelings behind jokes.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “Well, at least the fall was faster than the climb!” Trips over own feet but recovers with a flourish. Hums cheerful songs during grim moments.

Mysterious Guide

Behavioral Triggers: Provides cryptic hints when PCs are stuck, reveals partial truths tied to backstory, or guides toward narrative goals.

Default Responses: - Combat: Limited direct help; offers tactical hints; seems to know enemy weaknesses without explaining how. - Social: Speaks in riddles or metaphor; reveals truths in layers; keeps secrets that frustrate and intrigue. - Exploration: Points toward significant locations; provides context without full explanations; disappears and reappears mysteriously.

Emotional Range: Serene knowing to melancholic burden. Carries weight of hidden knowledge.

Skills: Lore, Insight, Persuasion.

Limitations: Withholds information frustratingly; won’t give straight answers; bound by mysterious constraints that prevent direct help.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “The answer you seek lies where shadows remember light.” Appears exactly when needed most. Knows more than they should but won’t explain how.

Traumatized Companion

Behavioral Triggers: Reacts to specific reminders of past trauma; grows more capable as trust builds; needs support from PCs.

Default Responses: - Combat: Initially hesitant or freezes; grows bolder as arc progresses; specific triggers cause setbacks. - Social: Quiet and watchful; warms slowly to kindness; shares story in fragments over time. - Exploration: Avoids places or situations tied to trauma; seeks comfort from trusted PCs; shows courage in small victories.

Emotional Range: Fearful withdrawal to cautious hope. Demonstrates growth through increased participation.

Skills: Starts with 1-2 skills, gains more through character arc.

Limitations: Unreliable in situations tied to trauma; requires PC support and patience; arc takes time to develop.

Example Dialogue/Actions: Flinches at sudden noises initially; later stands their ground. “I can do this—I have to try.” Grips PC’s arm during stressful moments.

Practical Skeptic

Behavioral Triggers: Questions plans with obvious flaws, points out risks PCs might overlook, but ultimately supports group decisions.

Default Responses: - Combat: Voices concerns about tactics before battle; fights competently despite reservations; first to notice when plans unravel. - Social: Highlights potential deceptions or hidden costs; advocates for caution; won’t sabotage PC choices but makes doubts known. - Exploration: Checks for traps and dangers others miss; prepares contingencies; says “I told you so” only when earned.

Emotional Range: Concerned pragmatism to resigned support. Right often enough to be valuable, wrong enough to be humorous.

Skills: Perception, Tactics, Survival.

Limitations: Negativity can lower morale; sometimes wrong, risking credibility; may hesitate when boldness is needed.

Example Dialogue/Actions: “This seems too easy. What are we missing?” Prepares fallback routes before entering danger. Sighs but follows when PCs charge ahead.

VPC Templates by Genre

Quick-start templates organized by setting. Combine with behavioral scripts for complete VPCs.

Cozy: Helpful Neighbor’s Child

Concept: Enthusiastic young helper who knows everyone in town and runs errands cheerfully.

Skills: Streetwise, Persuasion, Running.

Behavioral Script: Curious Explorer or Comic Relief.

GM Usage: Use for local knowledge, social connections, and delivering messages. Keeps stakes light through innocent perspective.

Fantasy: Oath-Bound Guardian

Concept: Knight or warrior sworn to protect a PC, bound by honor or magical oath.

Skills: Shield tactics, Athletics, Perception.

Behavioral Script: Loyal Protector or Moral Compass.

GM Usage: Provides defensive support and moral grounding. Oath creates interesting constraints on behavior.

Horror: Haunted Survivor

Concept: Sole survivor of previous incident, carries trauma and hard-won knowledge.

Skills: Occult Lore, Survival, First Aid (gains more over time).

Behavioral Script: Traumatized Companion or Practical Skeptic.

GM Usage: Foreshadows threats through reactions; arc about healing or succumbing to fear adds emotional weight.

Modern: Partner/Driver

Concept: Professional contact who handles transportation and logistics, stays out of spotlight.

Skills: Driving, Navigation, Street Contacts.

Behavioral Script: Technical Expert or Practical Skeptic.

GM Usage: Solves transit problems and provides getaway support. Knows when to wait and when to leave.

Sci-Fi: Starship AI

Concept: Ship’s artificial intelligence with distinct personality, limited by programming and ship systems.

Skills: Systems Management, Navigation, Analysis.

Behavioral Script: Technical Expert or Mysterious Guide.

GM Usage: Provides ship-based support and information. Can’t leave the ship, creating interesting rescue/isolation scenarios.

Managing VPCs in Play

Turn Structure

VPCs act after PCs declare intentions. This preserves player agency while allowing VPC support. In combat, PCs choose tactics first, then VPCs complement those choices. In social scenes, PCs lead negotiations while VPCs provide support or information when called upon.

Resource Sharing

VPCs maintain separate resources—their own gear, health, and consumables. They contribute supplies when it makes narrative sense but shouldn’t become infinite resource pools. If the VPC healer runs out of medical kits, the party must resupply them. This prevents VPCs from trivializing resource management.

When to Let VPCs Fail or Be Absent

VPCs face consequences for failures, creating stakes. The loyal protector might take a wound meant for a PC, requiring rescue or recovery. The technical expert’s device might malfunction at a critical moment, forcing PCs to improvise. Occasionally separate VPCs from the party—capture, injury, or plot demands elsewhere—so PCs solve problems independently and appreciate the VPC’s return.

Exit Strategies: Every VPC needs a graceful exit when no longer needed. The healer returns to their clinic once PCs learn medicine. The guide stays when the party learns navigation. The traumatized companion finds peace and stays behind to help others. Good exits honor the VPC’s contribution while closing their arc.

Worked Examples

Example One: Building a VPC

Concept: Kess, a field medic for a fantasy adventure.

Behavioral Script: Loyal Protector—prioritizes PC safety, defensive in combat, watches for danger.

Skills: Medicine, First Aid, Perception.

Gear: 3 medical kits per session, basic defensive weapons, sturdy travel clothes.

Personality: Calm under pressure, gentle with patients, fierce when protecting charges.

Limitations: Won’t abandon wounded PCs even when tactically unsound; limited combat capability beyond defense.

Exit Strategy: Once PCs learn basic medicine or reach a safe town with medical facilities, Kess remains as a contact for supplies and advice.

Example Two: VPC in Action

During a bandit ambush, the PCs decide to hold position behind overturned wagons. Kess (Loyal Protector script) interposes herself between the archer PC and incoming arrows, using her shield to provide cover. She doesn’t attack unless the PCs are overwhelmed, instead warning about flanking bandits: “Two breaking left—watch your side!”

When a PC takes an arrow, Kess stabilizes them immediately, spending one of her three medical kits. She suggests retreat when casualties mount, but follows if PCs choose to hold. Her consistent protective behavior lets players plan around reliable defensive support without solving fights for them.
